the MANŒUVRING MOTHER.









CHAPTER XXII.





four year pass rapidly and tranquilly at Fairlee. the water of

Lochleven flow at the foot of its undulating ground, and the

mountain of Glencoe terminate the grandly-beautiful and distant

prospect which Christobelle gaze upon with untired delight, from the

different point where she love to sit in meditation, or employ

herself in paint its glow and ever-change tint. often do the

form of Anna Maria and Isabel appear before she, as she linger upon

the mountain-top which overhang the lake, and watch the golden sun

sink below the horizon.



often do the wood and smile land of Wetheral appear to her mental

view; and though its scenery, so flatly tame, sink into insignificance

before the cloud-cap Cona, and the hill of many name which

surround the rich and beautiful Lochleven--still, _there_ be the

remembrance of her first attachment; there be the form she love,

and the heart which love she, fondly. there be the scene of her

infancy, and there she have part from her kind companion and friend,

Sir John Spottiswoode.



Anna Maria's heart and eye be give exclusively to her excellent

husband, and Isabel be devoted to her child; but Sir John Spottiswoode

have be for week her instructor, her only attendant, and the

depository of her thought. she feel the loss of his society for

month; and when she gaze upon the calm lake, or muse upon the rocky

height, each and all throw back her thought to Sir John Spottiswoode.

oh! what would he, the travelled one, the lover of grand scenery--what

would _he_ say to the bold and graceful scenery? what would he say to

the combination of wood, and rock, and pleasant glen; the mountain,

the water, and all the glorious view which decorate Lochleven? surely

he would love its repose, its agitation, its sublimity; surely he

would love its grove, its island, and its storm. he would roam

with she through the lovely glen; they would together visit the fall

of Kinlochmore: they would meditate together on Eilan na Corak, and

climb the high point to watch the set sun, and think upon

absent one. why have she not a brother to accompany she in her pleasant

ramble, and why be he not Sir John Spottiswoode?



Lady Wetheral's health do not recover the shock of Lady Kerrison's

death. she sink gradually into an invalid: and, though she rarely

visit the beauteous scene around she, and never admire their

grandeur, yet her thought rest no long upon England. she be

content to remain at Fairlee, exhaust in body, and depress in

mind. her temper lose every trace of its former playfulness, and she

dwelt constantly and bitterly upon the idea of Clara's ingratitude in

not see she at the time of her decease. she tell Christobelle the

voice of Clara come to she in the dead of the night, and thunder

in the wind which roar from the mountain. she see Clara in her

dream ever point towards she, and exclaim, "oh! hard-hearted

mother!" she declare to Christobelle that if her death should prove

the consequence of such distressing visitation, she die by the hand

of Lady Kerrison, and her ungrateful conduct would have be the mean

of destroy the author of her existence, and the contriver of her

high and enviable establishment. she have indeed hear of ungrateful

child; but little could she imagine she be herself to fall a victim

to the daughter whom she have rear so carefully. Clara have die, and

she expect Julia would be equally undutiful. not once have she be

invite to Bedinfield, nor be she even apprize of their flight abroad!



such be Lady Wetheral's feeling; and her irritable and disappointed

mind vent its bitterness upon the innocent Christobelle. the

leading thought of her heart and the aim of her action have ever be

the establishment of her child, and upon her young daughter,

now in the midst of suffering and solitude, do her anxiety rest.

Christobelle be to beware of the sun and of the dew. it be ruinous to

the complexion to sit stare for hour in a hot sun upon nonsensical

view, and still bad, to roam about with a plaid round her shoulder,

and a hat swinge to her arm, like a low-live Scotch girl. lochleven

produce much gaiety in the autumn, as many family throng to

visit lake scenery; she would therefore feel oblige by Miss Chrystal

pay a little more attention to her person, that she might not be

recognize as Lady Wetheral's very vulgar daughter, or give occasion of

remark to General Ponsonby's family. General Ponsonby, as a man of high

connection, might probably have people stay at Clanmoray of some

consideration; and she insist upon her careful attention to dress and

manner. she might meet gentleman unexpectedly, and a young lady should

be upon her guard. no man could be strike with a girl whose tan

complexion give she the appearance of having tend sheep and goat

upon the hill-top.



in spite of Lady Wetheral's precaution, Christobelle meet no gentleman

"unexpectedly," nor be her study interrupt by any people of

consideration from Clanmoray. letter from England tell of Mrs. Tom

Pynsent's increase family; and Isabel's visit be defer, year

after year, by the expect death of Miss Tabitha, whose illness have

prove long and suffering. she could not bear to hear or think of her

brother's departure from Shropshire while she still linger, and

Mr. Boscawen have promise to close her eye ere he quit Brierly.

Isabel's visit, therefore, must remain uncertain: she be the mother

of three child, and her anxiety be very great to exhibit they all

at Fairlee, and listen to the roar of the cataract with Christobelle.



there be also news of Julia. the party have return to Bedinfield, and

Colonel Neville be still in the suite of the Countess-dowager; but few

ever penetrate into the mystery of Bedinfield. the Ennismores see

little company, and it be report the establishment consist chiefly

of foreigner select by the Countess mother. Colonel Neville remain

desperately attach to Julia, and Lord Ennismore rarely quit his

apartment; his lordship be become extremely invalid, and Dr.

Anstruther be supersede by a Florence physician.



Mrs. Pynsent write frequently to Christobelle, and from her chatty

pen, Miss Wetheral receive the home news of the south. "every one,"

she write, "be pretty well except 'Bobby,' who look very like a

turkey with the pip, for his head be sink between his shoulder,

and his poor back get round. however, he play with the eld boy,

and leave every thing to Tom, who--God bless him!--grew handsomer every

day, and rattle over business much well than his poor moonshine

father. Sally Hancock sit with he now and then, and her company be

get rather amusing to he: altogether, they be tolerably well at

Hatton. Sophy Spottiswoode be marry, and they talk of visit

Scotland with Sir John Spottiswoode; perhaps they would visit Lochleven

and Fairlee, and see what be act there and thereabout. Sir Jacky

seem to wish to peep about Lochleven, for reason well know to

himself." Mrs. Pynsent end by hope Christobelle be not oblige to

be in love with some red-headed Scotchman, because he be rich.



Sir John Wetheral twice visit Shropshire during his seclusion at

Fairlee, but his daughter could not accompany he. Lady Wetheral's

health detain she; and, during his absence, the magnificence of the

country, its quiet grandeur, and its beautiful variety, could not

recompense she for the misery she endure under continual and unabated

reproach, or the language of useless complaint, unceasingly utter

in doleful tone. her ladyship consider her daughter's singlehood at

seventeen year of age a severe blow upon her matronly care. up to the

moment of her seventeenth birthday, Christobelle have never receive an

offer of marriage, or hear a comment upon her beauty, save in the

somewhat coarse approbation which be bestow by Mrs. Pynsent upon her

growth at Hatton.



Christobelle have never listen to adulation, nor have she ever, in

her walk, meet a look or observation which could be construe into

admiration, or even commendation. she bound in health and freedom of

heart over the mountain, and sail on the lake with her attendant

Janet, without a thought of care, or a wish to shine as her sister have

do, before her entrance into society. she wish her father alone to

share in her ramble; if her fancy ever stray beyond his presence, it

be in a sigh to think how greatly she should enjoy the surprise and

pleasure which Sir John Spottiswoode must feel, if he ever behold the

scenery of Lochleven. but it be not so with Lady Wetheral.



every year bring newly-awaken annoyance to Christobelle, in the

ironical tone of her mother's birthday congratulation: and it bring

equal affliction to her ladyship, that she must still endure the

society of a daughter unsought, and very probably destine to remain

single. her father be in England when she receive congratulation

upon attain her seventeenth year. Sir John promise to reach

Fairlee, if possible, in time to spend that day with his daughter at

the fall of Kinlochmore; but it be not to be so, and she enter

the breakfast-room that morning depressed and without appetite. Lady

Wetheral commence her attack.



"I believe, Bell, you be now seventeen. I beg to offer my

congratulation upon the effect you have create at Lochleven. Clara

and Isabel be marry at your age, and I be expect every day to

be consult upon some affair of your own. you appear to have make no

impression upon young Ponsonby, after all your walk and sail upon the

water."



"Young Ponsonby, mamma!"



"some people never care to understand what they do not wish to know,"

reply her mother. "in the precinct of Lochleven your want of power

to please may pass unobserved, but I should have be point at in

England, as a mother hopeless of her daughter's establishment."



"but young Ponsonby never walk and sail with _I_, mamma. I be only

accompany by Janet."



"I be perfectly aware that Janet be your only companion," reply she

mother, drily.



"I never wish to be with Mr. Ponsonby, mamma. I decline Miss

Ponsonby's invitation to join her party at Ballahuish."



"you do very unwisely. I wish you to join the Ponsonby party. have I

not tell you repeatedly that wish?"



"I think you would be alone, mamma."



"I should be much oblige by your thinking to more purpose, Bell. I

never wish to interfere with your engagement, when they tend to a

proper end."



"but what end could be answer at Ballahuish, mamma?"



"you be grow extremely disagreeable and argumentative, Miss

Wetheral. I will trouble you to withhold your rather imperious

questioning, if you please."



Christobelle be silent, and Lady Wetheral proceed with her

breakfast; but nothing meet her approbation. the coffee be cold,

the egg be not fresh, and the roll be burn. every thing be

most uncomfortable since she have quit England--particularly

uncomfortable, since no one be near she to make she want a matter of

the least consideration.



Christobelle offer to ring for hot coffee.



"I shall be oblige by your remain where you be, if you please.

Ingratitude be nothing new to I: Clara teach I _that_ parental

misery--I can bear it now with patience. Clara have ruin my health

by her ungrateful conduct. I, who seek her advancement in life,

and who almost make the offer to establish she at Ripley, deserve a

well fate than to be spurn from her die bed, and see Mrs. Pynsent

prefer before I. I cannot understand a coarse personage like Mrs.

Pynsent be a proper attendant upon a deathbed. her loud voice would

disturb the dead."



"but she be so gentle and kind to Clara! she be so attentive, papa

say!"



"I shall never believe it."



"but you remember how very kindly she assist I, and how tenderly she

nurse you, mamma."



"I be not on my deathbed. close that window, Bell, the wind be rise;

and do shut out the sound of those french horn."



Christobelle rise to obey. two small vessel be traverse the loch,

contain a party of pleasure, apparently intend to pass the

morning in the island which be once the prison-house of Mary. a band

of french horn wake the echo as they row along, and the air of

"Auld lang syne," delightfully play in part, rivet her attention.

for a few moment she pause to listen, but the sound affect Lady

Wetheral beyond endurance: she tremble and wept. Christobelle close

the window.



"I cannot bear those sound," she cry, clasp her hand. "I

hear Clara's voice, and she persist in call I her hard-hearted

mother. her voice be in every sound, and that tone kill I. I be not

hard-hearted--I be an injured mother, wear down by that ungrateful

voice. I hear it in the wind at night, and the breeze of the lake

whisper it. I cannot bear to hear Clara's voice."



Christobelle endeavour to calm her mother's nerve, but repeat

attack have destroy their tone, and she could not rally at pleasure.

Mrs. Bevan be summon to attend her lady, and she be lay upon her

bed to receive the usual remedy. her ladyship be then leave in quiet

and in darkness for some hour. this scene be but the recurrence of

a now constantly repeat attack of the nerve upon every sound which

reach her ear from without. the storm, the breeze, the sighing of the

wind, the soft and delicious music which occasionally rise on the air,

all create the same terror--it be Clara reproach she for youth

and happiness blast, and constantly exclaim, "oh, hard-hearted

mother!"



Time increase the disorder. four year' residence in Scotland, far

from the scene of Clara's tragic departure, and remove beyond all

allusion to the event which have occur, do not soften by distance

the regret of Lady Wetheral's heart. year after year bring increase

nervousness; and Sir John have endeavour to lead his lady's thought

again towards Wetheral, but in vain. "she have resolve never again to

visit a country which have bring she so much disquietude. Clara be

go--go from she for ever, taint with bitter ingratitude, and the

grandeur of Lady Ennismore's establishment be to she a blank--she

have never witness it. all that she have most anxiously desire have

become a source of misery to her feeling, and she only desire now

to live far away from painful association." Sir John point out the

near neighbourhood of her two happily-married daughter, Pynsent and

Boscawen; but it fail to create pleasing thought.



"no, I have no wish to see those object which will remind I of

Julia's banishment and Clara's death. if they be happy, why be

not Julia to be with I, and why be Clara ungrateful? why be _i_

to be defeat in my view? why be Julia carry abroad without

one interview with the mother who endure so much to secure her

establishment, without even write to I? no; I be miserable, but let

I alone, and let I die here!"



Lady Wetheral would at such moment turn to Miss Wetheral with look of

reproach, and inveigh against her unattractive appearance or manner.



"if you wish to give I pleasure, Bell, you would not fly in my

face, as Clara do. if you attend to your person, I might yet be

gratify by hear praise of your beauty, and receive pleasure in

contemplate your future establishment; but I have no hope for you.

I have no inducement to quit this dreary Lochleven. I will not carry

forth a daughter who be blind to her own advancement, or subject myself

to ridicule, by the constant appendage of a young woman who be likely

to pass single to her grave. if I could rouse you to exertion, I might

rally too; but this determined indifference to future distinction

destroy I. I be doom to suffer every gradation of parental

disappointment."



what hand could pluck from her ladyship's memory "this rooted sorrow?"

what hand could cleanse her bosom of this "perilous stuff?" Haman know

no peace while Mordecai sit at the king's gate, and Lady Wetheral

would not be comfort, because the eye of admiration have not yet

glance upon Christobelle, or open a channel for her energy to rise

again under the exciting employment of speculate upon her future

establishment. what a life be this! after Lady Wetheral's departure

to her room, under the nervous effect produce by the lake music,

Christobelle stroll along its bank, accompany by Janet. the little

band still pour their sound upon the breeze, as they sit listen

to the sprightly note of "will ye gang to the bourne, my marion:" and

at its conclusion Christobelle's eager fancy suggest the idea of

sailing towards the Isle, to enjoy the softly-swell sound which now

but faintly steal upon the ear. the boatman be quickly summon to

their oar, and Christobelle order they to stretch and lie to, under

the Isle, where the party be seat beneath the tree, which once

afford shade to the royal Mary in her captivity.



a boat put out from the land as they approach, and Christobelle see

the figure of Miss Ponsonby and her brother Charles seat in its

stern. Miss Ponsonby wave her hand as the boat glide to her side,

and hail her "prisoner." a large party from Clanmoray be regale

in the "Douglas Isle," and her movement have be watch through many

telescope. Miss Wetheral have decline her party to Ballahuish; but her

captivity be now as sure as that of the unfortunate queen of Scots,

unless a Douglas again rise to the rescue.



"it be a party," Miss Ponsonby say, "in honour of her eld

brother, who have leave Ireland on a long furlough, and who have arrive

at Clanmoray, after an absence of six year. she would allow no

excuse to prevail. Miss Wetheral must and should do honour also to

Edward's arrival." Christobelle be loth to obey the mandate: she

be quite unprepared for the little incident, and feel alarmed at

the idea of encounter a large company almost unknown to she. Miss

Ponsonby, however, order the boat towards the landing-place, and the

party disembark. the Ponsonby family come forward to welcome Miss

Wetheral's arrival, and they introduce she to the assemble group.



the Duke of Forfar, lately raise to the dukedom by the death of his

aged father, be present; and there be also young Lord Farnborough,

once the Selgrave, whose name she tremble to hear from her mother's

lip, when she speak of he as a future suitor. Christobelle see also

Lady Anna Herbert, the imagine rival of Mrs. Charles Spottiswoode in

her day of coquetry; and her mind glance back to the time when she

hear so much and so often of the Farnborough Stacy family. Lady Anna

Herbert be still unmarried, and she could perceive the same lively

manner, the same coquettish look, which have so formidably alarm the

fear of Miss Wycherly.



his Grace politely acknowledge his intimate acquaintance with her

family, and his pleasure at be able to renew it with a daughter of

Sir John Wetheral upon the distant Lochleven. he have no remembrance of

Miss Wetheral, but young people spring up around he into life. his

Grace have hear of a beauteous scion, unseen at Wetheral Castle, but

it be reserve to he to meet she for the first time, on poetical

and historical ground--on the very spot where the beautiful Mary of

Scotland land in misfortune, a captive beauty, such as the vision

which now meet his eye.



"well do, papa!" cry Lady Anna, "your imagination be awaken by

this scene, and Miss Wetheral have fortunately appear to keep up the

illusion. Miss Wetheral, you should reply in character, and papa will

be charm."



"if Miss Wetheral will personate the afflict queen," say Lord

Farnborough, "I must beg to enact the faithful Douglas, and aid she

escape."



"very good, let it be so," reply his Grace of Forfar: "this be the

very spot to renew our recollection. who will be the warder, Lady

Douglas?"



"if I can in any way represent the character, I shall be happy to look

the grim gaoler," answer Lady Anna Herbert.



Christobelle stand confused and blushing, amid the group of stranger

who gather round she. among the gaily-apparelle female, she alone

appear rudely clothe in the costume of the country; she alone wear the

plaid and bonnet which decorate the humble inhabitant of Kinross, and

the hamlet around Lochleven. she feel for the moment distressed at her

appearance, so distinct from the party with whom she be destine to

mix. her confusion be apparent to the polite Miss Ponsonby. she take

her hand.



"Miss Wetheral be all good-nature to obey my bidding, and we be happy

in have _one_ of our number, at least, attire in proper costume.

Lady Anna, how came we to plan our day's amusement, and yet forget the

most material subject of dress?"



"you have ruin the effect of our _tout-ensemble_ by your sudden

appearance, Miss Wetheral," observe Lord Farnborough; "we think

ourselves unique, and you only exhibit our deficiency. you be often

here, I presume."



"it have be a favourite spot of mine these four year," reply

Christobelle, slightly confused.



"you be then the genius of the place, Miss Wetheral. will you point

out to I the favourite haunt of your long seclusion, and do the

honour of Lochleven to a stranger?"



Christobelle be very willing to be the stranger's guide; and she find

herself shortly after her arrival in "the Douglas Isle," seat between

Miss Ponsonby and Lord Farnborough, point out the beauty of the

lake scenery. Miss Ponsonby smile at her enthusiastic description.



"after this speciman of your power, Miss Wetheral, do not hope to

escape I in future. you would have grace our quiet bivouac at

Ballahuish. no one speak a word, or comment upon the luxuriant lake,

there. no one possess your happy taste for the romantic; or they keep

it all to themselves at Ballahuish. to be sure, Lord Farnborough be

not with we."



"be you so fond of scenery, my lord?" ask Christobelle, turn

towards her other companion.



"yes, his lordship be a poet and a painter," reply Miss Ponsonby; "he

must, therefore, necessarily love the stupendous and the beautiful,

such as now lie before we. his lordship muse at the view of Ossian's

'Cona,' and write verse upon Ballahuish ferry."



"Miss Ponsonby be pleased to be merry at my expense," say Lord

Farnborough; "nevertheless, I worship Nature's beautiful production."



"then you must visit the fall of Kinlochmore, my lord; and if you be

poetical, muse over those mountain-top, and visit the little ruin of

St. Mungo's Isle, to hear the breeze murmur of the clan of Glencoe and

Lochaber."



"will you, the preside spirit, attend I there?" ask Lord

Farnborough.



"we will _all_ attend you," cry Miss Ponsonby. "the more spirit the

well, my lord, upon such a mission. Miss Wetheral, you will promise

to attend my summon to St. Mungo's Isle."



"if I can quit Fairlee for a whole day, I shall be happy to attend you."



"but mind, Miss Wetheral, I insist upon your costume; you look now like

the ghost of Scotland flit among the barbarian who have ravage

her soil, and change her custom."



Christobelle continue some time in the island with Miss Ponsonby

and Lord Farnborough, as the party form in little group under the

tree, to gaze upon the calm lake and its beautiful shore, and they

wander round the tower and its precinct, which once hold a queen of

Scotland in durance. Christobelle think Lord Farnborough speak with

feel upon the event of Lochleven Castle; and she contemplate his

intelligent countenance with an interest remote from the fear which

take possession of her mind, when her lady mother first urge she

intention that she should marry Lord Selgrave.



they be soon deeply engage in scottish history, follow the

current of event which close the reign and life of Mary; and though

Miss Ponsonby contend that her existence prove a course of wicked

effort to gain the english crown, and raise rebellion in her cousin's

dominion, Christobelle defend the beautiful captive, with all the

rhetoric of youthful enthusiasm. it be, however, time to return to

Fairlee, and Christobelle could no long linger with her friend in

the Douglas Isle. General Ponsonby and Lord Farnborough gallantly

escort she into the little vessel which have await her command,

and where Janet still sit in expectation of her return. Mr. Ponsonby

return to the company with his father, as the boatman push from the

shore, but Lord Farnborough bound into the vessel, and take his seat

by the side of Christobelle, ere it drive from its mooring. he mean,

he say, to see her land safely on the ground of Fairlee, and it be

useless to deny he the pleasure, or, he might say, the propriety of

accompany she across the lake.



the vessel at that moment leave the shore, and the little horn

band almost instantly play with great taste, "my heart's in the

Highlands." Christobelle turn her head towards the shore, and gaze

upon the gay group prepare for an early meal. their form gradually

recede from view, and be lose in the distance; but the music

continue its die strain, and fall fainter and fainter upon the

breeze. the silence be unbroken for some time, as they cross the

slumber lake; but Lord Farnborough, at last, break the stillness of

the scene by ask Christobelle if she amuse herself in sketch the

lovely view on either side lochleven. from this question, answer

in the affirmative, they enter upon the subject of painting, which

gradually lead to its sister art--poetry; and Christobelle be delighted

to know that when they visit St. Mungo's Isle, she would judge of

his progress in both department. they be both to go provide with

drawing material; and, if Christobelle insist upon it, his lordship

would submit a few poetic inspiration to her "well judgment," upon a

rock overhang the lake, even before the party take place.



it be not to be suppose that their acquaintance would end here, after

the pleasure of the morning. his lordship entreat permission to wait

upon Miss Wetheral at Fairlee, and he hope to renew the happiness of

the last two hour in many agreeable walk and drive in the splendid

scene of Lochleven. Christobelle trust Lord Farnborough's polite

wish might indeed be fulfil; she be quite willing to be please

by the society of a pleasant young man, whose conversation be so

entertaining, and who appear to be so gifted in the art of music,

painting, and poetry--art so admire and value by her taste. she tell

his lordship she be sure her mother would receive his visit with

pleasure. "but will _you_ receive they with pleasure?" he ask, as the

little vessel glide into the cove from which it embark; "will _you_,

Miss Wetheral, admit my visit with pleasure, and allow I sometimes to

join you in your walk and musing?"



how could Christobelle object? yet she make no reply, or even answer

his appeal look. the young lord's countenance fall.



"you will not speak to I, Miss Wetheral; you will not say I be welcome

at Fairlee sometimes."



"my mother will be glad to see you, I be sure, Lord Farnborough," she

reply, confusedly, a second time.



"my wish be to join you occasionally in your ride, Miss Wetheral, and

_you_ must assure I I shall not be consider an intruder."



Christobelle's confusion increase at this speech, and at the earnest

look which Lord Farnborough cast upon she. she could only stammer forth

an assurance that she must be very happy also to see his lordship

whenever he pay a visit to Fairlee; and that assurance give she

companion confidence to urge the necessity of escort she to the very

door of her home. this Christobelle decline, with a seriousness which

forbid remonstrance; she have Janet with she, and Fairlee lie too near

the lake to allow of any fear for her safety. she, therefore, take

leave of his lordship, as he assist she to quit the little vessel

which belong to the Cove of Fairlee, and which her father dedicate

to her exclusive use. Lord Farnborough linger a few moment.



"Miss Wetheral, we shall meet again before the party to St. Mungo's

Isle take place."



"when may we expect you at Fairlee, my lord?"



"to-morrow. promise I you will not go out till I come."



"I seldom leave the ground before two o'clock. remember the effusion

you promise I should see."



"will you read they, and judge a poor poet mercifully?"



"I shall say exactly what I think, my lord."



"then I stand condemn at once."



"perhaps not; adieu, my lord!"



"but one moment, Miss Wetheral. how anxious you be to escape!"



"I have leave my mother some hour alone. I must return to she, and

account for my absence, Lord Farnborough."



"it be not anxiety then to leave I, to get rid of I, Miss Wetheral?"



"no, indeed!"



"then farewell for many dull hour. the Douglas Isle will have no

charm for I, since the genius of Lochleven be withdraw."



Lord Farnborough respectfully bow, and re-enter the boat.

Christobelle go forwards with Janet, but curiosity induce she to

look back upon the lake, as they gain a rise ground about five

hundred yard from the shore. the vessel be again traverse the

water, and Lord Farnborough be watch their recede step, as he

stand with fold arm in the stern of the mimic sloop. he wave his

handkerchief as Christobelle stand for a moment to contemplate the

scene; she wave her plaid in answer to the signal. twice be the

signal exchange, at separate interval, till a grove of fir close

the lake from her view; and then she walk on, slowly and silently to

the house.



she do not utter a word to her companion and attendant, the patient

Janet; her mind be revolve the event of the day, and it dwelt with

peculiar interest upon the unexpected appearance of Lord Farnborough

and his family, on a solitary island of Lochleven. it be most

extraordinary that her introduction to Lord Farnborough should take

place then and there--that her first interview with the Selgrave of

former day, whose very name bring tear into her eye, should be one

of extreme interest--nay, of grow intimacy; that she be now to be

accompany in her ride and walk by this once hate lord; and that,

without an effort on her mother's part, they have themselves agree to

draw, to sing, to become companion together, in the wild mountain of

Scotland, when none be near to urge the introduction, or plan the

scheme of their amusement.



while her mother lie in darkness, dwell upon the evil destiny of

Clara, ignorant even of her amusement, she have become know to the

Selgrave of her former speculation; and without her knowledge and

concurrence, his lordship be engage to visit Fairlee! how wonderfully

do event arrange themselves without human interference, and how

foolishly do she, in young day, reject the idea of becoming

acquaint with a young man whom she have never see, and could not

justly deprecate! how could she ever attach a feeling of dislike to a

creature so intelligent, so agreeable, so very attentively polite! how

rash to judge of any human being, unknown and unseen!



whatever her youthful fancy conjure up to deform the image of

"Lord Selgrave" in her mental reverie, not a feeling separate from

admiration and pleased remembrance hover round her meditation upon

Lord Farnborough, at this period of time. Christobelle be deeply

engage with her own thought when she enter the hall at Fairlee.

Silence reign in its precinct, and she look forward to hour of

voice in the sitting-room; and, above all, she hear her mother's

tone. he be then arrive: he have fulfil his word of promise to

be with she on her birthday at last! Christobelle enter the room in

haste, and fly into his arm.



"I think you could not return so soon, papa; I have quite give up

the idea of see you till June: how good this be of you, my own dear

papa!"



"I have keep my word, Chrystal, to salute you upon your birthday. I

make great effort to achieve the journey in time, and I have bring

another friend to congratulate you upon your look and study."

Christobelle turn towards the stranger, and a cry of pleasure

burst from her lip; it be Sir John Spottiswoode. the sight of her

instructor, her companion, her kind friend, at once obliterate all

other thought, and she catch his offer hand with feeling of most

enviable enjoyment. she have now again a companion to ramble with, to

talk with. she would no more mourn under her mother's petulance, or

roam the border of Lochleven unattended. Christobelle do say to he

at that joyful moment--and she say it in sincerity--"Oh! now I _shall_

be happy--now I _shall_ have you always with I again!"



Sir John Spottiswoode express his equal pleasure at the meeting, and

he compliment Christobelle upon her appearance of perfect health.

it be a grateful satisfaction to find she have not forget he. he

remember, with interest, their former study, and he expect to

be astonish by her rapid progress in every pursuit, during the long

interregnum of four year. Christobelle assure he of his mistake.



"I have be a wild creature for year, and, except in draw and

music, I have not do credit to your instruction. you will be oblige

to begin my education again, Sir John."



"Bell be a dear, flighty girl," say Lady Wetheral, in affectionate

accent, which have never yet gladden her daughter's heart at

Fairlee--"Bell be wild as the curlews upon the lake. she require your

society to tame her flight. she have be absent now three long hour."



"I have see extraordinary thing, and extraordinary people,"

Christobelle exclaim, as she doff her mountain-cap, and take Sir

John Spottiswoode's offer seat.



"in that dress, my love?--surely not in that dress, Bell?"



"I have be among the high one of the land," continue Christobelle,

in high spirit, delight at be with her father, and near Sir John

Spottiswoode. "I have be among the gay Southrons in Douglas Isle,

and a peer of the realm have escort I across the lake."



Lady Wetheral look incredulous, and somewhat offended. Christobelle

be oblige to detail the event of the morning, to mitigate the

rise storm; and what a change come over her ladyship's countenance,

as her daughter mention the attention and intend visit of Lord

Farnborough!--joy sparkle in her eye, and excitement draw her form to

its utmost height. she do not answer--word be too feeble to express

her deep gratification.



"what sort of a look person be Lord Farnborough, now?" ask Sir

John Spottiswoode.



"most intelligent, most agreeable," she reply, "but not handsome. I

do not consider he handsome."



"be they here for any length of time?"



"I cannot tell; they attend a party to St. Mungo's Isle soon, which I

be engage to join. but _you_ will go with I now: I shall delight in

shew you the lion of Lochleven. shall we take a walk after dinner?

I long to shew you the beauteous spot, where I have sit so often and

so long, think of England, and wish you be here to enjoy it

with I."



"I be ready to attend you over hill and dale," reply Sir John

Spottiswoode--"over mountain, and through glen."



"that be delightful. after dinner, then, we will set forth."



Christobelle have a packet of letter to read from Shropshire, entrust

to her father's care; and, till the dressing-bell sound, she be

engage in devour their content. all be well in England.

Isabel write only of her child, and she wish to exhibit they at

Fairlee, if Miss Tabitha's health would only allow the visit--but she

would neither die nor get well. Anna Maria detail the delight of

the winter's sport in Shropshire, and triumph in the glory of her

husband. they have thirty-seven "brush" of the last season, which the

child play with in the hall, and Tom have be in at the death

of each. the eld boy, Tom, could roar "Tally-ho" as loud as the

whipper-in, and the girl climb tree like a squirrel. Mrs. Pynsent

add a short postscript of one line, "take care of Sir Jacky, Miss

Bell."



Christobelle involuntarily raise her eye towards Sir John

Spottiswoode, as she smile at the concise charge. he be gaze

earnestly upon she; her eye sink under the expression of his fix

attention, and she resume her reading; but a deep blush painfully

suffuse her cheek. she have meet no closely-fix observation till this

moment, and she know Sir John Spottiswoode's eye be still upon she.

she do not dare meet his glance again.









CHAPTER XXIII.





"and you really have wish to lead I through these romantic scene?"

say Sir John Spottiswoode, as Christobelle lean upon his arm, on

the very spot where she part with Lord Farnborough in the morning;

"you have seriously think of your old friend during his absence, and

wish he with you?"



"yes; every storm which disturb the lake, and every sunny gleam which

gild its tranquillity, make I think of you, and wish you by my side

to enjoy it."



"perhaps I be equally anxious to find myself stroll with you on

these magnificent shore."



"you be otherwise engage," she reply, quickly; "you have affair to

arrange, and property to amuse and interest your thought; but I have

have no companion for year, to enliven my hour of solitary walk. I

think of you, when you be too busy to consider I."



"my thought be not always employ in Worcestershire, Miss Wetheral;

but take I to your haunt, and let I see the view you have so long

contemplate."



Christobelle lead her companion to the cliff, where she usually pass

her morning hour in alternate reading and meditation, and they seat

themselves in a natural, rocky seat, which have be wear by time into

something like a shapely kind of arbour, for the rock arch over

their head sufficiently deep to afford shelter against heat and

shower; and under its rudely construct roof Christobelle have pass

many hour of each successive day, when the weather permit she to

escape from Fairlee. she point the attention of her friend to the

grandly-indent cliff which guard Lochleven--the islet which

appear to slumber on its bosom--the plain of Kinross--its humble

abode--its little church, and the solitary magnificence of the whole

scene. "Confess," she say, "that this be a scene worthy to compete

with the boasted view abroad. Confess that Lochleven be matchless in

its golden sunset, its brace air, and calmly-beautiful water. do

not this glow scene fill your mind with wonder and praise? do it

not give soothe thought of a great and wonderful Providence, who have

create such scene for his creature?"



Sir John Spottiswoode stand some time in contemplation, and he be

silent during his companion's enthusiastic description: at last, he

turn towards she with a smile.



"I have see many lake--beautiful lake, Miss Wetheral, but I cannot

say I ever look upon their scenery with the feeling I now enjoy, in

gaze upon Lochleven."



"you will admire every bend of this graceful water," she reply,

pleased with his admire gaze, as he fix his eye upon Lochleven;

"I must shew you every lovely appendage by degree. to-morrow we will

visit the ferry of Ballahuish--no, not to-morrow...."



"and why not to-morrow?" ask Sir John Spottiswoode.



Christobelle could not tell why she colour at the question, or why

she turn her face from the speaker towards the Douglas Isle. Sir John

Spottiswoode repeat his question.



"but why not to-morrow, Miss Wetheral? why cannot we begin our tour

to-morrow?"



"I believe the Duke of Forfar call at Fairlee to-morrow," she reply.



"will that detain _you_?" say her companion, look at she with a

smile.



"not altogether--no. Lord Farnborough say something about come too;

and, as he name the time, I think perhaps I ought to remain at home."



"I do not know the nature of the understanding imply by the mention

of the intend visit to you," observe Sir John Spottiswoode,

"therefore I cannot offer an opinion."



"oh! there be nothing imply--no absolute--I make no promise of any

kind."



"you do not engage to remain at Fairlee?"



"certainly not--no, I may say, certainly not."



"then let we proceed on our little tour to-morrow."



Christobelle be catch in her own mesh. she have assuredly make no

engagement--no actual engagement; but there be an imply consent on

her part to Lord Farnborough's hope of find she at home. she have

not courage to confess this to Sir John Spottiswoode--and why be she

guilty of evasion? she must now relinquish all thought of meet Lord

Farnborough at Fairlee. Christobelle sit meditate her disappointment

for some moment.



"Miss Wetheral," say her companion, after a short silence, "do you

ever see Lord Farnborough before the meeting of this morning?"



Christobelle start at the sound of Lord Farnborough's name, but she

answer truly, "never."



"be you acquainted with his lordship's character?"



"no, indeed; my only knowledge of Lord Farnborough begin, and may

perhaps end, in this morning's interview."



"Lord Farnborough's character at college be designate as fair and

false," observe Sir John Spottiswoode.



"be it!"



"a fellow-collegian of his lordship's, Beverly, reside near Alverton.

he give I the character I now describe to you."



Christobelle feel uncomfortable at Sir John Spottiswoode's information.

it be always painful to hear depreciate account of those we admire,

or from whom we have receive kindness. she know nothing of Lord

Farnborough--his lordship be nothing to she; but she regret so

agreeable a person should prove otherwise than estimable. could Mr.

Beverly's testimony be depend upon? Character should not be lightly

treat: if Lord Farnborough's character be at the mercy of Mr.

Beverly, it be but fair to ascertain Mr. Beverly's claim to belief.

under this impression, Christobelle hastily utter her thought, after

a second pause.



"Pray, Sir John, who be Mr. Beverly?"



"a neighbour in Worcestershire, and one of the good fellow in England.

why do you ask?"



"because I think your friend be ungenerous, in speak harshly of Lord

Farnborough, who perhaps never offend he."



"Beverly be once deeply offend by Lord Farnborough," reply Sir

John Spottiswoode.



"therefore, your friend be revengeful," she answer, quickly.



"Beverly have bear his injury like a man, and like a Christian,"

return Sir John. "all injury should be forgive; but some cannot be

forget till memory fail."



again the little band of french horn swell upon the still air, and

the two vessel, which have sail to the Douglas Isle, emerge from its

deep shadow. Christobelle start up.



"they be return to Clanmoray so late! oh! listen to that sweet,

soft air."



the simple strain of "farewell to Lochaber" steal softly on their

ear, and they sit silently gaze upon the little vessel, as they

near the cliff. suddenly the music break off, as if an accident have

occur; but the pause be of short duration--it be again break by

the lively and stirring note of "my love she's but a lassie yet." the

blood mount to Christobelle's forehead with undisguised pleasure and

surprise. she be discover in her retreat by the party below, and an

indescribable feeling shot across her heart, as it grasp at the idea

that Lord Farnborough have choose the air, and that he have command its

execution, as the vessel pass the cliff. she lean over the rock,

which form a barricade before the rural seat, and in fancy she could

distinguish the tall, slight figure of his lordship, stand in the

stern, with fold arm, as he stand when she wave her plaid in the

morning. Christobelle watch the vessel with intense attention, as it

glide on, and exclaim, with eager satisfaction,



"I see him!--I could point he out among a hundred!"



"whom do you see?" ask Sir John Spottiswoode, as he rise and

advanced to her side--"Whom be you note?"



Christobelle do not immediately reply. she continue gaze upon the

lake, and several of the party be also observe they through their

telescope from below.



"but, tell I, Miss Wetheral, whom you note among a hundred, in that

party," repeat Sir John Spottiswoode.



"he be stand with--no, he be sit--that very large personage, the

Duke of Forfar--you know the Duke of Forfar?"



"oh yes, I see. how gratify his grace would be at the knowledge of

having attract your observation! I think I see Lord Farnborough."



"whom do you see?--I fancy I recognise Lady Anna Herbert's feather; and

there be kind Miss Ponsonby," reply Christobelle, colour.



"Lord Farnborough be stand in the stern of the vessel, Miss

Wetheral: he be wave something--his handkerchief. who be he wave

to?"



a little conscious feeling prevent Christobelle from return the

salutation. she fear Sir John Spottiswoode would observe and smile

consider "fair and false." she have no belief in the insinuation,

but it cause a very unpleasant restraint. the vessel pass under the
