when it reappear, the distance do not allow they to distinguish the

party. they hear the full note of the french horn, however, till a

headland conceal the vessel from sight; and, ere the last faint note

die away, the sun be considerably below the horizon. Christobelle

and her companion return to Fairlee at the moment the servant be

pass through the hall with coffee.



the evening pass in conversation upon the past and present, and Sir

John Spottiswoode's society make Christobelle speedily forget the

attention of Lord Farnborough. the compliment of an attractive and

agreeable person, for a few hour, could not compete with the presence

of a dear friend, whose taste have lead her own in many instance, and

who have devote so much time to accomplish her talent for music and

painting. that friend have be remember during an absence of four

year; he have be often apostrophize in solitary walk, and she have

wish in silence and sincerity to renew their pleasant intercourse.

that boon be now grant. Sir John Spottiswoode be again her

companion, and what desire of her heart remain ungratified?



Christobelle lay her head upon her pillow, that night, in peaceful

thought; and if Lord Farnborough occasionally flit before her

eye--if the air of "my love be but a lassie yet" linger upon her

ear--yet Sir John Spottiswoode fill her mind. his dark hair, curl

in rich profusion over his brow--his manly expression--that benevolent

dark blue eye--who be equal to he in excellence?--nay, who be

superior, even in those evanescent gift which captivate the eye of

woman? whom do she love and venerate equal to Sir John Spottiswoode?



the following morning produce a long and perplexing conversation

between the mother and daughter, which extinguish all Christobelle's

happy feeling. no two being could possibly be more opposed in

feeling, in sentiment, and in action; and never yet do a colloquy

take place, without heart-burn arise on one side, and distressed

feeling on the other part, to sever the tie between parent and child.

in this morning's conference their opinion jar more painfully

than ever; for they be now in actual collision upon point which

must materially affect Christobelle's happiness, and her future

respectability of conduct. it take place after breakfast, while the

gentleman be perambulate the terrace, and order the horse

for an intended ride. Lady Wetheral commence her attack with that

flattering address which gain so much influence over poor human nature.



"my dear Bell, the arrival of your old friend have produce wonderful

effect. I be gratify at see your eye sparkle, and your

expression of countenance become animate. I may confess I be please

at behold my quiet daughter transform into a beauty, by the mere

play of pretty coquetry which Sir John Spottiswoode's arrival have

call forth."



"I det coquette and coquetry," answer Christobelle, seriously,

though she be not insensible to the agreeable intimation of she

suddenly acquire beauty.



"Nonsense, Bell; it be a woman's most potent argument--it be her most

powerful weapon--it be her most precious gift--because it be her

great attraction: do not undervalue it."



"I have not be many hour in Sir John Spottiswoode's company, mamma.

if I feel inclined to coquet, I have have no opportunity."



"a mother's eye be open, when the daughter's eye be close,"

reply Lady Wetheral, with her most win smile. "I dare say

you be not aware how prettily your eye seek, and fall beneath

Sir John's glance, last night, and at this morning's breakfast. I

congratulate you, Bell, upon a gift which will create more decide

effect from your ignorance of possess it. but I wish to call your

attention to my anxious wish--I wish you to attend to my counsel, and

I wish you to act by my advice."



"what be your counsel, mamma?"



"I have never yet fail in establish my daughter," her ladyship

continue, "because they act upon my advice, without argue its

propriety. Julia and Clara act solely by my wish, and they win

their high establishment."



"Poor Clara!" exclaim Christobelle, involuntarily.



"it be useless to pity those who would not conform to the propriety

of life," reply Lady Wetheral. "I give Clara to Sir Foster, with long

and serious entreaty to avoid all public scene and altercation with

her husband. I never countenance opposition in a wife. I implore

Clara to be obedient in appearance--so much can be do by prudent

management! I never contradict her father in my life. I effect

all my plan without a single quarrel. there be no occasion to quarrel

in matrimony. a woman's influence be and must be feel; but it end the

instant you appear to contend. Clara be ungrateful to reproach I as

the cause--the idea always make I nervous."



her ladyship apply lavender-water to her forehead and handkerchief,

and then proceed.



"Sir John Spottiswoode will propose to you before he quit Fairlee, but

I should wish"--



"Sir John Spottiswoode propose to _I_!" exclaim Christobelle, in the

utmost astonishment.



"all that surprise be foolish, Bell. you be now old enough to command

those start and blush which look so very fresh, so very girlish.

I be certain Sir John Spottiswoode will propose, and it rest with

yourself to attract Lord Farnborough."



the blood rush with impetuous pulsation into the face of

Christobelle, and it overspread her forehead, neck, and even her hand:

if Lady Wetheral observe the suffusion her word have produce, she

affect not to perceive it.



"I should advise you to be very cautious in your conduct to both

gentleman, my dear Bell. do not be see too exclusively with Sir John

Spottiswoode, to attract attention; and yet, do not check hope on his

part. if Lord Farnborough quit Clanmoray without intend any thing,

or merely flirt with you, then, let Sir John propose. Alverton be an

excellent _au pis aller_, but I would rather my dear Bell could be

salute Duchess of Forfar."



it be some moment before Christobelle could rally her thought

and spirit, after the mention of Lord Farnborough, in the light of

a future suitor. for one instant, only, the idea of his lordship's

affection shoot a gleam of ambition into her mind, but the paltry

feeling be soon extinguish for ever, and her heart fly back to the

remember excellence of her former instructor and friend. her mother

watch the working of her spirit.



"if Lord Farnborough call to-day, my love, I shall invite he to

dinner."



"his lordship be a guest at Clanmoray," observe Christobelle, hastily.



"he will be a guest at Fairlee soon," answer my mother, gaily. "I

could fancy myself quite well again, my dear child; quite alert, as I

use to be; your little '_minauderie_' will raise I into new life and

spirit. I be sure Lord Farnborough be handsome and of high bearing. I

be sure he be clever and agreeable; your little coquetry will divert

I into health again."



"but, mamma--"



"no 'if' and 'but,' my dear Bell. I have every dependence upon your

attraction. Sir John Spottiswoode be astonish at your improved

appearance."



"listen to I, if you please, mamma. I be no coquette; and I would

rather die than be consider a character so repugnant to all that be

holy and upright."



"my dear girl, forbear sentiment. a little sentiment, if you please, to

Lord Farnborough, but not to I."



"I have no wish to marry, mamma," plead Christobelle, with earnest

seriousness. "I have no wish to leave papa, and--I have no wish

to marry Sir John Spottiswoode, and I cannot try to attract any

body. pray, do not advise I to avoid Sir John, or to think of any

establishment. don't let I endure the fate of Clara, or Julia's long

banishment."



Lady Wetheral's hand begin to tremble, and her feature become

agitated as she speak.



"I be well use to disobedience, and this only add to my accumulation

of vexation. every thing have conspire to make I miserable, and I

have one child leave to reproach I with bitterness. how could I expect

obedience from a headstrong girl, whose masculine education defy

restraint!"



"indeed, mamma, I be anxious to do right. indeed, my wish be to please

every one; but I cannot think it right to treat Sir John Spottiswoode

ill."



"who enjoin you to do so?" say her ladyship, in a querulous tone.



"I cannot--indeed, I cannot trifle with two gentleman, till I ascertain

the intention of one of they. do not ask I to do so, I beseech you,

for it go against every feeling of my heart." Christobelle burst into

tear.



"I detest such stupid folly! pray, don't imagine that your frown will

destroy the peace of either gentleman. man do not now suffer more than

an hour's annoyance; a new flame soon light the expire ember of an

old _penchant_."



"I be very glad to hear it, mamma."



"I only counsel you to mete out your attention to each gentleman

alike, Bell, and to distinguish neither at present. I imagine nothing

unholy in a line of conduct which preserve a proper and just decorum

in your manner."



"I will do any thing you please, mamma; only do not ask I to trifle

with Sir John Spottiswoode."



"you will do every thing which please yourself, and nothing which

please I. I perfectly understand your meaning; but allow I also to

observe, that I will hold no intercourse with a daughter who presume

to lecture her parent. I will have no communication with a young woman

who insolently defy her mother, and insist upon act accord to

her own weak judgment."



"do not suppose I defy you, mamma. there be nothing I would not do

to give you pleasure--nothing I would not do to increase your comfort;

only I beseech you not to compel I into a conduct my heart disown as

ungenerous and wicked."



"of course, a parent be wrong--of course, a mother be not a proper

judge, compare with a child's great wisdom, in any affair connect

with that child's welfare. I be aware of your high opinion of yourself.

I have long know your freedom, from every proper feeling which

soften and decorate a woman's mind. remain single, Bell, and be the

prototype of your great aunt, Miss Christobelle Wetheral; sink like

she into insignificant old maidism. but don't let my eye contemplate

you, an excrescence in your family--an incubus upon its glory. all my

daughter have marry splendidly, and I cannot be encumber by a

stupid daughter, who throw every advantage from she, and consider an

admirer an unholy appendage."



Tears flow silently down her daughter's cheek. Christobelle could

hold no dialogue with a mother, whose ironical manner and determinedly

oppose view distress she, and darken the prospect of her life.

her silence become a matter of offence.



"if weeping be to accompany your talent for continual and insolent

opposition, Miss Wetheral, I will request you to leave I: my own

nerve be sufficiently shatter."



Christobelle rise, and quit the room; Sir John Spottiswoode

come towards she from the hall, as she close the door of the

breakfast-room. he do not notice her emotion; he do not even speak,

but he gently draw her arm within his own, and lead she upon the terrace

which command the view of the lake. they take one turn in silence,

and then Sir John Spottiswoode speak of his admiration of Lochleven,

and gradually draw Christobelle into cheerful conversation. he ask

her opinion concern the morning's plan of amusement. "if she do not

prefer riding, he should feel inclined to consider the day just the

very thing for a water excursion." Christobelle be very willing to

resign herself into Sir John's hand. the conversation of the morning

have damp the glow of pleasure, and give a melancholy tinge to her

thought, which could not be immediately shake off. she therefore

answer slowly--"Yes, any thing; let we go upon the water, if you wish

it."



Sir John Spottiswoode press her arm to his side so slightly, that she

could scarcely write it down a pressure, as he reply:



"I will have nothing do without your full concurrence. if you do not

feel inclined to go on the water, let the original plan be adopt."



"I believe my tone be rather dismal this morning," she reply, with

more cheerfulness; "but I quite approve of your idea. we will certainly

row to the Douglas Isle."



Sir John Wetheral accompany they in their little excursion; and as

they glide towards the Isle, the fresh air, the light dip of the oar,

and the conversation of her two companion, restore Christobelle's

spirit to its usual buoyancy. Sir John Spottiswoode watch the ebb

and flow of her countenance, and bent towards she. "this be perfect

enchantment. tell I now why you be so melancholy."



Christobelle shake her head smilingly. "do not put I in mind of it,

but sing I a swiss air; that air I love to hear at Wetheral."



"you have remember it, even among these distant scene?"



"it have never fade from my recollection. on the contrary, these rock

and mountain bring it still more freshly to memory."



Sir John Spottiswoode instantly sing the swiss air with spirit, and

his voice sound melodiously on the water, which lie so calmly,

so beautifully still: not a breath of air curl a ripple upon its

surface. again and again the song recommence, and all Christobelle's

trouble be forget in the delicious harmony. she do not know

she sit gaze upon the singer, till Sir John Spottiswoode suddenly

pause, and their eye meet: Christobelle be not aware her attention

be so exclusively bestow upon he, till the expression of his glance

recall her thought. she turn from he in confusion, and fix

her contemplation upon the mountain which rise gradually above each

other, till their head be lose in cloud. she look no more towards

Sir John Spottiswoode.



the little party sit converse some hour on a small pile of stone

raise under a tree, which, in former day, constitute the _plaisance_

of Lochleven Castle. this spot command the rich plain of Kinross, the

rocky hill which swell on either side, and the house which dot

the plain, and gleam in the sunshine. they think of the suffering

of Mary, when she inhabit the now-ruin building under which they

repose, not as a restless queen of Scots, but as a captive woman,

banish to an isle where her eye could only rest upon rock and water,

far from her home and friend.



Sir John Spottiswoode also tell of foreign scene, and compare the

beauty of Lochleven with the gigantic lake of the south. they could

not bear comparison; yet lochleven possess, in its diminutiveness,

every requisite for poetic beauty. it be lochleven; and lochleven

contain a succession of captivate scenery, delight to the eye

and mind. many might prefer the impose immensity of Geneva, of

Constance, or of Zurich; but all must admire lochleven. he do not see

the chamois bounding from cliff to cliff; but the mind love to repose

on the bold yet tranquil scene which he contemplate. he do not dread

the avalanche; but the soft landscape please an eye, sate with

precipice, glazier, torrent, and cataract. it be delightful to sit

by the side of friend, in the midst of scenery so beautiful, and yet

be able to say, "it be in our own land."



Christobelle listen, and forget Lord Farnborough. far more attractive

to her mind be the manly conversation of Sir John Spottiswoode,

than the empty compliment of a new acquaintance. how could she, for

an instant, feel disappointment at the thought of be absent from

Fairlee when his lordship call?



their return to the mainland be late; it be later still when they

reach Fairlee. they have linger by the way, and every turn present

new object to admire, and fresh subject for discussion. the half-hour

bell be peal its tone, and the echo reverberate from rock to

rock, as they gain the terrace. this incident produce another

pause: Sir John describe the effect of the echo among the mountain

of Switzerland, and the wild cry of the Switzers. Christobelle have

scarcely time to hurry into her room, and change her dress, before

they be summon into the dining-room. Lady Wetheral do not address

her daughter during dinner. she direct her discourse exclusively to

her husband, when any subject be intend particularly to attract

Christobelle's attention; otherwise, her manner be captivate as

ever, when she play the hospitable and agreeable hostess, at the head

of her table.



"my dear John, the Duke of Forfar call this morning." Christobelle's

colour rise, and her quick eye detect the little emotion. "I be

gratify by the call: his grace look remarkably well, and Lady Anna

Herbert as sprightly as usual. four year have roll by, and leave

their 'flow hair' unthinned. Lady Anna look quite as youthful as

she do when a '_belle confess_,' at your mother's ball, Sir John

Spottiswoode."



"she be a very fine girl, and an excellent flirt," remark Sir John.

"Charles and Lady Anna be great friend some year ago."



"I be very much pleased with Lord Farnborough," continue Lady

Wetheral, address her husband, and pass her eye slightly over

Christobelle. "Lord Farnborough accompany his party, and I have not

see such a finished gentleman many year."



Sir John Spottiswoode make no remark; and Christobelle be silent. Sir

John Wetheral ask if the great boy have grow into a fine-look

youth?



"I set Lord Farnborough down as decidedly handsome at the first glance,

my love; but I forget his beauty in his very finished manner."



Sir John make no further remark, and there be a short pause, till Lady

Wetheral resumed--



"Lord Farnborough speak with polite pleasure of his introduction to

my daughter yesterday; and he bring a note from Miss Ponsonby,

request we to join a party next Tuesday to St. Mungo's Isle. you

be include, Sir John, when our friend learn you be at Fairlee."



Sir John Spottiswoode bow.



"it be to be an early party, and there be sundry message deliver

which my poor head could not contain; but Lord Farnborough will call

again with more ample instruction. I tell he it be cruel to load my

memory with such matter."



"do the Forfar party continue long at Clanmoray," say Sir John

Spottiswoode, some moment after the subject have drop.



"I believe so," be Lady Wetheral's reply; "indeed, Lord Farnborough

mention his own protract stay, when the rest leave for Farnborough

Stacy. I forget when _they_ depart."



"perhaps there be attractive metal in Miss Fanny Ponsonby," observe

Sir John Wetheral.



"there be attraction somewhere," reply his lady, "for there be a

lover's touch in his description of Lochleven, and in his anxiety for

the party to St. Mungo's Isle."



"allow I the pleasure of take wine with you, Miss Wetheral," say

Sir John Spottiswoode, bend forward. the subject again drop.



the half hour's interregnum after dinner, be pass in lecture on

Lady Wetheral's part. the lady have scarcely enter the drawing-room,

when Christobelle's attention be again require upon the subject so

painfully argue in the morning.



"I wish to try your narrow capacity once more, Bell, and to ascertain

whether you really possess one spark of that wholesome ambition which

dignify a woman of birth."



"indeed, mamma, I hope so. I would not for world stoop to commit a

mean action, or indulge a mean thought. my very great ambition be to

act like a lady, and, by so do, meet every one's respect."



"that be all very well, Bell, but that be not exactly my meaning. to

be respectable, you must soar. it be vain to content one's-self with

grovel just above the head of the canaille. the proper ambition be

to grasp at high thing, and possess they."



"I have no wish for high thing, mamma."



"because your nature be common-place, Bell, because your mind be low

set. however you may pique yourself upon your accomplished education,

that very education have cripple my hope, and your own prospect. you

will live and die, satisfied with mediocrity."



"but, mamma, what do you mean, and what be I to do to give you

satisfaction? I cannot understand you."



"I will explain myself, Bell. be you a girl of such a mean spirit,

as to accept a baronet, when a duke's son enter the list of suitor?

answer I--be you so mean-spirit, so mediocre in your wish, as to

content yourself with a man who cannot raise you above your fellow?"



"certainly not, mamma, if I do not love he."



"love he! could you love a man--would you dare to plead attachment

to a man, as an excuse for lower yourself in marriage below your

sister' fortune? would _you_ meanly creep, while _their_ flight have

carry they to this world's pinnacle? I hope, I trust, you would not

do so, Bell!"



"whom can you allude to?" exclaim Christobelle, distress beyond

measure at her mother's word; "tell I at once, I beseech you, what

you mean. do not speak to I in parable."



Lady Wetheral become extremely agitated. she walk to the window,

throw open the sash, and close it again, as she speak.



"I have say enough to waken your understanding. any one might

comprehend my meaning--any one would know I detest the idea of your

marrying Sir John Spottiswoode."



Christobelle look up in her mother's face with astonishment. she

continue with increase nervousness.



"you cannot deceive _I_, Bell. you cannot deny your predilection for

that man, which will at once decide the intention, and end all hope

of Lord Farnborough. you be determined to pursue your will, and I will

act upon my own resolution. the very hour in which you accept Sir John

Spottiswoode, shall be the last of your residence with I."



"good heaven, mamma, I have not a thought of Sir John Spottiswoode, or

Sir John Spottiswoode of I! what can have cause such a supposition in

your mind?"



"you do not care for he--you _will_ not care for he--be that your

meaning, Bell?"



"I do not care for any one, half so much as for my own papa, and I hope

I shall always prefer he," she exclaim, energetically.



"Folly, and nonsense!--girl's folly," resume Lady Wetheral, "by your

blush I might have give you credit for ambition; but your walk and

sailing with Sir John Spottiswoode, incline I to fear you will give

yourself to a poor baronet."



"I do not know he be poor, mamma."



"comparatively speak with Sir Foster Kerrison, he _be_ poor. what

be a paltry income of three thousand pound, compare with the wealthy

dukedom of Forfar?"



"be I to marry the Duke of Forfar?" exclaim Christobelle, start

from her chair in horror.



"not the present duke, Bell, though he be a remarkably fine man, and

not more than sixty year of age. many young lady might approve the

Duke of Forfar; but I allude to his very handsome, very accomplished

mannered son."



Christobelle could have listen to her mother's eulogium with infinite

pleasure at an early period, and before she have deprecate Sir John

Spottiswoode. but her soul rise against persecution. she could not

endure to hear her friend lower, or to be at once command not to

like a man whom she love in innocence, and without a thought of future

connexion. from that moment, Sir John Spottiswoode become a martyr in

her eye, and Lord Farnborough sink into a secondary personage. Lady

Wetheral await her daughter's reply some moment, but her mind be

too busily employ decide her feeling.



"you be very thoughtful, Bell. think well upon my word, and act with

become spirit."



"I have think, and I have decide," reply Christobelle, firmly.



"but do not look so ashy pale, my dear Bell; these little struggle

be trifle, compare with a long existence of nonentity. I give up a

very powerful attachment, to please my wise and reflect mother. I

relinquish Captain Blennerhasset for your father, and I find she

remark perfectly just, by the course of event. she implore I to

forbear marry an irish officer, with little more than his pay, when

a prospect arise before I of become mistress of Wetheral Castle.

she assure my romantic fancy, that Love could not survive the attack

of poverty, and she warn I to avoid the misery of follow my

husband into disagreeable quarter, where I must sink into a captain's

lady, a title of far less importance than the general's mistress. I

follow my dear mother's prudent advice, and break off my engagement

with Blennerhasset."



Christobelle be interested in the fate of her mother's unfortunate

lover, and she ask what have become of Captain Blennerhasset?



"he marry somebody of distinction," she reply, "and fall at

Badajos. his widow and four child be now live upon the bounty

of their friend. my mother's counsel be wise, and I be fortunately

prevail upon to act with propriety."



"Poor Captain Blennerhasset!"



"poverty be always pitiable," resume Lady Wetheral. "I consider

people equally poor, whose income will not allow they to compete with

their neighbour. I should say poor Lady Spottiswoode, if you be the

wife of our excellent guest,..."



"Alverton be a handsome estate," remark Christobelle.



"very well for a nobody," reply Lady Wetheral, haughtily, "but a

wretched pittance for a Miss Wetheral, who have attract the notice of

Lord Farnborough. I see his watchful look towards the door, Bell. I

mark his lordship's glance towards the lake, when he hear of your

visit to the island; every thing be in your power, if you will but

listen to your mother's counsel."



"do not talk to I of marriage, mamma, I implore you," cry

Christobelle, as the gentleman enter from the dining-room. Sir John

Spottiswoode take his seat near she as usual; she think he look

more benevolent, more interesting than ever. Matrimony never couple

itself with her admiration of Sir John, but to be command to approve

he less than Lord Farnborough--to consider _he_ poor and undesirable,

who have improve her well taste, and increase her store of good!

no, that should never be. Christobelle be too young to wish to marry,

too happy and free to think of fetter: but her right hand would forget

its cunning, ere she cease to esteem Sir John Spottiswoode beyond

every human being.



"shall we walk this evening?" he ask, as thought pass too rapidly

through her mind to allow of speech. Christobelle colour, and turn

mechanically towards her mother. Lady Wetheral see her emotion.



"my dear child look fatigue, Sir John. shall we advise she to be

quiet this evening? a long morning upon the water have lessen she

bloom."



"one little turn upon the terrace only, Miss Wetheral." Sir John

offer his arm.



"my dear Bell, even the terrace will fatigue you," observe she

ladyship, with anxiety.



"one turn to watch that sunset, Lady Wetheral! I will bring Miss

Wetheral back before fatigue attack she."



"my dear Bell!..."



"I will not detain her many minutes--_one_ turn, my pupil."



Christobelle could not resist that title. she rise, and accompany

Sir John Spottiswoode upon the terrace. one turn be take, and they

pause to watch the golden beam sink behind the mountain. another and

another be agree upon, just to watch the pale gleam depart. be

it, indeed, her mother's prohibition which give so much interest to her

companion's remark? be it her prohibition which throw a charm over

his conversation, and cause Christobelle to linger in his society? she

know not--but it be dark when they return into the drawing-room, and

the coffee have be forget. Lady Wetheral's eye turn upon her

daughter with an offended expression, but Christobelle forget their

glance in please retrospection that night. Christobelle dream of

Sir John Spottiswoode, and their early first day of acquaintance, when

Lady Wetheral approve and seek his intimacy, and she have enjoy it

undisturbed, without a reference to Lord Farnborough.









CHAPTER XXIV.





Lady Wetheral complain the follow morning of her nerve. she

assure Sir John Spottiswoode her alarm about her daughter's health

induce the attack, and she hope Christobelle would not think of

quit Fairlee ground that day. when that dear girl be long absent,

her fear become overpower, and a frequent recurrence of such

disquietude might bring on a serious illness. she hope Bell would find

amusement in the house, and be prevail upon to forego her long walk.

Sir John Spottiswoode should not suffer by her nervous feeling. she

be aware her husband admire and seek out point of scenery almost

as enthusiastically as Bell, and he would be delighted to attend he in

his ride.



Sir John Spottiswoode smile. "I will also decline leave the house,

if you please. since my pupil have suffer by my selfish pleasure, I

will dedicate myself to her entertainment--we will sketch the lake from

the terrace."



"that would be most pleasant; but I fear my poor nerve be in the way

again, my dear Sir John. I do not like to see my daughter bend over

her drawing."



"Miss Wetheral shall not bend over her drawing: I will read to you

both; I will read the 'Lady of the Lake.'"



"that will be most agreeable--most entertaining," observe she

ladyship. "my dear Bell, you be so partial to Sir Walter Scott's

poem!"



yes, Christobelle be a warm admirer of Sir Walter's poetry; but she

think still more of the pleasure she should experience in hear it

read aloud by Sir John Spottiswoode. Christobelle acknowledge "how

gratify she would feel, by hear the 'Lady of the Lake'--that she

preferred '_la lecture_' even to a sketch of the bright Lochleven.

she would bring her netting, and her father should sit by she in his

comfortable chair."



every thing be arrange, shortly after the conclusion of breakfast,

for the reading; but, ere the gentleman return from their morning

visit to the stable and garden, Lady Wetheral express she

satisfaction at the arrangement.



"I have manage to withdraw you from a walk, Bell. I dislike those

walk. your name would soon become couple with Sir John Spottiswoode,

which I will not allow. if Lord Farnborough call to-day, every thing

be in its proper order. place a chair for the reader, between your

father and myself, my love: our ear be old than your youthful

member."



"I think the Clanmoray party call yesterday, mamma?"



"they do so--and yet I have a presentiment that Lord Farnborough will

appear again to-day. remember, Bell, I do not extend my prohibition to

Lord Farnborough. you may walk with Lord Farnborough."



"that would give offence to Sir John Spottiswoode, mamma."



"leave _I_ to manage Sir John Spottiswoode, my love."



"I shall not wish to walk; I shall remain at home to-day, if you

please, mamma."



"I do not prescribe your hour, my dear Bell. Walk when and where you

like, so you be not conspicuous with Sir John Spottiswoode. I warn you

in time, that I will listen to no proposal which do not emanate from

Lord Farnborough; and no plea from yourself, which have reference to our

present guest. you be warn in time, remember!"



"I should never think of, or hope to attach, Sir John Spottiswoode,"

Christobelle reply, calmly; "I only wish to be allow free liberty

to enjoy his conversation."



"there be a very homely adage, Bell, which say, 'prevention be well

than a cure.' Lay its meaning to your heart."



Christobelle do not continue the dialogue. she give her whole

attention to her netting, till the gentleman return, and till Sir

John Spottiswoode commence his reading: her whole soul be then

engross in the fate of the fair and gentle Ellen. gradually her hand

relax their grasp, as the story proceed--gradually her eye turn

upon the reader, and her netting fall disregard upon the carpet. she

be listen to the scene where Malcolm hear the praise of Ellen

from the lip of Douglas:



    "if a father's partial thought

    O'erweighed her worth and beauty aught--

    well might the lover's judgment fail

    to balance with a juster scale;

    for with each secret glance he stole,

    the fond enthusiast send his soul."



the eye of Sir John Spottiswoode rest upon Christobelle as he speak

these line, and she feel a pain at her heart before unknown, and now

indescribable. Lady Wetheral catch the mutual expression, and be

strike by the sudden paleness of her daughter's countenance. she turn

to Sir John Spottiswoode.



"you will smile at a lady's nerve, and decide we to be

incomprehensible being; but the continual flow of your voice vibrate

upon my nerve in a peculiar manner. I _must_ feel unwell, since a

voice like your own create nervousness. I will retire, for a short

period, to appeal once more to camphor-julep. my dear Bell will give I

her arm."



Sir John Spottiswoode rise in alarm.



"my dear sir, these trifling attack be become less and less

frequent. my daughter and myself will leave you and Sir John together.

I trust these attack be not to be often repeat; but we shall meet

at luncheon, I hope, quite recovered."



the mother and daughter quit the sitting-room; but, as they

pass through the door, hold open by Sir John Spottiswoode, he take

Christobelle's hand, and kindly hope she would not be too ill to enjoy

a breeze upon the terrace. "oh! yes, this evening I shall truly enjoy

the pure air," she reply, withdraw her hand as they pass on.



"if there be any thing most displease to I," observe Lady Wetheral,

as they enter her dressing-room, "it be comprise in that familiar

action of shake hand upon every occasion. I beg you will avoid it in

future."



"it be merely to express a kind wish, _en passant_," Christobelle

remark, "that Sir John Spottiswoode just touch my hand."



"familiarity which begin _en passant_, end in contempt _pour

toujour_, Miss Wetheral."



"would you wish to lie down, mamma, or shall I ring for Mrs. Bevan?"

ask her daughter, willing to change the subject.



"Neither, Miss Wetheral. I wish simply to remain here, and, if you

please, you shall read to I."



Christobelle continue read to her mother, who sat recline in a

lounging-chair, till a tap at the door announce Mrs. Bevan in wait.

her ladyship touch a little silver hand-bell by her side, to indicate

to Mrs. Bevan that she might enter. she bring a summon from her

master, to beg the lady would make their appearance.



"tell Frederick, Mrs. Bevan, to inform your master we will descend when

the luncheon be announce: I be very nervous and unwell."



"my lady, I believe my Lord Farnborough be in the drawing-room."



"oh! very well, Mrs. Bevan, we be come immediately." Mrs. Bevan

vanish. "my dear love, just draw out your beautiful curl; and if you

could pass a narrow blue ribbon negligently through your hair, it would

give great effect to its jetty abundance. a little more animation in

your manner, Bell, and a little less paleness, be to be desire. yes,

that blush have have great effect--now let we proceed, ere it vanish."



they enter the drawing-room, and Christobelle's eye first seek

Sir John Spottiswoode. he be stand at the window, but he turn

towards the lady, as the little bustle of their entrance reach his

ear, and advance with alacrity. Lady Wetheral hold out her hand. "I

be much well, my dear Sir John--your countenance ask the question.

I have have repose--perfect repose--and I be well. my daughter be

my medicine." her ladyship still hold Christobelle's arm, and move

gracefully forward. "my lord, you be the bearer of Miss Ponsonby's

wish. my poor memory have part with the recollection of your message

yesterday, before my daughter's return."



Christobelle bow to Lord Farnborough, and would have apologize for

her absence the precede day, by state Sir John Spottiswoode's

arrival, have not the attention of the latter be fix upon she.

she could perceive that he watch she narrowly, and that knowledge

impart an awkwardness to her manner which she could not shake

off. Christobelle stop abruptly in her speech, and hesitate.

Lord Farnborough have greatly the advantage in perfect ease of

manner. Christobelle feel her insufficiency, and it cause great

agitation--for what would Sir John Spottiswoode think of her folly?



Lord Farnborough enter gaily into conversation, and he do not allude

to his disappointment, or recur to the event which have pass. he be

charge with Miss Ponsonby's complimentary fear lest Christobelle

should become a defaulter to St. Mungo's Isle, and her hope that the

party would assemble at Clanmoray, before the aquatic expedition take

place. it be hope that Lady Wetheral would accompany the Fairlee

party, and forget her fear of the water.



"you will be under my guidance, Lady Wetheral," continue Lord

Farnborough, "and I be an experienced sailor. Ponsonby head a

detachment also, but I particularly request your daughter and yourself

will place yourself under my care."



"we will certainly enlist under your banner, Lord Farnborough; we

prefer the sailor to the soldier, upon the water," say Lady Wetheral,

her countenance light up with pleasure, and all her ailment

forget. "I shall accept, with pleasure, Miss Ponsonby's invitation,

and I will _try_ to forget my fear."



"I shall ride over on Tuesday, to escort you," resume Lord

Farnborough. "since you consider yourself my peculiar care, I

shall certainly take charge of you from your own door. Miss Ponsonby

declare, if I monopolize the lady, she will insist upon be

attend by the gentleman. she therefore appropriate Sir John Wetheral

and your guest."



"a charmingly novel arrangement," exclaim Lady Wetheral, delight

to believe that Sir John Spottiswoode would not enter her appoint

vessel. "I be amuse by the peculiar novelty. tell I who form your

exclusive party?"



"oh! I have secure Lady Anna and Fanny Ponsonby--Mrs. Ponsonby have

declare off altogether--the Greys, and the two Quintens."



"the handsome Quintens?"



"yes, the handsome, tall Quintens--second only to the incomparable

Fanny Ponsonby."



Christobelle think Lord Farnborough handsome--very handsome, that

morning. if Sir John Spottiswoode have not arrive at Fairlee--if Lady

Wetheral have not torture her heart, by _compel_ its obedience--by

endeavour to lower her opinion of the friend she esteem--if,

a thousand if--Christobelle have perhaps admire his lordship, and

fall a victim to her mother's wish. but, now!--a thousand Lord

Farnboroughs could not fill up the sum of her preference to the society

of Sir John Spottiswoode. love be a deity unknown, unwished for. she

only pray to pass her existence with her father, and to see sometimes

the friend she so greatly venerate.



Lord Farnborough remain some hour at Fairlee; and when

Christobelle's confusion, which arise at Sir John Spottiswoode's

scrutiny, have subside, she could also join in the passing

conversation. many complimentary nothing fall from Lord Farnborough's

lip, to which she reply with a banter which arise from the

collision of their wit--not from a heart gratify by empty verbiage.

Christobelle be at rest from reproach; and her spirit rise from their

contact with lively observation and sprightly repartee.



Sir John Spottiswoode do not join in the wordy war, but her father

smile in pleased approbation, and often rescue his daughter from the

horn of a dilemma. Lady Wetheral's satisfaction lie deep in her heart,

but she sit composedly silent, as the brilliant scintillation of wit

play round she. it be after the departure of Lord Farnborough, that

she speak her feeling in one concise, but too dangerous, sentence.



"Bell, walk _now_ when and where you please, with Sir John

Spottiswoode."



Christobelle be again at liberty to walk by the side of her

friend--again free to claim his society, without reproachful look and

unkind expression! how joyfully do she avail herself of the bless

privilege! her mother smile at their repeat absence, and express

no curiosity to learn their subject of conversation. Christobelle

draw fearlessly to the side of Sir John Spottiswoode, or lean upon

his arm with confidence, as they watch the sun's decline from the

terrace. she be the happy creature breathing, till the day of their

engagement to Clanmoray.



and yet Christobelle fancy there be symptom of reserve on the part

of her companion. conversation become, she think, less gaily free,

less continuous. there be repeat and long pause, which she could

not break, and Sir John Spottiswoode appear to covet. they sit one

morning in their rocky seat, without exchange a single word, till Sir

John suddenly exclaimed,--



"this be, indeed, perfect happiness."



Christobelle smile.



"we be _silent_ adorer of Nature; but our feeling be not the less

sincere."



"powerful admiration be in the heart, not upon the lip," reply she

companion, sigh.



"yet we admire the scene as fervently when we chat and sing upon

the lake," observe Christobelle.



"that be eye-service, Miss Wetheral. the glorious scenery _then_

delight my eye, but have not reach my heart; its effect now be

very soothing, yet melancholy."



"don't let I interrupt your meditation, then," Christobelle reply,

with a little feeling of offended pride, which have never rise in

her bosom till that moment. she be ashamed of its existence, but it

_would_ display itself.



"I have not the sprightly and win tongue of Lord Farnborough, Miss

Wetheral. I cannot be witty and yet feel deeply."



"Lord Farnborough," reply Christobelle, colour, "be not in my

thought."



"I speak unadvisedly, my dear pupil: forgive the stern schoolmaster."



Sir John Spottiswoode hold out his hand, and when do Christobelle

resist that affectionate title, which recall his instruction, and

their happy day at Wetheral? she give her own hand with the delight

of heart which every one experience who renew a happy intercourse

with half displeased friend. Sir John Spottiswoode hold it for some

moment; and when it be withdraw gently from his grasp, they again

relapse into silence. the dressing-bell startle they from their long

reverie.



"oh, that tiresome bell!" exclaim Christobelle, "how dismally and

faithfully it summon one from mental enjoyment to the creature

comfort!"



"it be wisely order!" reply Sir John Spottiswoode, place she

arm within his own. "I will tell you why I judge it so, as we climb

this steep. we enjoy all thing by comparison, and in their variety.

mental pleasure depend upon calm bodily tranquillity; and where the

constitution suffer, there be little leisure for the mind to absorb

itself in its own reverie. there! you have slip, and hurt your

foot!"



"but the dressing-bell--you have not yet illustrate your position!"

exclaim Christobelle, in some confusion, as her companion catch she

fall, by throw his arm round her waist, though it be instantly

withdraw.



"I be go to enter upon it as you fall, my argumentative pupil. a

calm mind depend upon bodily repose, which demand support, which

be effect by food, which be denote by the dressing-bell to be

prepare. have I not state it truly and concisely."



"yes; a perfect 'House that Jack build,' in its tripping measure. you

will rival Lord Farnborough."



"what be the meaning of this extraordinary arrangement, that all the

lady be to sail in one vessel, Miss Wetheral?"



"Miss Ponsonby's whim, I believe. I look forward with pleasure to our

party to-morrow."



"do you?"



"yes: I feel light as the heather-bell, and quite ready to aim poison

arrow at Lord Farnborough. will it amuse you?"



"not particularly."



"you do not like Lord Farnborough?"



"I have no reason to feel entertainment in his lordship's society. I be

not an admirer of his conversation."



Christobelle think Sir John Spottiswoode speak a little bitterly

of poor Lord Farnborough, but it do not surprise she. doubtless,

Mr. Beverly's suppose injury have take effect upon his friend's

mind, and prejudice he against his lordship. Christobelle do not,

however, continue a subject in itself uninteresting. Lord Farnborough

have no charm for she; she only feel amuse by his sprightly power.

while her father and Sir John Spottiswoode be near, Christobelle's

spirit ever rise into gaiety: she would be gaiety itself during

the water excursion, and Sir John would be gay too, only he speak

so deprecatingly of the affair. they do not linger on the terrace.

Christobelle have only time to promise her companion that the evening

should be devote to music, and she hurry to her room. the second

bell sound ere she could reach the drawing-room.



the following morning rise in sunny smile. a mist have clear away,

leave the sparkle water of the lake bright and clear; and

Christobelle's spirit be unusually high at the prospect of she

happy day of pleasurable anticipation. she speak in metaphor, and

think in rhyme; but her astonishment be great, in behold the

coolness of Sir John Spottiswoode's manner, and view the gravity

of his countenance at breakfast. Christobelle's most lively sally

pass perfectly unnoticed and unheeded. she could not win one smile,

or obtain one remark from her friend. his eye appear heavy, and

Christobelle fancy he must suffer from conceal illness, otherwise

he would have catch the infection of her spirit. the thought of Sir

John Spottiswoode suffering sober her vivacity. she become grave, and

gradually even sad, to witness his dejection. Christobelle approach

he when they rise from the breakfast-table; her mother have quit the

breakfast-room, and she fear no misconstruction of her anxiety.



"I know you be ill. I be sure you be unfit to join a party full of

mirth."



"I believe I be unequal to this morning's gaiety; certainly quite

unfit for mirth," be the dejected answer.



"how very annoying that it should take place to-day; or how provoking

that you should ever be ill! do you rise unwell?"



"no; I be perfectly well when I enter the breakfast-room; but a

few turn on the terrace with Lady Wetheral, before you appear, have

cause a head and heart ache. I cannot remain at Fairlee solitary, but

I be too ill to mix in a party of pleasure."



"I wish we be both go in our own boat, to our own island, to be

quiet," Christobelle exclaim. "I do not enjoy large party when my

friend be ill."



"you will not observe my sickness of heart, Miss Wetheral. you will be

gaily engage."



"not if you be ill."



Christobelle be not aware of the compliment convey in her

observation. she speak from her heart simply and sincerely, without

consider its flattering tendency. Sir John Spottiswoode catch she

hand, and release it again suddenly. he turn abruptly away.



"do not speak so recklessly, so heartlessly, I beseech you!"



"I never be suspect of heartlessness, Sir John Spottiswoode!"



Christobelle also turn away, for proud tear rise at the unexpected

attack. she be quit the room.



"stay one moment, and say you forgive I," he cry with energy.

"forgive I, Miss Wetheral--forgive I, my generous pupil!"



Christobelle turn at the last expression, and her emotion be

apparent, for he catch she in his arm.



"I cannot support this sight! what right have I to presume to give pain!

what right have I to breathe a harsh expression towards a creature all

heart, and all nobleness!"



"I be not angry," she reply, withdraw from his embrace--"I be not

angry, Sir John Spottiswoode; only I do not deserve the appellation of

heartless. I speak in sincerity and truth."



"I know you do. I be wrong to speak as I do--forgive I!"



"I do forgive you," she answer, smile, and another long pressure of

the hand attest their reconciliation.



"calm a penitent spirit by a stroll on the terrace, and talk to I,

that I may forget my fault and its cause. let I hear your voice

again, and let I hear it till Lord Farnborough arrive."



the friend walk nearly an hour together. Christobelle's spirit

be again elevate, and she chat with renew vivacity. Sir

John Spottiswoode walk smilingly by her side, listen to her

anticipation of his illness disperse in the fresh air of Lochleven;

but he be not himself. he reply to her remark, and lend his power

in playful conversation; but they be not _give_. he often sigh,

and repeatedly compel his companion to bespeak his attention.



"you tell I to chat, and your mind be far away," she say, at last,

weary with receive no reply.



"but I have not lose a word. I hear you with the most vivid attention,

because you will not long honour I."



"why so, I pray you, gentle coz?"



"you will be engross by Lord Farnborough!"



"that, then, will be your own fault!"



he look earnestly in Christobelle's face, and shake his head.



"say it once more, my pupil."



"it will be your own fault, if _any one_ engross my attention. why

should I be inattentive to my naughty schoolmaster?"



Lord Farnborough bound from the drawing-room window upon the terrace,

and advance towards they. Christobelle feel her companion's arm relax;

she look reproachingly towards he.



"you wish already to get rid of your poor pupil?"



"never, never!" be the subdue reply; but Lord Farnborough stand

before they.



"you be ready, I see. be not this a glorious day? Clanmoray be in a

proper bustle, and the lake never look so beautiful. Miss Ponsonby

declare she will be the 'Lady of the Lake,' and dress in costume as

you do, Miss Wetheral. she hope some 'Malcolm' of Lochleven will

start forth and woo she, but she rather despair of such good fortune.

Malcolm will be attract elsewhere."



a low bow from his lordship point the compliment, and Christobelle

curtsey to its meaning. Sir John Spottiswoode would not enter into

the unmeaning dialogue which succeed: he pertinaciously avoid

even lend a smile to Lord Farnborough. how deeply he resent, in

Christobelle's eye, the offence offer to his friend Beverly!



it be a beautiful drive to Clanmoray. Lady Wetheral, forgetful of she

long confinement--her nervous feeling--and the painful remembrance

of Clara's death, chat through the carriage-window with Lord

Farnborough, as gaily and as sportively as ever. Christobelle amuse

herself with rally Sir John Spottiswoode upon his illness, which

she assure he be affect, to try the sympathy of his friend. he

rebut the idea with excellent good humour, and they enter the

ground of General Ponsonby in most merry mood.



two or three groupe be seat in picturesque attitude, listen to

Captain Ponsonby, who strike a guitar with great spirit, and amuse the

company with Spanish Boleros and moorish song. the wetheral' arrival

be the signal to embark; and, in the confusion of introduction,

reception, compliment, decision upon the fit and unfit, and Miss

Ponsonby's determination to be the Lady of the Lake, Christobelle find

herself descend the woode hill which slope to the water' edge,

escort by Lord Farnborough and Mr. Ponsonby. Lady Wetheral follow

closely upon her daughter's step, lean upon the arm of the Duke of

Forfar. Christobelle cast linger look at the groupe which dot

the pathway, but she could not distinguish the figure of Sir John

Spottiswoode.



"well, we look neat, however," say Mr. Ponsonby, crack a whip,

which never depart from his right hand.



"be my father near, Mr. Ponsonby?" Christobelle ask, anxiously. she

be sure Sir John Spottiswoode would be near _he_, and her heart

wish to ascertain his movement. she dared not appear interested in

the whereabout of her "tutor," to attract notice. Mr. Ponsonby crack

his whip, and look behind he.



"Sir John Wetheral and your friend be escort my sister. do observe

the pretty effect of the groupe descend the glen."



they turn to admire the picturesque figure which adorn the woody

scene. Lady Wetheral also linger with his Grace of Forfar, to gaze

upon their effect.



"these scene be not to be find in Shropshire," observe his grace.

"the Wrekin lie upon the plain, like a whale in a dish, will be tame

work when we can remember Lochleven."



"and yet I sometimes sigh for the scene I have quit," say she

ladyship. "I confess I love the busy hum of man, and Lochleven be

dreary in the winter month. I wish I could persuade my daughter she be

dull at Fairlee."



"Miss Wetheral love the grand seclusion of Lochleven, because she

taste have not be vitiate by society."



"my daughter be wed to calm life, and love no agitation beyond the

ruffle lake. I believe her spirit would pine in the gay world."



"so much the well, my good lady; her young mind be uncontaminate by

the art of a worldly life."



"my endeavour be to preserve its purity, and watch over its happiness,"

reply her ladyship, sigh.



at this moment the whole party become united again. Sir John

Spottiswoode quit Miss Fanny Ponsonby, and approach Christobelle.

Lady Wetheral perceive the movement, and she turn hastily round.



"my dear child, you be tired, you look pale. my lord, you have

outwalke even your 'genius of the Lake.'"



Lord Farnborough offer his arm, with many polite regret.

Christobelle decline it, courteously. she be quite equal to the

walk;--she feel no fatigue.



"Oblige I, my dear child," say Lady Wetheral, anxiously; "I cannot

be satisfied unless you accept his lordship's assistance. my dear girl,

make I happy."



Christobelle could hesitate no long. all eye be upon she; she be

actually in the way, and a remark from his grace confuse she.



"my dear young lady, you stand there, turn all the young man's

head. Harry, take away your prize, for we be at fault till you

proceed."



Christobelle be lead away, accordingly; and she see no more of Sir

John Spottiswoode, till they gain the shore of the lake. he be

walk still with Miss Fanny Ponsonby, when she behold he again.

he be apparently explain something to her comprehension, for she

be lean upon his arm, and he be point to the peak of Cona.

be he quote Ossian to the beautiful Fanny Ponsonby, regardless

of the party, and of the friend who would have listen so gladly?

do he mean to become the partner of Fanny Ponsonby, when he tell

she, in their early walk, that he should hear her own voice only on

the terrace?--when he tell she, _she_ would be appropriate by Lord

Farnborough? a pang of jealousy pierce her heart at the moment,

indescribably bitter; it be a pang so sudden, so tinctured with

despair, that she close her eye, and press her hand tightly upon

her heart. the movement attract the notice of Lord Farnborough.



"I fear you be ill, indeed, Miss Wetheral. I be sure you have find

the descent very fatiguing."



"I _be_ rather ill," exclaim Christobelle, still keep her eye

close. she could not endure the light, or the figure which flit

before she. she feel extremely giddy; so much so, she be apprehensive

of fall. an exclamation from her companion catch the ear of Lady

Wetheral, who be immediately at her daughter's side. Christobelle be

place upon a bank, and she lean against her mother's shoulder. she

wave away the gentleman.



"let no one come near I, mamma. let no one speak to I, just now."



the duke and his lordship politely retreat. Lady Wetheral bestow

her sweetest smile upon her daughter, but it be not see; and it would

have add only to Christobelle's disquiet, if it have meet her eye. her

ladyship be very soothing.



"no one shall distress you, Bell; but if, as I suspect, Lord

Farnborough have speak to you rather warmly, you must accustom

yourself to this sort of thing. only silly girl be overpower by a

love-speech or two;--do collect yourself, my love, and avoid attract

notice. this be all as it should be, but collect yourself."



"Lord Farnborough have not--" Christobelle could not proceed: she feel

gasp for breath.



"of course not, my dear Bell. a symphony precede an air--every thing

will steal on in proper order. rouse yourself. your father be come to

we--do not appear girlish."



her father's presence and touch revive the spirit of Christobelle.

she rise, and lean upon his arm; she feel well when her father be

near she. she entreat to be allow to walk with _him_--to be with

he on the water and on land. she should be quite well and happy, quite

compose, if she walk only with _him_--with her father.



"my dear Bell, do not shock I by any display of folly. Lord

Farnborough be linger near we--resume his assistance, and let we

rejoin the company. we be detain they on the shore." Lady Wetheral

rise, as she speak, with great composure, but a smile of pleasure

lurk beneath her grave and calm expression of countenance. how

greatly be she mistaken in the cause of her daughter's emotion!



"you shall be my companion, Chrystal," say her father, draw his

daughter's arm within his, "and I will take charge of you. we will not

delay the party."



this be not quite in the fitness of thing. her ladyship be

discompose.



"but, my love, Lord Farnborough will have every reason to feel

offend: it wear a very extraordinary appearance that his lordship

should be so suddenly deserted. my dear Bell, you cannot altogether

desert your companion."



"a father's care will not give umbrage to any gentleman, Gertrude. I

will attend to my daughter, since she request it. no one will plead

desertion when I be in question."



this step discompose her ladyship's "arrangement," but impediment

only rouse her mind, and find employment for her energy. all the

resource of her genius be bring into full operation by this

unfortunate occurrence; and never, in Christobelle's early day, do

she remember her mother more present to herself--more fitted to contend

with the exigency of the moment. Lord Farnborough join they as they

proceed towards the lake. Miss Ponsonby fly towards Christobelle, at

the same moment.



"my dear, I would not intrude while you be under such proper

protection, but I hope you be recover. what be it?--a little

megrim?"



"we have forget its proper designation, and even its existence,"

reply Lady Wetheral, smilingly; "I be only anxious my daughter should

not undertake too much fatigue. I fear her effort in try to promote

amusement for our guest, Sir John Spottiswoode, have overcome she

strength."



"Miss Wetheral shall not suffer from effort of any kind this morning.

my lord, take possession of your fair cargo, but reserve the seat of

honour for our young friend."



Christobelle clung to her father's arm, but Miss Ponsonby do not

observe the movement.



"Sir John Wetheral, you be my property; you must relinquish your fair

daughter."



"be we not admissable together, Miss Ponsonby?"



"I will have no rival--yes, I change my mind; I will have Miss Wetheral

for my Eucharis, and be myself Calypso, instead of Ellen Douglas. where

shall I find a Telemachus?"



"Sir John Spottiswoode," answer Lord Farnborough.



"no, I hate a flirt Telemachus--he be say sugar sentence to

Fanny."



"Mortimer Grey," rejoin his lordship.



"Nonsense, Telemachus with a hare-lip?--now, out upon you! Miss

Wetheral, you be mine, and you be Eucharis. I steal you from my lord."



"I cannot resign my fair assignment--rack and torture shall not

extort my consent," reply Lord Farnborough.



Captain Ponsonby come up.



"what be we wait for? your boat be fill, Mary--we must not

delay. Miss Wetheral, be you of our party? allow I to lead you to the

boat."



"Miss Wetheral be mine," cry Lord Farnborough, "and I give she to no

mortal."



"it be a freight worth contend for, Farnborough: state your claim."



"the lady's own fair word, Ponsonby."



"I will hear it from her own lip. Miss Wetheral, Genius of the Lake,

as they truly style you...."



"I dispute the title," exclaim Miss Ponsonby. "I have also adopt

the costume, and I choose to share the distinction."



"Unfortunate Mary!--name fatal to peace upon Lochleven, be still. do

Miss Wetheral consign herself wholly and solely to Lord Farnborough?"



"I wish to go with my father," eagerly reply Christobelle.



Lord Farnborough bow proudly and coldly. Captain Ponsonby wave his

hat in the air.



"Hurrah for Miss Wetheral and independence! for once, my lord, you be

refuse--check in your high careering. Miss Wetheral, will you give

your fair hand to a portionless son?"



Captain Ponsonby hold out his arm to escort she to his vessel, but

Christobelle's hand be take gently yet firmly by her mother.



"my dear daughter thank you, gentleman, for your polite and

amusingly-agreeable knight-errantry. Captain Ponsonby, however, be only

unsuccessful from be too late. I believe honour be a treasure too

delicate to endure a breath of reproach, and we be pledge to my Lord

Farnborough."



"then, 'soft idea fly,'" say Captain Ponsonby, lay his hand upon

his heart.



"'see our oar with feather spray,'" exclaim Miss Ponsonby. "we

must stay here no long. I must not be Calypso--fair Eucharis be take

from I. I believe I have well remain only Mary Ponsonby."



"your sound judgment soon crush imagination," cry her brother.

"as Mary Ponsonby, you be a good-temper, noisy kind of girl--but

Calypso, or Ellen Douglas, would prove a failure."



"no lack of mentor, however," observe Miss Ponsonby, as she nod

her adieus, and take possession of Sir John Wetheral's arm. Captain

Ponsonby call after she.



"Mary, I be go to take charge of Lady Wetheral. tell Mortimer Grey,

to take my place."



"but your party will lose such a dominant spirit, my dear Captain

Ponsonby," say her ladyship, as Miss Ponsonby wave her hand, in token

of assent.



"disappointment be the lot of mortality," reply Captain Ponsonby,

gaily--"I cannot divide myself into two, and my heart be with _you_."



the party be soon launch upon the lake. Captain Ponsonby insist

upon take his station between Lady Wetheral and her daughter, and

his gay spirit almost while Christobelle into cheerfulness. she see

Sir John Spottiswoode enter the first boat with Miss Fanny Ponsonby,

but he never turn to cast a glance towards Christobelle--never once

come forward to say he hope she be well and happy. her heart swell

with sorrow so poignant, that she heed not Lord Farnborough's anxious

arrangement to make her comfortable--his effort to secure she from

the breeze which rise upon the water. she heed nothing--care for

nothing. Miss Fanny Ponsonby might consider the excursion a party

of deep delight, and Lochleven might be to she a remembrance of

pleasurable thing--but Christobelle feel the whole affair a mockery.

her mother endeavour to arouse her faculty.



"my love, Lord Farnborough have speak twice--his lordship hope you

feel no inconvenience from the sun?"



"thank you, I be very comfortable."



"my dear Bell, you be not aware Lord Farnborough have place his cloak

under your foot."



"thank you, my lord."



"for Heaven's sake," whisper her ladyship, "throw off the girl, and

be a woman of dignified, compose manner."



"I wish I be any thing but what I be, mamma."



"nonsense; not one of your sister act so girlishly. I beg you will

consider my shocked feeling."



Christobelle do make an effort to shake off the bond which seem to

bind her spirit with link of iron. she turn from the contemplation

of Sir John Spottiswoode and Fanny Ponsonby, but they rise before

she like the undying Hydra. she see they, in imagination, engage in

agreeable conversation--the beautiful eye of Fanny Ponsonby fix

upon her companion's face, and her mind inform by his remark.

Christobelle see he, in fancy, fascinate by her loveliness--eager to

please--absorb--forgetful of their own pleasant walk together--their

reading--their long and happy pause on the terrace, watch the last

beam of the summer sun. she start with terror.



"my dear Bell, you be not alarm?" exclaim her mother. "Lord

Farnborough be kind enough to take the helm."



Captain Ponsonby smile. "what! the Genius of the Lake alarm upon her

own element? forbid it, storm and cloud!"



"Miss Wetheral, you would feel more undisturbed if you be at my

_leave_ hand," whisper Lord Farnborough.



"indeed, Miss Wetheral would deceive herself, if she look for rest

near _you_, farnborough: I will not part with my supporter. Miss

Wetheral, do not be inveigle away from I. no whispering, unless it be

allow to all, if you please."



"you be point north this morning, Ponsonby; and now the wind sit

easterly."



    "East, west--alas! I care not whither,

    so thou art safe, and I with thee!"



exclaim Captain Ponsonby, turn towards Christobelle, with a smile.



Lord Farnborough become silent and sullen. a deep gloom spread over

his handsome face, and its bland expression fade. Lord Farnborough

wear a countenance, which Christobelle could never have recognise as

the agreeable set of feature which first please she at Lochleven.

his lordship turn with indignant pride from his friend, and give his

attention to the Miss Quintins.



"Bell!" whisper Lady Wetheral, as Captain Ponsonby again stoop

forward, to adjust his cloak, "you will lose he."



"lose he!" think Christobelle--"yes, I have lose he--for be he

not utter 'sugar sentence' to Fanny Ponsonby?--and be he not

regardless of his old acquaintance? how easy it be to sit in happy,

careless tranquillity, when no cloud veil our hope! how happy be

I, till the Clanmoray party break through the seclusion of Fairlee,

and bring a Fanny Ponsonby between I and my peace! how happy be

I in my freedom, rove amid the grove of Fairlee, before Sir John

Spottiswoode arrive, to teach I the glow of friendship, and then to

withdraw its light!" Ill, unhappy, and indifferent to the scenery,

which be her former object of devotion, Christobelle heed not the

sullen silence of Lord Farnborough, or the fear of her mother. the

little attention she could spare from the conjuration of her wretched

fancy, Christobelle give to the gay and kind-hearted Ponsonby.









CHAPTER XXV.





"Miss Wetheral," say Captain Ponsonby, "when I quit Clanmoray, six

year ago, I never dream of a fair neighbour at Fairlee."



"six year ago I be nurse a doll, Captain Ponsonby."



"even so. you tease your doll in youth, and tease our heart in age.

like a fall star, you have shoot from your sphere, upon the bank of

this lake, and where shall you make your rest?"



"you have be some time in Ireland, Captain Ponsonby, and have catch

the true hyperbole."



"I have no practice there, Miss Wetheral. if I tell a lady she be

charming, it be 'ah now, you're joking'--and if I advance with

classical allusion, or sparkle metaphor, it be 'ah now, Captain

Ponsonby, you're so droll!'"



"the irish lady possibly guess your character, Captain Ponsonby, as

it appear to have be a general answer. they know you be either in

jest, or sarcastic."



"upon my honour, you be wrong. I be sincere in word and deed. I be

neither fair nor false, the motto of some of my neighbour. don't look

this way, Farnborough."



"Miss Wetheral," say his lordship, "do not believe half you hear from

Ponsonby's lip."



"it hit my lord hard, you perceive, Miss Wetheral."



"my friend Ponsonby be a rover--san eye, san heart, san every

thing, Miss Wetheral."



"Excellent--ha, ha!" laugh Captain Ponsonby. "I can disprove the

charge, Miss Wetheral. I be in love three whole day, once, at

Castlebar."



"and why so speedy a cure?" Christobelle demand.



"the lady keep silence three day," he reply, "but, on the fourth

morning, the charm dissolve, for she speak."



"what could she have speak, to break a spell so powerful, Captain

Ponsonby?"



"I meet the lady in a pour rain, and, though I have not be

introduce to she, we have meet often, and be acquaint by name and

sight. I offer she my escort and my umbrella. 'ah, now, Captain

Ponsonby, there's rason in what you say, and I'll be obleege to

you'--was her good-humour reply. I could bear the brogue tolerably,

Miss Wetheral, for six month' residence have enure I to its twang;

but I could not away with the perfect nonchalance with which she

exhibit a pair of enormous ancle, and appeal to I upon their use.

'ah now, Captain Ponsonby, if I've get no understanding above, there's

plenty below, and I'll be charge for two pair of leg through the

penny turnpike.' farewell the glow complexion and bright eye of my

love!--I never more gaze upon Miss M'Nab."



"be that your _only_ enlargement of heart, Captain Ponsonby?"



"some few relapse there might have be, but none of any

consideration. Miss M'Nab be the most serious love."



"you be difficult to please."



"no, I think not; but I desire to find a sufficiently lovely woman,

with sweetness of temper, and delicacy of manner, to love with

constancy. if I ever love sincerely, it will be my life-string--the

very breath of my life."



"then be very cautious, Captain Ponsonby," say Christobelle, with

a feeling of painful interest. she feel how sorrowful be the

disappointment of friendship. what would the pang of unrequited

affection be?



"will you be my guardian angel, and watch over I, Miss Wetheral?"



"I cannot undertake such a momentous charge," she reply. Lord

Farnborough watch the conversation in gloomy silence, and converse

no more with Miss Quintin. Lady Wetheral be gratify by the

expression of jealousy which darken his lordship's fine face, for,

during the little bustle of debarkation, she smile, and hastily

whispered--"Christobelle, very well manage, my love; a little jealousy

be useful, but beware of give _offence_."



"Mamma, you be quite mistaken, indeed you be."



"Nonsense, Bell; I be keenly watch and decide." Lord Farnborough

offer his hand to assist her transit at that moment, and the subject

be of course drop. Captain Ponsonby offer his hand to Miss

Wetheral, and they follow in succession: he place her arm within his

own, as they touch the shore of the little island. "mind, you belong

to I, Miss Wetheral; I shall not relinquish you now."



Lord Farnborough consign Lady Wetheral to his father's care, and

immediately return. his lordship appear offend at the disposition

of thing.



"Miss Wetheral, I be depute by Lady Wetheral to bring you to she:

allow me--" Lord Farnborough put forth his arm. Captain Ponsonby

interfere.



"no one take my guardian angel from I. I will take charge of Miss

Wetheral with equal care. Miss Wetheral be mine."



"my claim begin early, Ponsonby," remark his lordship, with a look

of fierceness.



"I will fight for every inch of mine. my good fellow, the Quintins be

unattended."



"my delegate place be by the side of Miss Wetheral." Lord Farnborough

throw a look of defiance at her companion, which terrify

Christobelle. "oh, pray take I to my father, Captain Ponsonby," she

cry; "pray let I walk with my father."



"you shall be obey." Captain Ponsonby draw she among the group, who

be decide the plan of refreshment, or arrange their dress, and

give she into her father's care; but Christobelle still dread the

look of Lord Farnborough. she do not withdraw her arm from Captain

Ponsonby's support: he smile.



"you _be_ my guardian angel, after all. I see your fear, and, while

they operate to my advantage, I hope they will continue. how delightful

it be to be the object of a woman's tender care! every thing be so

kindly and silently do."



"I do not like Lord Farnborough's look, Captain Ponsonby."



"nor I, at all. I be very much alarmed, and I beg you will keep near

I." Christobelle laugh.



"what be you laugh at?"



Lady Wetheral approach, lean on the arm of his grace; and Lord

Farnborough also come up. Captain Ponsonby affect to tremble, and

assure Christobelle, if she quit his protection, he should be a

lifeless corpse. he could not bear the lightning of Lord Farnborough's

eye, or the thunder of his angry voice, at be deprive of his prey.

he think they have well contemplate the ruin of the little chapel,

while the party be quarrel about the dinner-table. Sir John

Wetheral be willing to move, and Christobelle also be anxious to

leave the spot where Sir John Spottiswoode stand pertinaciously by the

side of Fanny Ponsonby. Sickness of heart come over she, and she turn

from the scene.



lochleven crowd all its beauty into the panorama view from St.

Mungo's Isle, but Christobelle gaze upon they with vacancy: her eye

could not distinguish, and her mind would not relish they. she sit upon

a low, ruin wall, in utter listlessness; and, in silence, listen

to Captain Ponsonby's statement of the scene which have take place on

the spot where they rest, when it have be the sepulchre of the clan

of Glencoe and Lochaber. Christobelle's adoration of ancient legend

be sink in apathy. she dared not turn her head, lest the fearful form

of Fanny Ponsonby and her companion should startle her sight. she

gaze on the height, without perceive their beautiful outline. she

listen to Captain Ponsonby, without the power of retain his word.

a summon to the rural dinner alone rouse her spirit and energy.



"Bell, my love, I have preserve a seat for you, near _I_," say Lady

Wetheral, hold out her hand--"Come to I, my love; you have play

truant."



Captain Ponsonby seat Christobelle, and prepare to take possession

of an empty chair on her left hand, but Lady Wetheral smilingly

interfered--



"my dear Captain Ponsonby, I believe your seat belong to another, but

probably we can make room for you. ah! the bench interfere so; but

where will you find a seat? spottiswoode, be there not a vacant seat

near you?"



"do not disturb yourself, Lady Wetheral. I be perfectly satisfied with

my present quarter; and when a turn out be beat, I shall take a

sentinel's place."



Captain Ponsonby accordingly seat himself, and devote his time and

attention to the want of Christobelle, till Lord Farnborough join

they, with a cold fowl upon his silver fork.



"I have be my round for a supply, and can only gain one recruit,

Lady Wetheral, a fowl screen from observation by a bed of parsley.

Ponsonby, you'll excuse my resumption of a seat which be mine by right

of conquest."



"I only hold it in fear and trembling, Farnborough. I relinquish my

and admire my resolution." Captain Ponsonby rise, and station

"my dear fellow, there be two seat at the bottom of the table for

"I be very happy in my present situation," reply Captain Ponsonby; "I

be attend upon Miss Wetheral."



"but the Greys be quite by themselves, Ponsonby; do go down, and offer

your service there."



"Miss Wetheral, you be wish for a slice of cold turkey; I see you

contemplate it," observe Captain Ponsonby, who take no notice of his

friend's speech. "I fly for it."



"I wish Ponsonby would attend to the Greys," say his lordship, as

Fanny Ponsonby start at her brother's address, but she smile

"altogether, love deform and beautifie; it make the humble and

deity, the happy we be in freedom of heart and spirit; but once

"I think, Arthur, 'love be heaven, and heaven be love;' at least,

"Mary!" exclaim her brother, "presume not to touch upon ground where

Fanny Ponsonby rise hastily from her verdant seat, and Sir John

"who would have suppose so many grave, heraldic device, and rude

"Miss Wetheral, I bespeak your attention to those masse of cloud

"well, can you distinguish a chariot and pair, Miss Wetheral? I see

"it pain my sight, Captain Ponsonby, to fix my eye upon the heaven."



"I will shade the light with my hat," say Captain Ponsonby; "there,

now your eye be safe: the sun be behind my hat."



Christobelle be oblige to give her attention to the indefatigable

the vivacious Captain Ponsonby call the party round he, and they

Desolate, indeed, be the ground upon which they sit; and silent,

it seem to describe in one short sentence the tale of life; and it

    "there's something in that bonny face,

      I never see before, lassie;

    your action a' have sic a grace,

      I gaze and I adore, lassie."



Captain Ponsonby turn towards Christobelle, as he conclude the last

line of the first stanza, and he press his hand gallantly upon his

heart, as he give the last verse:



    "Sweet be the spring, and sweet the rose,

      when moisten by the shower, lassie;

    bright on the thorn the dew-drop glow,

      at morn refulgent hour, lassie:

    but bright, purer far than these

      Thou art, and charm'st I more, lassie,

    than tongue can tell; I wonder gaze,

      I gaze and I adore, lassie."



Christobelle blush deeply at the general notice which Captain

"my dear Miss Ponsonby, be not those cloud threaten? I have

the attention of the party be turn anxiously to the west, and

Lady Anna Herbert pass, lean on Mr. Grey's arm. "be quick, fair

"but I have _no_ comfort in deprive you of warmth and shelter: you

"never mind, Miss Wetheral; my mother and Sophia will nurse I well at

"at Alverton!" exclaim Christobelle, in astonishment, "at Alverton!"

"oh, no, if you be ill, I will nurse you; and Fairlee shall be

"because," he say, "lochleven be now a fever spot upon my heart."



Christobelle weep silently. Captain Ponsonby sit in the stern of

"Miss Wetheral, I claim you _this_ time," say his lordship, hastily

"I cannot understand why you persevere so pertinaciously in

Lord Farnborough throw a haughty look at Captain Ponsonby, but he make

"I be sorry, Captain Ponsonby, if any thing unpleasant should arise

"Farnborough have be use to such easy conquest, that he resent

"I differ with you, Miss Wetheral. Fanny and your friend have

Christobelle start at Captain Ponsonby's suggestion. impossible! she

how come Captain Ponsonby to imagine such folly! the very supposition

it be a relief to find Lord Farnborough absent, and still more a

and restless, and eager to return home.



the carriage be order as soon as Lady Wetheral arrive, and

the Fairlee party be supply by Miss Ponsonby with comfortable

it, all this cannot last, fair Christobelle, and, like I, you will

some day search in vain for a Télémaque."



"_i_ shall not live to see the day, Miss Wetheral," say her brother,

as he lead Christobelle forward.



"don't attach the small credit to Arthur's compliment," cry Miss

Ponsonby, kiss her hand.



"Mary be very incorrect in her statement, Miss Wetheral," say Captain

Ponsonby, as they pass through the hall. "you will receive I with a

smile, if I call at Fairlee to-morrow?"



"with many smile, Captain Ponsonby."



"no, one little particular welcome smile be my hope--give your many

smile to Farnborough. fare you well!"



Christobelle enter the carriage, and Sir John Spottiswoode follow;

but he seat himself by Lady Wetheral's side. Captain Ponsonby wave

his hand, and stand in the rain, till the tree conceal he from

CHAPTER XXVI.





Lady Wetheral speak of the morning's entertainment with perfect

approbation, as they drive home. "every thing be so agreeably

arrange--every body be so inclined to be amuse, which constitute

the charm of a party _al fresco_. Lord Farnborough, perhaps, be less

disposed to consider himself at ease than the rest of the group; but

circumstance do go a little '_à tort et à traver_' with poor Lord

Farnborough. some people be in the wrong place, assuredly, which

might create a little uneasiness; but, consider the difficulty of

select and arrange a large morning party, it have be admirably

conduct. there be a little too much vivacity in Captain Ponsonby's

manner: he be rather too _empressé_--but Lord Farnborough display

the man of fashion in every movement." Christobelle do not argue

against her mother's opinion, and Sir John Spottiswoode sit,

determine to be silent.



"Lord Farnborough," continue Lady Wetheral, "tell I he intends

wander round Lochleven some week long."



"at Clanmoray?" ask Christobelle.

the cloud break away towards the evening, and the rain cease; the
