impetuosity."



Lady Wetheral accept Lord Farnborough's offer arm, and she be

hurry upon the terrace; but not a glance or movement on her part

betoken fatigue, or a remnant of her past languor. her step be firm,

and her eye beam with expect triumph.



"Chrystal, my own Chrystal," cry Sir John Spottiswoode, as the

recede figure be lose to sight, "if ever I love and admire you

more truly and fondly than I fancy I could do, it be at the moment

you renounce your plan to obey a parent, with a smile on your lip,

and regret in your dear heart."



"but my real misery be yet to come," exclaim Christobelle, though

she feel herself press to the warm heart of her lover, and there be

blessedness in the pressure.



"but why so?" he ask tenderly; "what have Chrystal to fear?"



"Lord Farnborough's visit be connect with _myself_; I know it as

surely as if I hear the word speak. I know I have so much to endure

from my mother!"



"but you be mine, Chrystal; and who can take you from I now? be you

not my own, my very own?"



"I know it: I feel secure of _you_; but my mother will say such harsh

thing!"



"fear not, my beloved. if we be true to each other, surely we can

endure a little trial of patience."



yes, he speak truly. Christobelle could endure a long, long trial

for _his_ love. she could suffer a protract misery, to deserve a

heart so excellent. she have, too, a dear hope to sustain she, for she

father approve her sentiment, and he would shelter she from the

harsh reproach of her mother's ambitious spirit: she could meet

her lover's entreaty to be patient with smile. she tell he she would

endure all thing with firmness--that she would not anticipate evil.

she call upon he to rejoice with she in her father's approval, and

she tell he of her interview and confession. how swiftly do their

short _tête-à-tête_ glide by! and how delightedly do Christobelle

listen to his fear respect Lord Farnborough!



"but why do you not tell _I_ all this? why do you suppose such

incredible thing in silence--to leave I in such cruel and useless

suspense? oh, Spottiswoode, _one_ word yesterday morning, and all this

have be spare we!"



"no, I be silence by your mother for ever, in our morning lounge

upon the terrace. have you not disclaim all idea of Lord Farnborough

this morning, and have not hope rush into my heart unbidden, by the

confession of your misery, I have never dare to breathe a word of my

sentiment. I believe you love Lord Farnborough."



"she could not tell you _that_! surely my mother do not tell you

so--oh! she never tell you I care for _he_!" Christobelle shudder

at the thought; but the encircle arm of her lover restrain the

movement which impel she to start from her chair.



"I be assure your heart be on the point of acknowledge Lord

Farnborough's power, Chrystal--and your mother speak in term of proud

approval."



"oh, my mother, my mother!" exclaim Christobelle, weep at the

thought of her cruel policy; "I might have be sacrifice for ever to

your ambitious wish! I should have be give in utter wretchedness

to a man I do not love, and consign to hopeless misery!"



"weep not, dearest," say her companion, "and I will tell you how your

presence shall brighten and bless the scene of my solitary wandering

at Alverton--how it shall illumine my future life, and reward I for my

patient waiting. do you but know, my love, how I fear you would be

appropriate, ere I could claim an interest in your heart, and, yet,

how firmly I resolve to leave you to the working of its own resolve,

you would pity and love I for my resolution."



"I _do_ love you!" Christobelle conceal her face upon his shoulder.

they be silent for some moment, but his lip be press upon

her forehead, and she be in his arm--both too engross, with

the certainty of be at last happy, to break the deep silence.

Christobelle forget her mother--she forget Lord Farnborough; she

think not of his errand, or her future anger. she be in a

trance:--she think all reproach--all suffering--all unkindness, have

no further power to wound, for she belong to her lover, and he would

shelter she, as he do at that moment, in his beloved embrace. the

world without might struggle with deep and mighty commotion--it might

drink deeply of the element of strife, and do battle with the stirring

nature of mortality--but _she_ be safe from strife and suffering now.

she have give herself, heart and soul, to the man she love dearer than

herself, or aught in creation besides. she be the promise bride of

Sir John Spottiswoode!









CHAPTER XXVII.





Lord Farnborough's mission be speedily develop to Lady Wetheral,

as they pace the terrace together. his lordship at once open the

subject, which lie so closely upon his mind's peace, and at once lay

his dukedom at Christobelle's foot.



"upon my honour, Lady Wetheral, my respect for your daughter be

excessive, and I wish to make known my sentiment at such an early

period, because I see I have rival. Ponsonby be over head and ear

in love, and there's no knowing how many more will become so; for

her beauty be really something extraordinary. Miss Wetheral be the

lovely creature I have ever see."



"my daughter be flatter by such remark, my dear lord, and her mother

feel proud of such an encomium. she be a treasure to I, in every

sense of the word, as she will prove to the man who win she."



"I can offer much more than Ponsonby," continue Lord Farnborough;

"and I think, at this very early stage of our acquaintance, an earl's

coronet in possession, and a duke's strawberry-leave in prospect, may

perhaps entitle I to her notice beyond the claim of humbler man."



"Lord Farnborough may claim a lady's preference upon still high

ground than mere rank or fortune," say her ladyship, with smile

approbation, and gently press his lordship's arm. Lord Farnborough

be gratify by the compliment.



"it be very soothing, Lady Wetheral, to my feeling, to be assure that

my suit be not displease to her parent. may I hope Miss Wetheral's

affection be disengage?"



"I can answer for my daughter's free heart, my lord. I be sure--indeed,

I know her affection be untouched."



"you think Ponsonby have not get before I into her good opinion?"



"I have authority to say, Captain Ponsonby have not _yet_ succeed, my

dear lord."



"Ponsonby have be very successful with the female heart, and I know

he be attentive yesterday--but he have nothing to offer. I think Sir

John Spottiswoode flutter round Fanny Ponsonby--nothing _there_, you

think, Lady Wetheral?"



"my daughter's affection be not so lightly win, my lord; and my

interest be not with Sir John Spottiswoode."



"it would be a great feather in my cap, to win Miss Wetheral from all

competitor. she would be a star in town, and cause a great sensation.

she shall be the well-dress woman in St. James's, if she appear

there as Lady Farnborough. she would have the most splendid jewel in

the drawing-room."



"my dear child's taste be simple and unexpensive, Lord Farnborough.

she do not court notoriety. her heart be happy in her own home."



"that be a lady's throne," observe his lordship, "and man be happy,

who marry a creature devote to his comfort."



"be the duke aware of your present application, my lord?"



"I believe he surmise what I be about, Lady Wetheral, for your

daughter's charm almost lead to a quarrel this morning with Ponsonby.

it decide I at once to announce my wish here, or he would be

lay siege to Miss Wetheral. I see that pretty clearly. however, if

you stand my friend, Lady Wetheral, I be safe."



"my lord, I _think_ my wish will point my daughter's affection;

I believe I possess her entire confidence, and the control of her

judgment; and the very proper way in which you announce your wish of

an alliance with our family, prompt I to exert my influence in your

favour. I admire your high-spirited address, my lord, in consulting

_I_ before you apply to the lady."



"I wish to do every thing in order," reply his lordship, "and I know

Ponsonby have serious intention, which give I some alarm. when may I

pay my respect to Miss Wetheral? will she allow I an interview soon,

Lady Wetheral? you may conceive my impatience to be receive as one of

your family."



"I will summon my daughter, my lord, and leave you together: I be sure

of my child's ingenuous heart; and she will scorn to allow any man to

remain in suspense, when his full intention and hope be disclose."



Lady Wetheral's appearance at the sitting-room window unfold she

thought and expectation to Christobelle's mind in one glance. the

subdue look of triumph, the force calmness of manner, contrast

with the glowing expression of every feature, leave her daughter not an

instant in ignorance of what have take place. she feel that her hour

of trial be already arrive--that she must collect her thought, and

meet, with patient firmness, all the crosse in her path--that she must

redeem her promise of patience to her lover. Christobelle have little

leisure for mental reflection, for Lady Wetheral enter the room, and

compel attention.



"my dear love, Lord Farnborough request the honour of your attention

for a few moment: I have promise that you will join his lordship

on the terrace. your instant acquiescence will oblige I, Bell." Sir

John Spottiswoode quit the room. she continued--"It be a relief to

lose sight of one's friend for a few minute; I wish Spottiswoode have

find amusement elsewhere. Hasten, my dear girl, and meet I after your

little consultation in my room. I willn't say a word till you rejoin I;

but, my dear child, this be the very happy hour in my existence--a

happy hour than when my Julia tell I she have win Ennismore. my wish,

Bell, have be gloriously fulfil; every thing have crown that

wish, without an effort. I be a proud and happy mother!"



"oh, mamma," cry Christobelle, kneel before she, "do not

misunderstand I, and do not hope against hope. I cannot marry Lord

Farnborough!"



"do not rouse I into anger, Bell, as you hope for peace in this world;

and do not let I find you a mean-minded creature, content to live in

insignificance. go instantly, and meet Lord Farnborough."



"I cannot go, mamma; I have no affection to bestow upon Lord

Farnborough--do not let I meet he! tell he, I deplore his

disappointment, if it prove such--but I cannot see he!"



Lady Wetheral's face turn pale as marble, as she catch

Christobelle's hand, and drag she forward.



"tell I only that you be think of Captain Ponsonby, to break my

heart at once, Bell!"



"oh, no, not Ponsonby--I care not for Captain Ponsonby, mamma: but do

not look so pale and angry--you terrify I!"



"so you have lead I into error--deceive my hope--and destroy I,

while you seek the love of Spottiswoode! be that truly so? be it

spottiswoode you love?"



Christobelle shrink from her grasp in terror. Lady Wetheral's face and

manner become fearfully change; she catch the back of a chair to

support herself.



"Bell, I have answer for your dutiful submission to my wish. I have

promise for you--I have tell Lord Farnborough you be free. go to he,

and say that I speak in truth. a dukedom, Bell!--a dukedom!--my last,

my only child, a dukedom be offer you!"



Christobelle sit in terrified silence: she could not endure to see

her mother suffer, but she have no consolation to offer. Lady Wetheral

approach she, and take her cold hand in her.



"Bell, a child never yet prosper that give pain to her parent, and

now you can raise I into happiness by your obedience. to see my

daughter a duchess--a duchess, move in stately magnificence, be the

dearest wish of my heart. it have be my hope, ever since your first

introduction to Lord Farnborough--my dear project by day and by

night, my earthly contemplation for many day! go to he, Bell, for I

have answer for you, and you _will_ go; think well of it, think of

your future regret, when repentance will come too late! go to Lord

Farnborough, I command you, Bell."



a mother's command have never be dispute by Christobelle, and it

be, perhaps, well to meet his lordship. by an open declaration of

engaged affection, which would end all further hope on his side,

Christobelle's disquietude would cease; and her mother would reconcile

herself to a step which must be unavoidable, by every honourable and

upright principle of justice. Christobelle have no doubt to solve, no

inquiry to make with her own heart. every feeling of her soul be

give to Sir John Spottiswoode, and Lord Farnborough have not deserve

to endure suspense. she obey her mother's command, therefore, to meet

his lordship upon the terrace. her step be slow, and her mind be

tear with contend feeling, but she go forward.



"Bell," say Lady Wetheral, as she pass through the open window, "do

not be rash."



Lord Farnborough approach with respectful pleasure. Christobelle

return his greeting with a silent bow, but she could not command

word, and she stand in silence before he. his lordship hesitate.



"Miss Wetheral be aware, I presume, of my errand here--of my anxious

wish--of my hope?"



"my mother have inform I, my lord, of your wish--of the honour do

I, but...."



"will you do I the honour to walk up the terrace, Miss Wetheral, while

I explain my feeling and motive." his lordship offer his arm;

Christobelle decline it silently. "I trust you will not misinterpret

my action, Miss Wetheral. I be not a confident man, or one who presume

upon a parent's interest in my behalf--it be do in all respect, Miss

Wetheral."



"I be sure it be so, my lord--my only motive for decline your

assistance, be the fear of give a hope, where none be intend."



his lordship appear startled and annoyed.



"when I have explain my wish, Miss Wetheral, to you, and when I

state my hope that you will allow I to visit you at present, simply as

a friend, till you can give I a dearer title, I trust you will listen

calmly to what I be far anxious to say."



"Lord Farnborough," reply Christobelle, with trepidation of voice and

manner, "I will not deceive you for one moment. pray do not think of

I, for it be useless. I--I--cannot love you, or even give you hope

that I ever shall love you. pray do not think of I."



Lord Farnborough bow with great stiffness. "I beg your pardon, Miss

Wetheral, for this annoyance, but, allow I to say, I be assure you

be disengage."



"my lord, I was----, I am----." the word die upon Christobelle's

tongue; she could not utter they.



"Ponsonby have make an impression upon your heart, Miss Wetheral! I

think--I be _sure_ of it yesterday! that fellow be bear to be my

misery."



Christobelle lay her hand upon his lordship's arm, and endeavour to

speak distinctly, but she could only articulate, "no, no, no!"



"do not fear I, Miss Wetheral," reply his lordship, with offensive

hauteur, "I be not intend to wreak vengeance upon a man you approve,

but this be the second time he have traverse I!"



"Captain Ponsonby be nothing to _I_, my lord; Captain Ponsonby can

never be any thing to I!" Christobelle exclaim, "but pray excuse I

if I drop the subject for ever. I be honour--I be flattered--but it

never can be, Lord Farnborough."



his lordship gaze eagerly in her face. "repeat those word again, Miss

Wetheral! assure I again that Ponsonby be, and will be, nothing to

you!"



"I do repeat it, my lord."



"then, Miss Wetheral, I be content. I will believe your assertion, and

it give I hope. do not be in haste to reply. allow I your attention

for a few moment."



"I cannot listen, my lord. I have speak the truth, and I beg to be

allow to say this subject must end for ever. I have no affection to

bestow upon yourself, or upon Captain Ponsonby."



"I beseech you to listen one moment--one moment, Miss Wetheral! I do

not ask for your affection yet. I could not presume to hope even for a

preference, upon our short acquaintance. I only pray for leave to visit

you--to try and interest your heart, by my attention and my love. Lady

Wetheral give I hope, Christobelle!"



"no one can give hope for another, my lord."



"Lady Wetheral assure I your heart be free."



Christobelle hesitate. why do she feel ashamed to utter the truth,

and end at once the displeasing subject? why hesitate? she suffer

many struggle between shame and timidity, but at last the victory be

gain, and she speak with resolution.



"my lord, I love another--I have give my affection to another

person--excuse I."



his lordship bow low, with peculiar frigidity of manner. "I wish you

good morning, Miss Wetheral. I regret my intrusion: I be answer."



Christobelle curtsey with equal hauteur.



"I be sorry my word have produce dissatisfaction, my lord. it be

but just that my sentiment should not be misunderstood, and I do not

reproach myself for having withhold an ungenerous and delusive hope.

good morning, my lord."



they part with a second silent and distant salutation, and Lord

Farnborough quit the terrace.



Christobelle stand some moment, vainly endeavour to gain fortitude

to meet her mother--but now the deed be do, and his lordship have

depart, the terror of Lady Wetheral's anger fall upon her heart, and

she fly to her father for protection. Sir John Wetheral be in his

study converse with Spottiswoode, when Christobelle appear, and

both gentleman rise, smile at her entrance; but she throw herself

into her father's arm with hurried step, and besought he to save

she from her mother's reproach. she feel it be impossible to meet

she alone, or bear the indignant flash of her eye. she implore she

father to be the bearer of her refusal, and to endeavour to soften her

mother's anger, when she learn that Lord Farnborough be return to

Clanmoray, a reject and offend suitor.



Sir John Wetheral soothe his daughter's fear with kind approval of

her conduct. he speak affectionately of her attachment to his friend,

and commend the propriety of her sincere avowal to Lord Farnborough.

he would leave she now under the soothe care of Spottiswoode, who

be destine, he hope, to be her future guide through life. he would

give his Christobelle to his care, to listen to his reasoning and his

affection, while he himself seek the presence of his lady. he bid

his daughter fear nothing. he would shield she from all the storm of

life, till he relinquish she into a husband's care. and, while she

continue to act with honourable and high principle, untouched by

sordid temptation, and the misery of an insatiable ambition, she

must be free from self-reproach, and would be patient under trial

which could not greatly affect her peace of mind. Sir John Wetheral

then place his daughter's hand within the warm grasp of Spottiswoode,

and leave they together.



lover' happiness be compose of a million nothing, and every

indescribable rapture, which in after day provoke the laughter and

ridicule of its votary. all those who have love understand it

well--and to those who have never know a sincere attachment, it be

a seal book. Christobelle utterly forget all mundane concern, as

she listen to the fond effusion of her lover's heart, and own an

affection deep and imperious as his own. Christobelle almost forget

there be a drop of bitter yet leave, in her cup of joy.



Sir John Wetheral pass on to his lady's apartment, little aware of

the scene which await his peaceful nature, produce by defeat

ambition, in an ardent spirit. Lady Wetheral be suffer severe

nervous excitation as he enter her sitting-room, for Christobelle's

protract absence bode evil to her hope. she look earnestly in

her husband's face, to read its import.



"you have see Bell, you have see Bell--tell I at once, if I be to be

the mother of Lady Farnborough."



"Gertrude," reply her husband, calmly, "do not destroy your health by

these nervous excitement."



"Sir John, my nerve be excite by the conduct of my child. be I

the mother of the future Duchess of Forfar? be I to be the proud mother

of a child raise to the very pinnacle of worldly grandeur?"



"you will be the mother of a child truly happy in her worldly

prospect, if your mind will but look rationally upon its promise, my

dear Gertrude."



"be I to be the mother of Lady Farnborough?" repeat the excited

parent.



"our daughter have not commit the base folly of accept one man,

when her heart belong to another, Gertrude."



"do _i_ not do so before she, Sir John? I never repent _my_

marriage!"



"it might be so, my love, but Chrystal never possess your ambition,

to soar over affection and honour, through its cold dictate."



"be I to understand Bell have refuse Lord Farnborough, Sir John? be

that the reading of your word? pray speak it in intelligible word."



"Christobelle have decline his lordship, Gertrude. her heart prefer

Spottiswoode, and my concurrence go with it."



"perhaps you will convey a message from I to the future Lady

Spottiswoode of Alverton, among the flat meadow of Worcestershire, Sir

John," reply his lady, in the calm tone of suppress anger. "tell

my Lady Spottiswoode it be my request she never presume to appear

before I during the day of her singlehood."



"Gertrude, Gertrude," exclaim her husband, "be this request a fit

message from a mother to her child?"



"I have always a dislike to scene," observe her ladyship, "therefore,

I shall not reproach Miss Wetheral with her deceptive conduct in

allow attention from a quarter which I never countenance, and

after my express command to avoid they. I ever deprecate scene

before Lady Kerrison--but her violent spirit scorn restraint. she

give I deep pain; and Lady Ennismore's banishment have cause I

pain--but this stroke lie far deeply in my heart!"



Lady Wetheral become all nerve: her whole person be in a nervous

trembling. her husband fail in every effort to tranquillize her

spirit.



"you may dilate upon the supreme excellence of your favourite

Spottiswoode, my love, and you may assure I your daughter be an

enviable and a happy being; but I know she have cast aside a ducal

coronet, to wed the poor baronet of Alverton! I will see she no more. I

will never see she again."



"my dear Gertrude, be tranquil, and be rational." Sir John Wetheral

never could be persuade to lose his temper.



"I be perfectly tranquil and calm, Sir John. I be perfectly tranquil,

and very rational in my command, when I persist in banish my

unnatural child from my sight. Miss Wetheral have take her measure,

and I assert mine. we do not meet again."



"this be wrong, a wicked wrong, towards an innocent child, Gertrude!"



"she be very innocent," retort Lady Wetheral, with bitterness. "she

her sister never presume to do. they never contravene my view, nor

thwart my wish in their establishment, and they marry well!"



"be it _well_ with Clara?" demand her husband, with earnestness

of manner as startling as it be novel--"was it _well_ with Clara?

do she not quit her home clandestinely, to become the wife of

Kerrison?--miserable in her short career, and sudden in her death, be

she happy? be Clara to be hold forth as an evidence of maternal care,

in such a momentous concern?"



"Forbear, Sir John, forbear!" cry his lady.



"Nay, but Gertrude, have it be well with Julia, whom you teach to fly

at quarry so distasteful? wed to imbecility--banish from her home

and country, unseen and unheard of--be she, too, an evidence of your

talent in contrive establishment?"



"I tell you," exclaim her ladyship, "Julia be an earl's wife; and

Clara's position be high and grandly place, but her own hand pluck

she down. who can reproach _I_?"



"I will not reproach you, Gertrude, but I counsel you to spare this

last poor child. remember only your fatal mistake, and do not add a

third victim. I will not allow Christobelle to be sacrifice to your

ambition."

come near I. I be _not_ hard-hearted! Clara call I hard-hearted:

I be not hard-hearted! I be a disappoint, deceive mother. where be

Bevan?"



Mrs. Bevan appear with the remedy, which time have teach she to

dispense as judiciously as her predecessor, Mrs. Daniel Higgins, have

do; but Lady Wetheral's attack threaten a long continuance than

usual. she would fain have retire to her own room, but Sir John

tremble excessively. he bear she in his arm to her bed-side, and

more nervous.



"Bevan, let I have light--let I have light! if I cannot see the sun,

it will be darkness of body and mind. don't leave I, Bevan. Sir John,

where be you?"



Sir John stand near, in a state of offend alarm: his mind be

discompose--it never become angry.



"Sir John, I cannot remain at Fairlee: take I back to Wetheral. Bell

have destroy my health. I be quite well till this wretched match,

which have destroy all my plan, and throw down all my hope! it have

make I ill--bad than ever!"



it be not in the power of reason, much less in Sir John Wetheral's

power, to check the indignant feeling which affect his lady's

mind upon the subject of Lord Farnborough's refusal. each attempt to

argue away their violence do but increase the evil. nothing could

induce her ladyship to receive Christobelle at her bed-side, or hear

a word plead in her defence. Christobelle's attachment to Sir John

Spottiswoode, and her subsequent refusal to accept Lord Farnborough's

be closely weave together, for his ladyship declare her daughter's

presence would kill she upon the spot. the sad event which occasion

dismissal. Scotland would be to her sickened heart a remembrance of

misery. either Christobelle or herself must quit the shore of the now

desolate and cold Lochleven, upon whose bosom she have once enjoy

such bright anticipation. all be end, and joy have close her

brilliant wing for ever. this be an irrecoverable stroke.



Sir John Wetheral do not conceal from Christobelle the mandate which

banish she from her mother's presence; and the consolation of

Spottiswoode's presence be indeed necessary to soothe her distress of

heart. Christobelle would cheerfully have contribute to her parent's

comfort, have her wish extend to less than the sacrifice of all

happiness; but surely it be not reprehensible to withhold a shadow

of hope, when her heart be not with Lord Farnborough! surely it be

not right to turn from the man she love, when her affection be

his beyond the power of recal, and when the voice of ambition alone

demand it! true, Julia and Clara's view be mould by her mother's

spirit, but then their heart be untouched, and their unshackle

affection might submit to her dictation. ambition also impel they

to meet her wish, and no private feeling struggle within their soul

to deaden its influence; but Christobelle be another's!--she might

suffer, but she could not change!



Sir John Spottiswoode believe that time would soften Lady Wetheral's

displeasure, but Christobelle know too well the bitterness of the

disappointment, to lay such unction to her soul. have she ever be a

favourite with her ladyship--have her youth be pleasant in her sight,

she might have hope to obtain an influence through the operation of

time, sufficient to effect a reconciliation in favour of her present

attachment. but that have never be the case. her birth be consider

out of time--her sex displease she--her education be unintereste

to her mother's mind. it be only at interval, and under particular

circumstance, that Christobelle receive any commendation, and it

expire with the cause which elicit its birth. Christobelle felt

assure her mother would never forgive the wound inflict upon her

ambition. she feel assure her mother would never forgive the dismissal

of Lord Farnborough.



Sir John Wetheral decide upon quit Fairlee as soon as his lady

feel equal to undertake the fatigue of a journey; and he also express

a wish that a twelvemonth should elapse ere Christobelle become the

wife of Spottiswoode. "his daughter," he say, "be young, and a

twelvemonth might effect a change in her mother's feeling. it be

Christobelle's duty to make some concession to an offended parent,

and twelvemonth would operate as a fair trial of the constancy of her

own nature."



Christobelle submit most willingly to this arrangement. the

least wish of her father have ever be her rule of conduct, and his

indulgence would have win obedience, have his wish extend the period

of the engagement. but Sir John require no painful sacrifice, no

useless trial. spottiswoode might visit Christobelle whenever his

avocation enable he to become a guest at Lidham, and he trust time

would soften Lady Wetheral's disappointed view. he dare not pronounce

upon its certainty, but they have a right to hope the good.



the continued mortification which embitter Christobelle's repose, by

Lady Wetheral's harsh mandate, at last induce Sir John to resolve upon

his daughter quit Fairlee. it be a painful and perpetual grievance

to Christobelle, to know that her mother be ill, and confine to

her apartment, yet that _she_ be not suffer to alleviate her

confinement, or attend she. it be a grief which the affectionate

attention of her lover could not control, and which her father's

soothe presence do not lessen. her appetite decline and her spirit

flee. Spottiswoode also become dispirit and uneasy in witness she

regret, and Sir John Wetheral, alarm at his daughter's increase

depression, write to Mr. Boscawen to meet they at Edinburgh, and

conduct Christobelle to his own home for a season.



it be judge, that absence from a scene so painful, and the society of

Isabel, would cheer her spirit, and soften her present sorrow; while,

under the protection of Mr. Boscawen, she might receive the visit

of Sir John Spottiswoode, and move among the friend she love, and

whom she have not see for some year. Spottiswoode would remain some

time long at Fairlee, and it might be, that his lengthened sojourn

in the north would produce a favourable effect upon the invalid's

mind. it might be, that the knowledge of her daughter's withdrawal

would rouse she, and if any human being could amuse and soften a harsh

determination in Lady Wetheral's soul, that be be the good and kind

Spottiswoode.



Christobelle wander each day with her lover through the scene of

their early walk, and if the exercise fail to bring the bloom into

her cheek, yet she be happy while lean on his arm, and listen

to his hope. they speak of Alverton in the rocky bower which she be

so soon to quit. Christobelle look upon the water, and she think

of the year of careless freedom in which she have wander among those

beautiful scene with Janet for her sole companion. she think of the

Douglas Isle, where she have first see Lord Farnborough, and the spot

from whence she have wave her plaid at their parting. Christobelle

think of she gradually increase passion for Spottiswoode--the hope

and fear of St. Mungo's Isle--the pang of jealous feeling which

she endure when Fanny Ponsonby engross the attention of he she

loved:--she think, too, of her present situation, the betrothed of

Spottiswoode, yet the banish one from her mother's side. would that

mother, indeed, continue to drive her child from her presence, or might

she yet receive her smile and hear she welcome? should she be indeed

the happy wife of Spottiswoode, and become, as he fondly style she,

the bright star of Alverton? Christobelle weep. Spottiswoode press

she to his heart.



"my own Chrystal, this silent grief destroy I, for it be through I

you suffer. would to Heaven you be safe at Brierly with your friend,

and that I be with you! I shall soon follow you, for here I cannot

remain without you. I should hear you sigh, and see your weeping figure

in every spot where we have be together. I shall look like the ghost

of depart pleasure. you will leave I on Friday, my Chrystal."



"for a few day only, Spottiswoode."



"I shall know you be in kind protection, dearest. you will be with the

good of man in Boscawen's company, and you love he as well as Isabel.

her child will amuse you. you will see the pynsent. you will hardly

have time to think of _I_, Chrystal."



"I will try to forget you sometimes; I wonder if I shall succeed."



"you have nephew and niece to engross your attention; you will be

joke by Mrs. Pynsent, Chrystal, for fancy your poor lover. every

body will crowd round you, to admire your loveliness, and wonder at

your graceful figure; and yet I must not be there to witness it. what

shall _I_ do without you?"



the tear spring to Chrystal's eye; she endeavour to disperse they,

but they fall unbidden. her mind be weaken by recent event. she

clasp her hand, and exclaimed,--



"oh, do not say you will suffer, Spottiswoode, by my absence."



"no, no, my love," he reply, with fond endearment, "I will not think

of our little trial. you will go from I on Friday; but be you not my

own betrothed bride, and be we not one in heart and hand? you will

rejoice your friend, and be rejoice in meet they. I shall also

rejoice, in the knowledge of your happiness. our thought will meet

often and often in our absence; and then, my Chrystal, we shall meet

again in peace. I cannot and will not remain a week absent from you."



Christobelle smile again to think their separation be not eternal.

how many attach heart have be tear asunder, while they be

mourn over the parting of a few short day! how many heart have bid

a long farewell, while they should meet again and be in peace! again

she lean against her lover's shoulder, and her thought be of

grateful thank. she be not call to severe affliction--she be not

belove by a man whose birth or character be repugnant to her friend,

and to whom her happiness be a source of discomfort and disgust.

even her mother have not deprecate Spottiswoode: his offence in her

eye arise from having win her daughter from Lord Farnborough. her

father love he, and approve her attachment. yes, Christobelle have

reason to be thankful to the hand which minister so wisely for her

happiness. her mother would not always be harsh; surely she might in

time be summon into her presence! she would never be forgive; she

never _have_ be love; and the slight cord of affection be break for

ever between they: but they might meet without pain, she hope, on her

mother's part; and she herself would exert uncease effort to win she

into complacency. her mother _must_ love Spottiswoode, when time should

soften her remembrance of Lord Farnborough's attention.



Christobelle speak of Fanny Ponsonby to her companion, now that her

heart be at rest; and allude to her emotion in St. Mungo's Isle.

Spottiswoode have, from the first hour of their introduction, discover

her predilection for Lord Farnborough.



"I could trace it, Chrystal, in the agitated and close attention

she bestow upon yourself, and in her anxiety to remove from your

vicinity. I see the distressed feeling of her heart, as she watch

the engross attention of Farnborough at dinner: and I feel for she

suffering during the singing. I feel for she and for myself at that

moment, Chrystal, for I also fancy you approve his lordship."



"how could you think so, Spottiswoode?"



"Lady Wetheral's word be in my ear, Chrystal, and I have

relinquish all hope for myself."



"what a day of misery it be to I!" Christobelle exclaim, as she

think of all she have endure. "Fanny Ponsonby's feeling be all

echo by my own."



"it be a painful day to many, Chrystal; but to we it have prove a

blessing, for it confess our sentiment at once, and prepare the

way for our mutual understanding. have you not express disapprobation

of Lord Farnborough, I have not dare to tell my feeling. be it not a

blessing, my love?"



"but poor Fanny Ponsonby, what will be _she_ destiny?" Christobelle

ask, as she return the pressure of Spottiswoode's hand. "what will

be her fate in her attachment?"



"Miss Ponsonby have sow her seed among thorn," he reply, with

feeling, "and her heart must wrestle with its feeling, unless she

become his wife. Lord Farnborough may turn to Miss Ponsonby, since you

have reject he, and she may become the envy Lady Farnborough; but

she will only sink into a neglect wife. he cannot make a woman happy."



"oh! if I have see he with my mother's eye, and fall a sacrifice!"

observe Christobelle, with a shudder.



"I should have quit England, Chrystal. I would not have witness

your misery, or remain within hearing of your gradual decay. you

could not long live under his harsh treatment, and I could not have

bear to hear the world remark upon your pale cheek and fade form."



"how grateful I be! how happy I ought to be, Spottiswoode! when you

come to see I at brierly, and I do not suffer daily under my mother's

angry prohibition, you will find I so changed and happy! when I think

of Lord Farnborough, I turn to you with such grateful delight! yes,

Spottiswoode, I _will_ be happy!"



and Christobelle exert herself to be happy, even the few day which

intervene between she express gratitude and her long farewell to

Fairlee. she sit hour after hour upon the terrace, with her father

and Spottiswoode, look with deep interest upon the lake, the scene

of all her pleasure, while she be happy in ignorance of a deep

affection, and the scene of suffer during the stir incident

which have awaken her heart to the strife of worldly sorrow. lochleven

be endear to Christobelle by a thousand recollection, by the

enjoyment of her young age, and by the happy hour pass by the side

of Spottiswoode. she be go to the south, to a new scene that would

be change in its aspect; gayer, full of friend, and still bl by

the society of he she love. but would it be to she like the grand

Lochleven?--that shelter from the world's care? that spot where she

have enjoy such long tranquillity? Adieu for a long, long time, the

cloud-cap mountain, the heather hill, the placid water! farewell

the island on its bosom--the grove, the far Cona! farewell the

stir breeze, the lonely Eilan na Corak--the repose, the grandeur of

the shore of Lochleven!



Christobelle endure much, ere she set forth with her father from

Fairlee. she bear her parting interview with Spottiswoode with great

intrepidity; but she weep at her mother's obdurate determination not to

see she before she quit the parental roof. Sir John Wetheral soothe

she as she gaze upon the lake, till it vanish from her ache sight:

and he prophesy that her trial would not be lengthen beyond her

power to endure it. he speak kindly and well to her overpowered spirit,

of the sorrow which must accompany a progress through life, and of

the consolation which attend a patient and pray spirit. he do

not consider it right to allow Spottiswoode to accompany they, as she

mother evince such decide repugnance to the match; but at brierly,

Christobelle might enjoy her lover's society in peace; and he would

accompany his lady to Wetheral as soon as she express a wish to set

forth on the long journey. new scene, new face, and a new set of

idea would, he hope, drive gloomy fancy from her mind, and bring

comfort to all party. she be on her road to Isabel, and with she and

Boscawen she must enjoy the calm pleasure of domestic happiness. they

would lead she to agreeable thought, and Spottiswoode would speedily

add his society to the enjoyment of Brierly.



such be Sir John Wetheral's reasoning, and its effect be apparent

upon Christobelle's spirit. she gradually recover cheerfulness

under his indulgent soothing, and she could even admire the change

scenery which vary their route to Edinburgh. Mr. Boscawen be true to

his appointment; they find he at the place of rendezvous, ready to

conduct Christobelle to the county of her birth, and induct she once

more into the domicile which have shelter she so kindly year before.



Mr. Boscawen be surprised at her growth, and wonder at the girl who

have glide into womanhood so rapidly. he think her feature bear a

strong resemblance to her unfortunate sister Clara; but he politely

assure she, her manner be entirely her own. she be no long, he

say, "the gay little half-grow girl, who have delight to nurse she

nephew under the mulberry tree at brierly. she must be look upon in a

very different phase; and he fear the quiet haunt of Brierly would

ring with the charm of the beautiful Miss Wetheral. he be pleased to

think she possess a protector whose claim would at once put an end

to the fearful contention of furious rival." Christobelle's blush

be the only answer she could give to the agreeable remark of Mr.

Boscawen.



Sir John Wetheral's absence would not be of long continuance, therefore

Christobelle do not sorrow much as she bid he adieu. she pray he

to speak of she to her mother, and to express her deep regret at

having incur her displeasure. she pray for her pardon, and that

they might meet again in reconciliation. she look to her forgiveness

with anxious desire, with a fervent and unceasing hope. she embrace

her dear father, and bid he remember she kindly, affectionately, to

the friend she have leave at Fairlee, and then she give her hand to Mr.

Boscawen.









CHAPTER XXVIII.





how delightedly do the eye of Christobelle rest upon the matronly form

of Isabel! she be clasp in her arm, as she descend the step of

the chaise at Brierly.



"well, I never see such a beautiful creature in my life! my dear

Christobelle, who would have think you would turn out so very pretty?

my dear Mr. Boscawen, be you not surprised at Christobelle's beauty,

when you first see she?"



"I be not so overcome at your sister's handsome face, as I be

astonish at Isabel's neglect of her long-absent husband," reply Mr.

Boscawen, smile. Isabel fly towards he.



"I cann't think how I come to be so wicked; but, indeed, it be the

sight of my sister's face. my dear Boscawen, I have so long for your

return, and so have the child! I be oblige to tell the dear thing

papa be go a long way off, for some cake and their pretty aunt, or

they would not have be comfort. I do not know what hour you would

arrive, therefore I be wise enough not to say a word to they about it."



"they shall be summon after dinner, Isabel. we be hungry traveller

now, and a meal will be most gratefully welcome."



"my love, ring the bell, and order it in now, if you please, while I

escort my sister to her room. follow I, dear Christobelle. well, I

declare I never see such a change in a human being! you be tall and

handsome, and have such beautiful ringlet! I shall certainly have

ringlet too. I cann't fancy you be the little, jump Chrystal, who

be dress out so fine at my wedding, five or six year ago. well--and

don't you think _I_ be change?"



Isabel be change. her sylph-like figure have swell into a stout

form: her waist have increase materially in breadth, and her dress be

rather disorder.



"the child be so playful, Chrystal, that my collar have always

a ragged appearance; and corset of any kind be so very painful,

because I be get large, that I never wear they. Boscawen, though,

do not mind my appearance: he be not particular. he allow I to do

just what I like, and I be so happy! ah! do you remember my geography

day, Chrystal? how I detest that dreadful map of the world! I be

determine my little Bell shall be happy and ignorant, as I be before

she. she be call Bell after you, dear--not your long name, but simply

Bell. I hope she will be as pretty as you; but what do it signify?

who can be happy than I be, with my broad waist and rumpled collar?"



the moment the servant have withdraw, after dessert be place upon

the table, Isabel continue her happy prattle.



"I have so much to tell you, of one kind or the other, dear Bell, that

I don't know where to begin. what shall I tell first, my dear Boscawen?

oh! Bell, such thing be whisper of Julia!--I don't believe a word

of they--not one word. however, you will find Anna Maria more French

than ever, for the Count de Nolis have be stay every Christmas at

Hatton, and Félicé do lay on the rouge terribly. it's quite amusing

to hear Tom Pynsent boast of his wife's bloom, when it's rouge all the

time. I think I write you word, that odd Mrs. Hancock have a paralytic

stroke some time ago?"



"no, indeed, Isabel, I never hear of it."



"don't you? oh! it's the case, I assure you--her mouth be all on one

side. poor Miss Tabitha be not dead yet, bell: I don't think she mean

to die at all; and Mrs. Ward write word, she find fault with every

thing, poor soul, that be say or do. shall I ring for the darling,

my dear Boscawen? I be sure Chrystal be die to see her little boy,

whom she nurse so carefully. ring the bell twice, my love, and the

little wild thing will soon rush in."



"do not give Tom wine, Isabel; I have a dislike to child take wine

so early in life."



"no, my dear Boscawen, certainly not. I think, with you, that wine be

very improper for child--but little Tom have such a win way of

coax his poor mother!"



the child soon burst into the room, and run bound round the

table. Isabel be all triumphant pleasure. "now, Charles--my dear

Charles, go quietly to papa, and ask he how he do, and whether the

cake be come from merry Scotland. ah, ha, Charley go for his cake.

Tom, my beauty, come to mamma; and Bell, go to your aunt--your new

aunt, and admire her pretty hair."



Christobelle endeavour to attract her little fat niece to her side,

but she hide her face in Isabel's lap.



"oh, Bell, I'm ashamed of you!--your pretty aunt, too--oh, fie! my dear

Tommy, don't touch mamma's glass. no, no wine, Tommy--papa say no. a

few strawberry, my dear little boy, and a biscuit, but no wine."



Tommy, however, advance his mother's wineglass to his lip, watch

her countenance with a cunning glance.



"now, my little, good Tommy, mamma will be angry, very angry, if you do

what she tell you not to do. Bell, my love, go to your aunt, like a

good girl."



Isabel take some pain to persuade her little girl to raise her head,

but her appeal be useless. in the mean time, Tommy have silently

quaff the remainder of the content of her glass. Mr. Boscawen rise,

and take he from Isabel's knee.



"my dear Boscawen, what be you go to do with Tommy? he be very good

with I, my love."



"I be go to banish he, Isabel, for disobedience."



"oh, my dear Boscawen, it be the least little drop of wine in the

world! it be scarcely a teaspoonful--pray don't punish Tommy for that

little drop, my love."



"the disobedience be the same in the action, Isabel. I shall send he

into the nursery. I shall take he there myself."



Isabel's eye suffuse, as Mr. Boscawen leave the dining-room with the

cry recreant Tommy. she turn to her sister.



"oh! Chrystal, be it not severe to carry away that dear child, for one

drop of wine only?"



"Boscawen be quite right, my dear Isabel."



"do you really think so? do you really think he be right to banish the

darling child, when it be such a pretty, coax trick? do you see

his little, cunning, dear eye?"



"Boscawen justly think, Isabel, that cunning habit and disobedience

will increase, if it be not check."



"oh! I hope it willn't increase--dear child--I should be sorry to see he

grow sly." Mr. Boscawen return. "my love, do Tommy cry? be he very

much hurt--poor dear? do you leave he cry?"



"my dear Isabel, you have bring this upon he, by not check

disobedience in the first instance. you have allow the child twice to

steal your wine."



"my dear Boscawen--steal! indeed, that sound dreadful! I hope my

child will never steal! it be indeed my fault! poor, dear

child!--I dare say he be cry dreadfully now. let I beg for my

child, Boscawen."



"let he remain where he be, isabel: he have be very naughty."



"but I have teach he to steal, and I ought to suffer for he, dear

Boscawen. let I go to my child, my love!" the tear stand in Isabel's

eye, and Boscawen be still under their influence. he soothe his lady

as fondly as when in early day she weep for the droop ostrich

feather.



"my dear Isabel, when we retire into the drawing-room, your child shall

come down again. don't weep, my love. I cannot wonder at your fondness,

but he be very naughty. wait a few moment, Isabel, and the child

shall join we in the drawing-room. don't let I see you weep--stay, I

will bring he down to you."



Isabel smile, and little Tommy be restore to her arm, as he enter

the drawing-room. Mr. Boscawen feel he have act unwisely by his child;

but how could he resist Isabel in tear? thing be totally change at

brierly. a tear from the bright blue eye of Isabel melt Boscawen's

good resolve, and operate against the excellence of his own system

of education. Isabel hold the rein of government in her hand. she

never argue with her husband--she never offer a word of opposition

to his wish--but she rule by submission, and win his acquiescence

by her tear and gentle self-upbraiding. fortunately, her child

possess her own sweet disposition, which defy indulgence, or they

might have suffer through Isabel's inability to check their bud

fault. as it be, they love she too well to persist in offend a

parent so devoted; and it must have prove a very serious offence, ere

the light-hearted Isabel could lament conduct which be ever palliate

by her affectionate heart. she be indeed the happy of wife and

mother.



Mr. Boscawen and Christobelle resume their occupation instinctively,

as if year have not intervene since they last walk and read together

at Brierly. Isabel be delighted.



"ah, there you go again!--read, read, talk, talk, all day long. I

like to hear you argue, when I have time to devote to you both; but

the child require so much attention, and the dear little thing

love to be with I so often, that dear Boscawen have be a great deal

alone, haven't you, Boscawen? it be such a pleasure to have you here,

Chrystal--my poor, dear husband willn't have to endure my ignorance."



"who say my Isabel be ignorant?" say Mr. Boscawen, pat his lady's

shoulder affectionately.



"yes, dear Boscawen, I be very ignorant, and very unfit for you; but

I do very well to play with the darling, and superintend every thing

but their education--_that_ you will do, except for poor little Bell:

let she be happy and ignorant, Boscawen. if she be half as happy as her

mother, she will not require knowledge--only her husband and child.

I never wish for any thing beyond you and they." Isabel cast an upward

and affectionate look at Boscawen, who bent his long figure to kiss his

laugh wife.



Mr. Boscawen tell Christobelle he have engage to take she for a day

or two to Hatton. the pynsent be very anxious to see she, and

Mrs. Pynsent have make it a point with he to bring the "tall, gawky,

good-look girl" to she, as soon as she have rest a few day at

brierly. the Charles Spottiswoodes, also, be wish to see she

again, to contemplate the improvement which five year must have

effect in her appearance. the name of Spottiswoode bring blush

into the face of Christobelle.



"my dear Bell," exclaim Isabel, with laugh delight, "how droll

it be to think you have a lover; when I see you last, you be such

a bit of a girl! Sir John Spottiswoode be just the man I would have

choose for you--just the very person I should have single out--be he

not, Boscawen?--just the sort of man, with curl dark hair and high

forehead, that you ought to like, dear Chrystal!"



"_i_ have not dark curl hair, Isabel?" say Boscawen, smiling--"I have

not a high forehead, have I?"



"my dear Boscawen, your hair be always dreadfully wiry, and I think

you very plain, but I like you for all that, you know."



"then why _ought_ Chrystal to choose and love such thing, my Isabel?"



"ah! I dare say I be talk nonsense again," cry the humble Isabel,

"for I should really recommend no one who do not resemble you, dear

Boscawen. I should advise every woman to wait till they could find a

kind, dear man, like yourself, and then they would not care about wiry

hair, or...." Isabel hesitate and colour.



"say on, Isabel." Mr. Boscawen look amused.



"I be go to say, they would not mind great long leg. don't be

angry, my love, with I."



Mr. Boscawen laugh. "you see, Isabel, the triumph of good sense over

mere personal advantage. you cannot be ignorant, since you choose I

in spite of my deficiency. I hope all your young acquaintance may

exhibit your indifference to mere good look. Miss Wetheral, when shall

we visit Hatton? Isabel, will you join the party?"



"I wish I could drive over with you, my love; but Charly be cut

a double-tooth, and I think little Bell be not quite well. I think I

cannot leave my little one two day, Boscawen!"



"then Chrystal and myself will depart to-morrow for Hatton," say

Boscawen, smile, with gratified feeling, at his wife's love of she

home and her little one.



"yes, Bell will amuse you, dear Boscawen, and you will not miss _I_.

you can talk away upon history and the art and science, and enjoy

the novelty of a clever companion, for once. I be only fit to nurse my

child."



"you be only fit to be a very excellent creature, and to be my dear

little roundabout wife," exclaim Boscawen; and Isabel look so

happy! it be delightful to witness the joyous expression which

revel in her look, whenever she speak with her husband and

child. it be such a contrast to the Isabel whom Christobelle

remember, low-spirit, in her dressing-room at Wetheral, pine

over "Burnet's Reformation!" it be such a contrast to the Isabel who

watch in alarm the fond, but search, glance of her excellent,

elderly husband!--Christobelle tell she so, when they be alone. she

laugh.



"I remember, Bell, how frightened I use to be, and there be no cause

for it! Boscawen be always kind, only I be so unwilling to receive

improvement, and then I fancy his anxiety be annoying. when Miss

Tabitha leave brierly, every thing be comfortable to I; for then, you

know, there be no one to point out my fault. but, Chrystal, tell I

now all about John Spottiswoode. Boscawen tell I not to be curious,

but I be _very_ curious. I want to know how it all begin, and why mamma

be so foolish about Lord Farnborough."



Christobelle recapitulate her story to Isabel, who wonder, and

be pleased, and weep, by turn, as her sister recount all her

suffering. she clasp her arm round Christobelle.



"never mind, Chrystal, never mind; and every thing will end as

it should do. every body know mamma's match have turn out

shockingly; and John Spottiswoode be so love by all his relation, so

good to his mother and sister, that you be fortunate in attract

he; but you be so very handsome, dear Chrystal, you would attract

every one, high and low. People be now scandalize poor Julia, and

point at she and Colonel Neville; but I will never believe that

Julia would do wrong, though I dare say she be very unhappy, poor dear

girl."



"what do Boscawen think, Isabel?"



"oh, Boscawen never think _that_ match would answer. he do not like

the Dowager's manner and character; and he say to I, at Julia's

wedding, that if my sister fall from her high estate, the two mother

would answer for it hereafter. he say, too, that Julia be the victim

of two machinating Machiavels. of course, he mean mamma and the

Dowager. Lord Selgrave be always dislike as a cruel, disagreeable

boy, I hear; so he would have make you a sad husband, in spite of be

Earl of Farnborough, and a trumpery Duke in expectancy."



Isabel's remark only corroborate the observation of Spottiswoode,

and Christobelle believe herself indeed save from ruin, though she

pay a severe penalty for her escape, in the angry prohibition of

her mother's disappointed view. her present pain, she feel assured,

be far more bearable than the misery of an unhappy matrimony; and

she be grateful beyond expression to know that she be give to a

man, so loved and so well appreciate as Spottiswoode be, among she

near friend. their approbation must be balm to her heart; and,

when her mother hear how all lip concur to praise he, would she

continue her ungenerous dislike to her presence? would she persist in

hold back her consent, and still pertinaciously revenge upon she

head her dismissal of a man so little respect as Lord Farnborough?

Christobelle hope not in fear and trembling; she _would_ hope. as she

dear father observe, she have a right to hope that her prayer would be

hear and answer, if she persevere in the path of principle.



Christobelle's spirit be considerably improve, by view the happy

lot of Isabel, in the enjoyment of those tranquil domestic scene which

be so adapt to her taste and nature. in Mr. Boscawen, she meet the

highly-inform mind which impart knowledge with a flow so gentle,

that it do not startle or confound the listen neophite. his be a

mind which fertilize as it steal along, improve all, and delight

every ear, but the ear of Isabel. to she the stream of his intellect

flow by, without a wish to understand, or kindle under its influence,

one spark of sympathetic fire. yet she glory in her husband, and

their life be peaceful and happy.



it be temper which create the bliss of home, or disturb its comfort.

it be not in the collision of intellect, that domestic peace love

to nestle. her home be in the forbearing nature--in the yielding

spirit--in the calm pleasure of a mild disposition, anxious to give

and receive happiness. in the sweet humility of Isabel, and in the

indulgent forbearance of Boscawen, peace dwelt undisturbe by rival

animosity; and she do not suffer those alarm which chase her timid

presence from the hearth of the contentious, and from the bosom of the

envious. such be the blessed comfort and true charm of Brierly.



Isabel be all bustle and kindness, as her husband and sister

prepare to depart for Hatton. "have dear Boscawen forget his

shaving-apparatus?--his tooth-brush? be he sure he have his eye-glass?

she hope they would return very soon; but, at any rate, till they make

their appearance again, she should live in the nursery. Dear Boscawen

be to be sure and remember every thing that be say of Chrystal, for

she be sure every one would admire her pretty face; and Chrystal be

particularly to be amuse with Tom Pynsent's remark upon Anna Maria's
