far as the Lodges, and she would put on her hat and meet they."



Isabel run off to the nursery, and return with her three child.

Tommy be a soldier, and a drum be append to his neck. Charly have

a new fiddle in his hand, and little Bell be sort with a trumpet,

that she might approach as nearly as possible to her brother's style of

amusement. Isabel place they in the carriage. "now, Charly and Tommy,

don't make a great noise; and don't snatch your sister's trumpet from

she, my love. papa will put you out at the Lodge, and mamma will be

ready to take you. charly, don't squeeze your aunt's pretty dress; and,

Bell, my love, don't push back your bonnet--I don't like to see little

girl push back their bonnet. Chrystal, give my love to every body, and

say I could not leave the little one; I should have do nothing but

think of they. my dear Boscawen, come back very soon; and, Chrystal,

don't stay long."



"any thing more, Isabel?" demand Boscawen. "any thing about curl

head and high forehead?"



"ah, you be laugh at I, now, wicked man! I believe I talk a

great deal of nonsense, but little Bell will not have her mother's

infirmity, I hope."



"I hope she will possess all of they," reply Boscawen, "and only

inherit half her mother's sweet temper. she will then have enough to

raise my pride."



Isabel laugh gaily, and blush at her husband's energetic speech;

but she kiss her hand, with such a happy expression of countenance,

as they drive from the door! how pleasing be the sight, and how it

tend to raise the spirit of Christobelle!



the child do not make more noise than usual, as they drive to the

Lodge; and little Bell only lose her trumpet and her temper once,

during the transit. they be then deposit with the Lodge-keeper

till their mamma should join they, and Mr. Boscawen proceed on his

journey. they stop to change horse at Bridgnorth, and, as they

remain some minute at the Crown, Christobelle remember the

appearance of Thompson, at her first visit there, and the cause of

her sudden recal home. it be after the luckless elopement of Clara,

that she be hurry to Wetheral, to be the companion of her mother,

under circumstance most annoying to herself, connect with the very

marriage she have promote so anxiously. Christobelle be now herself

the object of her anger, for decline to enter into an engagement,

hateful to her heart and principle! how and when would her mother's

soul be divest of its ambitious worldly anxiety?



Christobelle's reception at Hatton be gratify to her feeling in

the high degree. she be surround by affectionate greeting and

congratulation. Mrs. Pynsent wring she hand with kind violence.



"Hollo, Miss Bell, so we have get you back again, and I willn't ask where

Sir Jacky be, because and because. got your blush still, Miss Bell!

so much the well--and I'll be bind you have bring back your good

heart. no, not your heart, but your good temper. here, Bobby, come and

look at our new beauty. I tell you what, Miss Bell, you be a fine

girl than any of your sister; not even that unfortunate, poor Lady

Kerrison come up to you in good look."



"come, come, I'll match my little wife with the good of you," exclaim

Tom, more good-humour, more red-faced than ever. "I'll match my

little wife's bloom even against the handsome 'Bell.' Bell's fine

colour come and fade away again in an instant; but Anna Maria's

cherry cheek be everlaste. look at they!" Tom Pynsent drag his

laugh wife before Christobelle.



"ay, ay," reply Mrs. Pynsent, wink her eye, "we know Anna Maria's

bloom be the right sort--renewable at pleasure. look at Bobby, screw

up his eyes.--What's the matter, Bobby?"



Mr. Pynsent never do, and never could appear to advantage, under the

ridicule which his lady's address always throw around he; but he

do not observe the _de haut en bas_ manner, or else long custom have

take away all feeling upon the extraordinary nature of her remark.

probably he have long felt assure the evil be irremediable. his own

manner be very courteous, but Mr. Pynsent be not a man of many word.

he surrender all speech quietly into the hand of his lady, and

content himself with silently listen to the remark of other,

without adventure his own. Mr. Pynsent take no notice of her question.



"what's the matter, Bobby?" repeat Mrs. Pynsent. "cann't you admire

Miss Bell, without screw up your poor old pair of grey? it's a fair

face to look upon, ben't it, after gaze upon poor Sal. Miss Bell,

poor Sally Hancock be in a precious pickle."



"I hear of it from Isabel."



"you never see such a poor thing!--all over with Sal, Miss Bell; but

the poor creature be so cast down! she have a room here now, to be

amuse by the child, and watch their antic; and, luckily, you know,

she cann't speak plain, to put bad word in their mouth. poor Sally! I

could not let she remain at Lea, in that state; and I think she be very

comfortable here with the child and Bobby."



"where be the child? where be Tom, and where be Moll and Bab?"

ask Tom Pynsent. "Bell must see the child--Bell will want to see

the child--I think I hear they scream just now, somewhere."



"they be fight over a brush just now in the second hall," reply

his mother, "and they nearly kill the baby. I expect the poor

little thing would have get a broken head in the scuffle, but he fight

like a fury, and send his fist into Moll's eye."



"they do fight dreadfully," observe Anna Maria.



"let they alone, I willn't have they check," cry their grandmother;

"when they have have thumps enough, they will be quiet. Moll be bad

than the baby; but their spirit be so high, I willn't have they cow."



"Bell," say Tom Pynsent, with a tone and look of honest pride, "you

have pretty scamp for your relation. Tom ride a shetland after the

hound, and Moll run up a tree like a young squirrel. Bab and the baby

be improve, too, by their example. Tally ho! I hear they!" he run

to the door, and open it. "Tally ho, there! Tom and Moll, bring the

litter this way!"



the four child burst into the drawing-room like a pack of hound,

and the baby, a stout child of a year old toddle in, he fall down,

and the other run over he. Tom Pynsent catch up the sturdy boy.



"don't give tongue, you young rascal, but fight 'em, Bill--here, double

your fist at they all."



the child mechanically close his little fist, and his father place

he before Miss Bab.



"battle she well, Bill, rattle she."



the child, who have not yet cast his cap, deal a blow at his sister,

which Miss Bab return by knock he down. the child do not attempt

to cry at the blow, but, rise from the floor, he again double his

infantine fist for the battle. Tom Pynsent be delighted.



"well do, Bill, well do, my sharp lad! come, that's enough at a

time! live to fight another day, Bill!"



"come to your granny, my sharpshooter," cry Mrs. Pynsent; "I have

something in my pocket for stout-hearted man!"



Billy toddle to his grandmother, who draw a box of sugarplum from

her capacious pocket, and reward his prowess by a shower of sweet.

Tom and Moll be likewise engage in a controversy, which threaten

to end in an engagement. they be quarrel over Christobelle's

parasol, Moll demand it to walk with, and be a lady, like aunt Bell,

while Tom insist upon shoulder it like a bayonet, as the Count de

Nolis have teach he. the dispute run very high.



"Tom, dear, don't let the child fight," say Anna Maria, as she

examine the make and material of her sister's silk pelisse, "they have

be fight all day."



"Aunt Bell, mayn't I have it?" scream little Moll, as she struggle

with her brother for the possession of the parasol.



"I will have it first!" roar Tom, drag the handle from her grasp.



Mr. Boscawen extricate the parasol from their hand, and keep

possession of it during their stay in the drawing-room, but no one else

attempt to release Christobelle's property from the struggle. Tom

Pynsent call their attention from their defeat.



"now, Tom, catch papa if you can, and show your aunt whether your leg

be as stout as your lung. Moll! Bab! Bill! now for it!"



the drawing-room become a scene of dreadful confusion. Tom Pynsent,

delight to show off his child, and always the foremost to give

they pleasure, thread the maze of the table and chair, while the

little one race scream and halloo after he. Mr. Boscawen

seek a retreat from their deafen shout by quit the room,

and even Anna Maria half close her eye, as she assure her sister

they make really more noise that day than ever, in compliment, she

suppose, to her arrival. Mrs. Pynsent sit with the box of sugarplum

on her lap, enjoy the din of voice, and incite they forward,

by clap her hand and exclaiming:--"Hurrah, my lad and lass,

catch he! round the chair, Bill!--down you go--up again--well do,

my hearty! halloo there, Moll! Bab, you be hang!" Mr. Pynsent look

overpower, but he say nothing.



such be the sport of Hatton. the commotion continue till both

party, the chaser and the chase, became weary with their

exertion, and then the child wish to go and ride their

rock-horse. Mrs. Pynsent load they with sweetmeat and good

advice, as she dismiss each from her presence.



"I say, you young Tommy, don't suck your finger, but look to that poor

morsel of a Bill, and don't run over he. if he fall, pick he up, and

wipe his nose, like a little gentleman. here, my Moll in the wad, look

at your torn frock, and don't thump Bab upon the back so hard. never

mind, Bab, here's six sugarplum for that thump, and you must give it

Moll well, to-morrow. what, Bill, old boy! you must have sugarplum,

too, must you? there then, and toddle after they, my sharpshooter. go

all of you to old aunt Hancock."



when the child be go, Christobelle have time to give she

attention to Anna Maria. the elegance of Miss Wetheral have in a

great degree lose its tone, but Mrs. Tom Pynsent be fashionably

french still in her dress and appearance. rouge very highly give an

unnatural brilliance to her eye, and her figure have become enlarge,

though not in the same proportion with Isabel: Christobelle think

her handsome and striking, but she be not the pale, still, and

interestingly elegant woman, who have volunteer her affection to the

stout, good-look, red-faced Tom Pynsent. many might have consider

Anna Maria improve by the change which have gradually take place in

her appearance; but Christobelle have admire she so greatly in she

more youthful day, that her eye could not reconcile itself to her

present style. there be, she think, something too garish in the

deeply-rouge cheek and glitter eye of her sister.



her affection for her husband be quite unchanged: she still speak of

he with powerful affection, and dilate upon his unvaried kindness and

good temper with vivacity. during Christobelle's long absence, Tom

have never change towards she in indulgence and interest. Mrs. Pynsent

be all that could be desire in a mother-in-law, for her warm heart

never fancy she could do enough for those she love; and poor Mr.

Pynsent be in nobody's way. Anna Maria dote upon her child, and

she confess herself to be "the happy woman in the world, when the

child do not fight, but they certainly do fight furiously, and Tom

and his mother encourage it."



Christobelle ask after the health of Félicé.



"oh, Félicé be very well, but she cannot comprehend a word of English,

stupid girl, and I be lose my French. every body borrow Félicé, and

she travel all round the county before any public meeting take place.

Félicé be always borrow by Pen Spottiswoode before the race, and,

when she appear, you would declare her clothe be cut out by the

Lidham cook, instead of Félicé. Pen never dress well, you know, Bell."



"Miss Bell," cry Mrs. Pynsent, from a distant corner of the

drawing-room, "have you hear any tiding from Bedinfield? because

there be a rod in pickle for somebody in that quarter. your poor sister

have make a sorry concern of that grand match, which be to her so

desirable. not much well a business than Lady Kerrison's! report be

say the deuce and all of poor Miss Julia and that colonel. that

dowager never be like by any one, for her acquaintance be always a

blight upon the poor soul who make it."



Christobelle ask if her sister have be in Shropshire, since their

removal into Scotland.



"no, my dear; Lady Ennismore have never suffer her son to bring your

sister into Shropshire, since her return to England. she have her own

reason for it. I know what I could call she, only I have promise

Sally Hancock never to use large word, now her own mouth be stop,

poor thing! if your sister elope with that moustache fellow, it will

be the fault of those who marry she to such a poor creature as my

lord."



"have you hear any thing in _particular_ about Julia, my dear Anna

Maria?" ask Christobelle, in a low voice.



"report only," be her reply, "but they begin to assume a form. there

be very strange report about Colonel Neville, but we do not hear from

Julia; she have never write to I since I go to the altar with she,

and I be tired with write unanswered letter. Papa tell we when he

return with you from Bedinfield, that it be vain to hope she would

ever be withdraw from the dowager; and if she do discover treachery,

it would only make she wretched, without a hope of escape. Lord

Ennismore be devoted to his mother, and Julia would suffer, he think,

by any complaint on her side. we hear Lord Ennismore be in a poor way,

but we know nothing: Julia might as well be in another hemisphere,

since we neither meet nor correspond. how miserably Clara and Julia

have be in their choice, Bell! I cannot be too thankful I win my dear

Tom at last."



"I say, Miss Bell," cry Mrs. Pynsent from her corner, where she sit

knitting, "I say, Miss Bell, when do Sir Jacky return to we?" Anna

Maria smile at her sister's confusion. Christobelle hesitate for a

moment to reply.



"Miss Bell, Sir Jacky be a great favourite of mine, and I want to hear

a little about he. come here, Miss Bell, come near to I, I want to

ask a question."



Christobelle approach Mrs. Pynsent, amuse at the idea of she

intend joke, which could not offend, since only Anna Maria be

now present. the two gentleman have seek for Mr. Boscawen when the

child disperse. she, therefore, seat herself near she.



"what have you to say to I, Mrs. Pynsent, that be not kind and

pleasant at all time?"



"I have this to say," she reply, with a seriousness foreign to her

usual manner, "I have to say, that, much as I like you as a girl, I

love you far well than ever, _now_, because you have the sense to

refuse a young coroneted rascal some time ago, and choose a man who

will be a jewel to you. you show sense and spirit in refuse to be

manœuvre into wickedness, to lead the abominable life which two of

your sister have be doom to suffer; and you show a right woman's

warm heart, in take a man whom I like and respect next to my own Tom

for comeliness and godliness. when a young woman marry such a tight

lad as Jacky Spottiswoode, she know she will be happy to the day of

her death, and be respect among her friend. now, Miss Bell, what do

you say to that?"



Christobelle say nothing. tear fill her eye, and speak volume

for she. she be affect with the idea that all her friend, except

_one_, approve the connexion she be about to form. Mrs. Pynsent's

remark affect she still more, because they be speak with unusual

quietness of manner and phrase, and her word ever come from the

fountain of her heart in all truth and sincerity. she speak, too, the

sentiment of those around she; Christobelle judge so, by Mrs. Pynsent

assure she of the respect of her friend. it be a deep gratification

to think her attachment be sanction by those she love and honour;

and it be a grateful pride to feel assured the finger of scorn or

ridicule have never point observation offensive to the high character

of her beloved spottiswoode. be it to be wonder at that she sit

silent by the side of Mrs. Pynsent, enjoy feeling too bless for

utterance? she see her agitation, and forbore to notice it; but Mrs.

Pynsent be not want in real delicacy to those who do not offend

her notion of right; and to Christobelle she have ever show peculiar

kindness. she address her daughter.



"I say, Anna Maria, we'll have old Sally Hancock to spend the evening

with we, to compliment Miss Bell. it's all over with Sal now, so she

willn't shock the company. you need not be afraid of Sally Hancock any

more, for she cann't speak, if she be die for it. I can see her poor

eye glare up sometimes; you willn't mind Sally Hancock. she like to

watch the child, and when they don't pull her crutch from she, they

be great friend. Moll and Bab often imitate her walk, but Sally

Hancock only laugh at they."



"I wish the child would not fight so much," remark Anna Maria.



"Fiddle diddle! my Tom fight Pen Spottiswoode like a dragon when he

be their age, and he be all the well for it. I don't like to keep

down their spirit. how would our little Moll climb the sycamore,

if she haven't a fearless spirit? well, look at Bobby, between those

two great monster upon the lawn! Boscawen look such a long animal,

compare with Bobby. don't you think Bobby be wear into half a goose,

Miss Bell? he looks truss for the spit."



"I think Mr. Pynsent look very well--well than your letter

insinuate, Mrs. Pynsent."



"Poor Bobby! no, he's nothing well than half a goose now; but Sally

Hancock and myself remember he a smart lad. there go the half-hour

bell!"



"come, then, Bell, we will depart, for my toilette be a long affair,"

say her sister, rise.



"I say," call Mrs. Pynsent, as they leave the room, "I say, Anna

Maria, don't let Bell into the secret of the prison-house."



"oh! Bell know all about _that_!" reply the laugh Anna Maria.

"Tom be the only person who do not know my secret. every body know I

only rouge to please Tom."









CHAPTER XXIX.





Hatton be by no mean so agreeable a _séjour_ to Christobelle as in

former day. the pynsent be never happy without the four unruly

child constantly in their sight; and their amusement be the chief

subject under consideration. the child scramble over the table to

snatch at the dessert; they be admit in the drawing-room at all

hour, and in every phase of dirt and fighting; they be to drag heavy

weight round the room at pleasure, and every one make themselves a

party in their quarrel.



Mrs. Pynsent generally advocate the part of the baby, whom she

designate "that proper divil of a bill," with a hearty vehemence which

increase the uproar and confusion; while her son, with stentorian

voice, argue in favour of the girl. Anna Maria rarely interfere

in the alarum which occur. she sit smile at the fray, only her

distress be occasionally awaken by the length and frequency of the

battle, and her taste be offend at interval, by the disagreeable

abbreviation of their name. she "wish her Mary could be call by

her right name, and not Moll. she would give any thing they would call

the baby Willy, instead of that horrid 'Bill;' and as to Bab, it be a

shocking word; but bab she would be call for ever. Barbara be too

long a word for her mother and Tom to pronounce. sometimes she fancy

their noise must be disagreeable to their guest, but Tom love to have

the little thing round he."



the Pynsents be, therefore, the happy people possible in

themselves; but it be extremely disagreeable. every body must think

Hatton a very disagreeable place to stay at now; and Christobelle be

glad to escape with Mr. Boscawen the follow day to Lidham. she

have little difficulty in privately persuade he to curtail their

intend stay at Hatton, and proceed to the Spottiswoodes. he, as well

as Christobelle, feel the utter hopelessness of procure a peaceful

moment, where every thought and feeling be absorb in four remarkably

noisy child. how very different to the Hatton of other day, when

she enjoy the society of her sister undisturbed, and spend there such

happy hour of her life! it be there, too, she meet first the man whom

she hope to make happy for long year of futurity.



Christobelle think they should never be allow to enter the carriage

when it draw up to the door. the child be delighted to get in and

out, and Tom particularly amuse himself with put up the step and

throw they down again with as much noise as the leather would allow.

Tom Pynsent detain Christobelle in the hall, to enable she to enjoy

a scene which he consider most delectable; and Mrs. Pynsent utter

exclamation of delight, as she watch the baby try with all its

might to imitate his companion.



"I say, Tom, do look at that divil of a Bill, try to clamber into

the carriage; do you ever see such a young dog? Moll, put Bill into

the carriage. let my sharpshooter take his turn. Moll, you'll break his

leg!"



the "sharpshooter" be hand into the carriage by the butler, for

"Moll" could not lift the scramble child, and they all begin jump

upon the seat till a battle commence, through the instrumentality of

Bab, who have pull Tom's hair rather too roughly. the scream of Tom

be echo by the baby; and Bab cry violently at her own ferocity.

Tom Pynsent and their grandmother both speak at once, in their loud

key.



"Halloo there, you young one. Bill, what be you at, all of you? hand

out that young dog," cry Mrs. Pynsent.



"what the devil be you all roar at? Moll, what's the matter?"

call out Tom Pynsent.



"Pa, Bab pull Tom's hair!" scream Miss Mary, alia Moll; Mrs.

Pynsent's word now became confound with those of her son.



"what the devil!--don't fight there, you rascals!--Hand out Bill,

James; they'll kill that poor Bill.--Here, Tom, never mind your hair:

bring some cake here, Dick, to stop this row.--Hand out that bill

thing, Thomas, he's on his head.--They're murder bill!"



"I declare my child will fight themselves to death," say Anna

Maria, who take no part in the affair, "I be sure they will kill each

other. Tom, dear, don't let the child fight so."



Mr. Boscawen take advantage of the moment when the carriage be empty

of its noisy content, and hurry Christobelle into it. she be too

willing to quit the uproar of Hatton not to rejoice at his polite

movement, and both be glad to remain silent for some time after they

have quit its ground.



"I fancy," say Mr. Boscawen, after a long pause, "that Isabel

spoil her child, till I have now compare they with their cousin.

I shall remain satisfied in future that they be not more vivacious

than healthy child should be."



"it be altogether a different form of government at brierly. you be

monarch, though an indulgent one; but it be a frightful democracy at

Hatton."



"I shall keep my young one out of the infection," he observe; "for

though Mary and Barbara may have heart as kindly affectione as their

grandmother, those manner be deplorable. I should be sorry to see my

little Bell become coarse and loud in her way of speak."



"what could be the cause of the Miss Wycherlys imbibe such manner,

in the first instance?" ask Christobelle.



"Old Wycherly be a broker," reply Boscawen; "and he retire to

Lidham with an immense fortune, and a young wife whose connection

be far superior to his own. Mrs. Wycherly do not live many year,

and the daughter be allow to educate themselves, and to act, in

every respect, as seem good in their own eye. they be always the

subject of conversation; and, though they never be suspect of any

thing more reprehensible than extreme wildness, their conduct subject

they to many extraordinary scene, and much objectionable remark.

Captain Hancock drank, I believe, to drown care, and Mrs. Hancock be

infinitely the bad of the two. how the young lady learn their

swearing propensity, I cannot tell; but I have hear that their

brother Wycherly lead they into very exceptionable society in his

youthful day. they be an extraordinary trio. no one, however, speak

ill of they."



"they have great good nature, I suppose."



"they have great wealth, Bell, or they never could have hold any

position in society. four hundred thousand pound draw the first

gentleman in the county to Lidham."



Christobelle chat till the Lidham wood rise in sight, and then she

become silent. every thing connect with the name of Spottiswoode hold

a powerful interest in her heart, and concentrate her thought upon

himself. she have hear from he once since her arrival at brierly, and

then he speak so cheerlessly of her mother's spirit, that he linger

to assist and console her father. he know, he say, that he be give

her pleasure by attend upon her father, and he hope so much from

his assiduity! dear Spottiswoode, how much more sanguinely than

herself do he expect a change in her mother's sentiment!



as the carriage drive through the lodge, a gentleman on horseback

gallop towards they. "here come Charles full of news," say

Boscawen; "and he be ride fast, I suppose, to carry it fresh into

Shrewsbury. a little news be a passport among one's neighbour."



Christobelle bend forward to observe Charles Spottiswoode; but no, it

be _not_ Charles Spottiswoode. her heart beat thickly, and her eye

strain to gaze. she know the horseman from afar: she know the air,

the figure, and the style of riding, well. be her lover wing his

way to her?--was he think of Brierly, and one there who love he

well than herself? Christobelle catch her brother's hand. "Boscawen,

it be not he, it be _John_ Spottiswoode! what bring he so soon from

Fairlee?"



"God bless I!" cry Boscawen, "it be indeed our friend from the

north, and I must stop he, or he will never condescend to look at

we." he put his head out of the carriage-window, and wave his hand:

Sir John Spottiswoode heed not the movement. he be ride rapidly

by, and would have pass with a slight inclination of the head, have

not Christobelle catch his eye. his handsome face glow with surprise

and delight, while her own feeling, so very suddenly call into

action, completely take away her power of speech. she could only hold

out her hand, as he check his horse, and wheel round to the window,

but it be press _so_ fondly--and he look _so_ bright and happy!



"why, Spottiswoode, what fair lady be you scamper after, that you

nearly pass by _us_?" exclaim Boscawen, shake he by the hand.



"never mind, I be go to turn back with you. indeed, I be gallop

to Shrewsbury, to get upon the coach for Brierly. I only arrive this

morning."



"my _mother_, Spottiswoode," utter Christobelle, in alarm--"how be my

mother?"



"on her road to Wetheral, by short stage; I be but their _avant

courier_, Chrystal. I have bring a letter for you. you will now,

perhaps, offer I a seat in your carriage to Brierly, when you return.

my brother say nothing about your visit--do they expect you?"



"no, I be make a tour with my young sister--but let we reach Lidham

before we enter into particular."



the carriage move on, and Spottiswoode ride by its side. how

unexpected be this meeting, and how busy be Christobelle's thought,

conjecture upon the motive of her mother's early journey! if it have

but a happy reference towards herself, how would her cup of joy be

fill! but, no, her mother never forgave an offence like her!



Christobelle be prepare to meet astonishment at Lidham, as she have

find it every where else, since her return into Shropshire, and she

be not deceive. Mrs. Charles Spottiswoode hold she from she at arm's

length, as she examine her person and growth.



"my dear little Bell, be this really and truly yourself? I have hear

of you from person whom I do not consider altogether unprejudiced in

their account; but, indeed, I now see it with my own eye! John, you

have not say half enough of this creature. I recognise her eye--those

large eye--but these ringlet--that figure--no, John, upon my honour,

you do not do her justice!"



Spottiswoode stand by Christobelle, and his eye flash a proud

satisfaction at the remark of his sister-in-law.



"but, Charles, Charles," she continue, "tell I if _you_ could

recognise Bell Wetheral in this grand creature! tell I if it be not a

vision, for I cannot think I really see the prim little Bell, always

pore over book, and diving out of her father's study with a little

shock head, like my terri Tarter!"



"yes, I recognise Miss Wetheral," answer Mr. Spottiswoode, "for I

see the same expression of good-humour, and the same fine outline,

which give such promise of what we behold. Miss Wetheral, you be most

welcome to Lidham."



"by the Lord Harry!" cry Mr. Wycherly, emerge from his own room in

spectacle, "here's a _posse comitatus_! well, I'm come to welcome the

new filly myself. how d'ye do, Miss Wetheral?--how d'ye do, ma'am? God

help we, how the young people grow! they run we down, Mr. Boscawen! you

be come to stay a week--a month with we, I hope? come in, come in,

all of you!" they enter the sitting-room, and the conversation be

general for a short time, till Mrs. Spottiswoode suddenly turn to

Christobelle.



"my dear Bell, I know what your anxiety must be, to hear of those whom

John have leave behind. I see, by the expression of those large eye,

that you be long to hear news of Fairlee. come with I, and I give

Excellent Boscawen! how fortunate be Isabel in secure a man so

and Christobelle be leave at Lidham with the Spottiswoodes--the Miss

Dr. Darwin at once discover the secret of Lady Ennismore's state, and

what could be the reason which prompt the Dowager to poison the

CHAPTER XXX.





Lady Ennismore's illness be long in its continuance and severe in

its effect. Mrs. Spottiswoode watch over she with unceasing and

affect attention; and it be often painful to witness her agonized

fear, lest her friend should sink beneath the combine attack of

mental and bodily suffer. it be sad to look upon a creature so

change as Julia, and hear her touching exclamation under the effect

of almost constant delirium. it be heart-rend to hear her call upon

Lady Ennismore with passionate entreaty, demand admittance to she

lord, and implore she to save she from Neville--it be still more

touching to hear her imprecate sorrow upon the head of those who could

lure a desolate wife to her destruction.



the sight of Dr. Darwin terrify the invalid for many day. she

address he as Colonel Neville, and her wander fancy conceive

that Colonel Neville be seek an interview. she wave he from the

room with an impetuous movement, and implore he to leave an unhappy

wife in peace. she say he know she love he--he know she love the

friend who be kind to she when all other have desert she, but she

command he to fly from her!--she be the wife of Lord Ennismore, and

no power on earth should induce she to listen to he. "if her father

be alive to protect she," she say, "he would shelter she from woe and

treachery--but all be go who ever love she, and she would go to

her long lose friend Penelope."



it be evident Lady Ennismore have fly from Bedinfield to escape

from her own heart, as she avow to Mrs. Spottiswoode. it be

evident, also, that the Dowager-countess have act an unholy part by

her daughter, in throw she constantly into the society of Colonel

Neville, and bar her entrance into the apartment of her sickly

and weak-minded husband. such cruelty towards an innocent and trusting

creature affect the heart of Mrs. Spottiswoode almost to illness: she

mind dwelt long and strongly upon the distress of heart, which must

have be endure by her friend before she could have flee from her

husband's house; and Mr. Spottiswoode become alarmed lest his wife's

health should fall a sacrifice to her devoted attachment to the friend

of her early day.



in this time of severe trial to all party, Sir John Spottiswoode

act with the kind consideration; and he undertake to meet Sir John

Wetheral upon his arrival, and break to he the mournful intelligence

of Lady Ennismore's situation. before his arrival, however, her

herself watch over and protect by those she love. her weakness be

excessive, and her mind still dwelt upon the sorrow which oppress

she; but it be no long accompany by that fearful aberration of

intellect, which waste her strength, and tear the heart of those who

witness its effect. Mrs. Spottiswoode pass her wake hour at

her friend's side, for it be a cordial to Julia's spirit to see she

near, and to know Penelope be watch over she; but Christobelle be

depute to guard her short and uneasy slumber. it be a period of

deep and tremble anxiety.



Sir John Wetheral's care be crowd upon he. he have be destine

for some year to witness the shattered nerve and depressed spirit

Julia, and to support she through the difficulty of her situation. it

be a severe trial to his feeling; but he meet the intelligence with

fortitude. his unworldly action cause no self-reproach, and he have

never sacrifice his sense of right to the idol of a vain imagination,

therefore his regret, however bitter, be untinge by the gloomy

reflection that his own hand have barb the arrow which strike his

heart.



not so Lady Wetheral. the intelligence of Julia's flight to Lidham

prostrate she to the earth, and bow down all her hope. she confine

herself to her own room, and talk incessantly to Mrs. Bevan of the

misery which overpower she. "she have do such thing for Clara and

Julia!--she have form, and prosecute, and bring into effect, plan

for their advancement, which should have raise they far above their

companion; yet they be dash to the ground, after such anxiety

and fear! the daughter whom she peculiarly consider under her own

rule have prove themselves unequal to enjoy the exalt situation she

have mark out for they; and Bell, a perfect child, foster among the

wild hill of Lochleven, have presume to refuse a dukedom, in order to

follow her mean affection, and content herself with a small estate

in Worcestershire! what be Julia, now? a truant wife--a woman, so

lose to propriety, that her future position in people's mind must be

equivocal--a creature who would in future be gaze upon as a deserter

from Bedinfield. such be the fate of those who create scenes!--a

world's wonder, and a thing of nought!" a period of silence would

ensue, subsequent to each irritable complaint; and Lady Wetheral would

sink into deep fit of despondency, follow by sudden reaction, which

threaten to undermine her constitution.



when Julia become sufficiently calm to bear the sight of a third person

Mrs. Spottiswoode embrace she, and bade her think of happy time.

Julia look at she with eye swim in tear.

"if they tell you, Chrystal, that life have no blessing beyond high

I feel for Lord Ennismore's ailment. I do not know how deeply those

ailment affect the temper and weaken the mind. I be ambitious--I be

Penelope, save I from myself!"



"fear not," reply Mrs. Spottiswoode, kneel by her bed-side, and

clasp Julia's hand in her own, "fear not, my companion and friend!

I will be with you, and my counsel and affection shall support you.

think no more of the past, but hope every thing from the future: forget

youth, all prepare to welcome and cherish you, dear Julia."



"I wish I _could_ forget--I wish I could struggle, and burst the bond

which destroy my tranquillity, Penelope! I wish I could wipe away

Mrs. Spottiswoode sign to Christobelle to withdraw, and she retire

"Julia, open your heart to your old favourite, and remember there be a

time when we never conceal a thought from each other's knowledge."



Julia gasp and tremble.



"Julia, your friend have guess the struggle you have endure, and

"you be young, Julia; you could not suspect an artful and

Mrs. Spottiswoode weep over the friend who lie prostrate, in body and

"Penelope, as you love I, never name even to myself the horrible

"rest, rest, Julia," say Mrs. Spottiswoode, "and do not exert your

Lady Ennismore shake her head, and the tear which trickle through her

"exert yourself no more, dear Julia. I know all that be pass in

but the conversation have not weaken Lady Ennismore. her spirit be

Lady Ennismore weep silently for some time, but she do not weep with

Sir John Wetheral's visit to Lidham be of daily occurrence; he

"Julia, look there!"

the sight of her father _do_ rouse every recollection!



it be needless to dwell upon a scene so fraught with distress, or to

when Sir John Wetheral and his daughter become more tranquil, Mrs.

Lady Ennismore recapitulate the event which have take place since her

"after her father's departure from Bedinfield, as she think, so

"she begin to fear Lord Ennismore do not love she; she begin to

Julia stop; deep sigh burst from her bosom, and her head fall

"at Florence, a complete separation be silently effect between Lord

Julia's emotion increase, and she be nearly faint, but she wave

Sir John Wetheral press his unhappy daughter to his bosom, and

Lady Ennismore sink upon her knee before her father, and clasp his

"as I live to breathe again my native air, and see the form I love,

Poor suffer Julia! how she tremble as her father raise she from

the virtuous conduct which have not desert she, and for the prayerful

spirit which have lead she to seek refuge in her God, instead of throw

herself into the arm of man. how do a father's blessing soothe the

lacerated heart of the ill-use Julia!



from the hour of her confession, Lady Ennismore become more tranquil;

and, though her constitution have receive a powerful shock, it be

it seem that Clara and herself have be doom to woe; and, though

when Lady Ennismore hear of Christobelle's engagement, she throw she

"Chrystal," she say, with a look of sadness, "I can wish no one joy

Sir John Spottiswoode be at Alverton. he devote the period of

Charles Spottiswoode also depart with his brother for a short season,

Lady Ennismore could not be expect to forget that "such thing be;"

but there be blessedness in feeling that her youthful error have not

Sir John Wetheral write a calm and powerful appeal to the heart

by her husband. he inform he of his resolution to protect that

more would he allow she to return to the man who have so deeply injure

her feeling, and her spotless reputation. he beg to say all future

intercourse must end for ever between the family of Ennismore and

Wetheral."



in due time a note be receive, in the handwriting of the

Dowager-countess, bear these concise sentences:--



 "the Dowager-countess of Ennismore regret that the increase disorder

 of her son, Lord Ennismore, must compel she to become his amanuensis.

 the flight of Lady Ennismore be well know to herself; and the flight

 of Colonel Neville, at the same period, be also well understand by her

 and his mother will endeavour to supply her place by devoted attention

 to his offended and outraged feeling. Lady Ennismore be happy, if

 not respectable, in be uphold by her friend. the family of

 Bedinfield and Wetheral will meet no more."



this note be date from Florence, and its content be withhold from

Julia. it would have cause the wound of her heart to bleed afresh. it

be well for her peace of mind, to remain in ignorance of Neville's

flight, and to be unconscious of the remark which point at her fame.



Julia decline see even Mrs. Pynsent, till her nerve have recover

mind, fatal to her tranquillity; and the first sight of Anna Maria's

acquire under the gentle soothing and support of Mrs. Spottiswoode.

Julia contemplate the meeting with alarm, and in tear.



Mrs. Spottiswoode accompany Sir John Wetheral and Christobelle, as

mind how mournfully it implore I to avoid repentance! every thing
