"forgive me!--forgive my obstinate presumption, papa! I feel how

truly you speak! how blindly I follow my own judgment! this be a

bitter stroke to I! all be here who do not advocate my ambitious

choice!--but where be they who tell I I should be greatly envy?

where be my mother, who prophecie worldly happiness, and tell I I be

right to persevere? who assure I of bright reality, and year of

happy freedom?"



Julia rise from her kneeling attitude, and the expression of her eye

be fearfully wild, as she hold out her hand to her father.



"why do she tell I my father love lowly thing, and could not

comprehend a woman's heart? why do she tell I Ennismore be easily

influence, and that a wife's word would supersede a mother's

management? have it be so? have I not suffer scorn, and ridicule,

and banishment, in silence? have I not endure a thousand regret--a

thousand struggle--a thousand insult?"



Julia pause as her eye again wander over the mirror, and she see

the reflection of her own waste figure and pale countenance. for one

moment her whole attention be engross by the change which have take

place in her person. she gaze at her thin form, and raise her hand

to examine the waste finger which have lose their once plump roundness

and extreme beauty. she then flee from the apartment.









CHAPTER XXXI.





sorrow, renew by the association which press upon her mind,

impel Lady Ennismore to seek her mother's apartment. Mrs. Bevan be

attend her mistress, and Julia's noiseless step glide across the

carpet floor of the dressing-room, where Lady Wetheral lie extended

on the sofa, complain to her attendant of her own wretched feeling.



"Bevan, I be very ill to-day: I cannot see Mrs. Tom Pynsent, or admit

any one. my nerve become bad and bad, and I be in a dreadful state

of tremour at this moment. I cannot hold my salt bottle, it fall out

of my poor nervous finger--I be very ill to-day."



Mrs. Bevan speak pleasing word of comfort, but her ladyship reject

they.



"don't talk nonsense, Bevan. I hate to hear people say thing which be

not likely to occur. how can I expect to be well, when Miss Wetheral

obstinately defy my wish, and all my child be determined to

fly in my face? I have a dream, too, last night, which increase my

disorder; I dream I see Lady Ennismore brilliantly dress, walk in

a procession; and she walk so stately in jewel, and her rank place

she so high among the great one, that I be proud of my daughter, and

I smile to see she in grandeur. poor Julia, where be she _now_!"



"she be here," exclaim Lady Ennismore, stand before her mother,

with her thin hand cross upon her bosom; "here be the envy

Countess of Ennismore!"



Lady Wetheral gaze upon the vision in dumb amazement.



"look at I," continue Julia, "look at my figure, and tell I if you

believe all this would come to pass? when you assure I that wealth

and rank be happiness and virtue, do you think I should return a

fugitive, to seek shelter at your hand?"



"Julia!" gasp Lady Wetheral, "Julia! go! who be _you_?"



"go?" exclaim Lady Ennismore, "where shall I go? to Clara? shall I

rest with poor Clara? must I be lay by poor Clara, to find peace

after my sacrifice, my absence, and my grief?"



the tremour which attack Lady Wetheral's frame be alarming. it

preclude speech: she hide her face with her hand, as Lady Ennismore

proceed.



"for quit a husband's home, I may be censure and avoid by the

world, for it may never know my provocation and my struggle, but

I should not be turn from my mother's presence! I should not be

banish by the author of all my misery, as if she have no part in the

misery which I endure!"



"do not say so--oh, do not say so! do not blame I, as Clara do!" Lady

Wetheral sob aloud.



"I reproach no one," answer Lady Ennismore, mournfully. "I reproach

no one, though I be promise happiness as the wife of Ennismore. where

be that happiness? you foretold it, mother. you say I should for ever

enjoy wealth and station, and become the envy gaze of thousand!

where be it all?"



"cease, cease!" cry Lady Wetheral, wring by feeling of alarm and

self-reproach. "I wish you to marry Pynsent, Julia!"



"cruel mother," exclaim Lady Ennismore, as she catch her hand, and

look earnestly in her face, "do not say so, to drive I wilder than

my poor brain feel now! do you not hold up Ennismore to my view, as a

creature to worship? do you not tell I his coronet be worth a daring

grasp, if I could gain the courtly bauble? oh, you bid I secure the

lofty establishment, and I do so, and have suffer! I wish I be with

poor dead Clara! we both turn from our father, and would not heed

his mild precept. we listen to project which suit our ambitious

nature, though he deprecate the unholy passion. oh, mother, you

foster the wild and dangerous feeling! I wish I be lay by the side

of Clara! I wish I be at rest, like she!"



"Bevan, Bevan," ejaculate Lady Wetheral, "where be you?"



Mrs. Bevan curtsey as she stand in mute astonishment behind her

lady's sofa. she be unable to speak: her eye be rivet upon Lady

Ennismore, who still grasp her mother's hand, and still continue her

wild address.



"this have be a fearful affair! two of we have fall--one into the

grave, and one into living sorrow--self-banish from a heartless

home. be it not a fearful thing?"



"do not blame I, as Clara do--do not blame I for your flight,

Julia," say Lady Wetheral, endeavour to withdraw her hand, but Lady

Ennismore clasp it more closely.



"I blame no one--but two of we be lose for ever. I blame no one!"



"I detest scene--I ever detest scene, Julia!" Lady Wetheral rise

into a sit posture as she speak. "I warn you from the beginning,

all of you, never to offend I by violent measure, which draw down

ridicule and disgust. Clara and yourself be marry greatly, Julia!"



"where have be our greatness?" say Lady Ennismore, despondingly.



"you be both place in affluence," retort her mother, with

nervous trepidation, "and your high position be exalt above your

companion. you be greatly marry--that be _my_ do: but you have

throw yourself from the pinnacle of earthly honour--and that be

_your_ do!"



"Mother, I have be betray, banish from my husband's

presence--unhappy, and uncare for," say Lady Ennismore, release

her hand, and sink upon the floor in despair.



"I tell you," continue Lady Wetheral, become almost vehement in her

manner, "I tell you many thing might occur to distress your heart, but

nothing could arise to make you an object of ridicule to the world,

except your own folly. you have fly from Lord Ennismore's house--who

will receive you? who receive a truant wife?"



"I be miserable," say the prostrate Julia.



"how few be otherwise," return her mother, "if all secret be

disclose? happiness be a nonsensical word--a rock to shipwreck

romantic hope. we may not command happiness, but we _can_ command

external blessing. with every luxury that reflect honour upon human

being, what right have you and Clara to be otherwise than content?"



"how cold--how cruel to speak so harshly!" ejaculate Lady Ennismore.



"have you not rank?" continue Lady Wetheral--"had you not a princely

home--an earl's coronet? have you not all the world can bestow, when you

flee from your husband's protection?"



"I flee from treachery and from infamy!"



"Infamy! who dare report of infamy?" Lady Wetheral start to her

foot, and support herself by grasp the back of a chair. "have my

daughter, Lady Ennismore, allow herself to become--? have the breath

of suspicion breathe upon a Wetheral!--has _one_ suspicion glance

upon you, Julia?"



"I have fly to my father, to avoid my own reproach," cry Julia; "I

care not for the world--I have fly to escape the reproach of my own

heart."



"Folly--madness!" observe her mother--"flown from your heart! what

heart have you which be not wed to your station--to the eminence in

life upon which you be call to stand above your companion? be you

not wed to the title of Ennismore? be you not the proud wife of a

British peer?--an earl's wife? be not your heart hide behind the fold

of your ermine, and bury in the magnificence of your lot?"



"no, no--it be not there!" cry Julia, clasp her hands--"it

be not there! my heart be give to kindness. I would have love

he faithfully, but I be banish from his presence--second to

his artful mother in his thought--betray by the person I most

trust--proscribe as the mistress of his house. many woman would

have resent the indignity I have bear--but I have fly from the

temptation which surround I, and my father have give I shelter.

oh! you have sacrifice I, but do not upbraid I--I have do no

wrong to any one. why should _you_ look hardly upon I, who promise

I happiness, and have break its fulfilment? poor Clara! how we have

suffer for our fault. my father warn I of my wickedness!"



"do _i_ not warn you, Lady Ennismore?" ask Lady Wetheral, with a

raise complexion, as she behold, unmoved, poor Julia's suppliant

attitude. "do _i_ not say, I scorn a woman who be mean enough to

seek the world's upbraid by her conduct? you be the Countess of

Ennismore--your flight have bring down obloquy upon the name. who will

believe the statement of a runaway? who will believe the fugitive Lady

Ennismore have be unaccompanie in her flight? the voice of the world

will be loud in censure upon the step you have take."



"oh, my father, my father! save I from the world--save I from

reproach like these!" exclaim Julia, rise from her prostrate

attitude, and endeavour to quit the room; but her mother catch she

dress, and detain she. there be something awful in the expression of

her countenance, as she address Lady Ennismore.



"if a mother sacrifice her time and endeavour to form a child's

happiness, have she not a right to expect its completion? do I not act

for you--think for you--and labour for you? do I not place you in

affluence and grandeur? be you not the Countess of Ennismore? tell I,

be you not Countess of Ennismore, the mistress of princely Bedinfield?"



"I be the unfortunate and unhappy wife of Lord Ennismore," answer

Julia, "the nominal mistress of Bedinfield, but the real proprietor of

only sorrow and degradation."



"away with such folly!" cry Lady Wetheral, with vehemence; "let I

not hear such mad complaint, such horrible madness! have you not

all that be covet by human beings?--state, high rank, wealth, and

influence? what do your arrogant heart covet _now_? what do you

presume to wish, beyond the splendid lot you have obtain?"



"happiness--I ask for happiness! I ask for my husband's heart--I ask

for domestic peace," reply Julia, press her forehead with she

tremble hand. "I ask for the simple pleasure of domestic peace. I

will not accept grandeur without they!"



"you have bring public remark upon the name of Wetheral," resume she

mother, her eye dart fire. "you have betray the confidence of

your mother, who hope to see her daughter an envy creature! you have

throw away the jewel of life, to grasp at shadow. Happiness!--_who_

be happy?--not those who be bear to stand apart in grandeur--not those

upon whom the eye of the multitude gaze in admiration. it may be a

word bandy by the humble, to balance the evil of poverty, and give

a zest to lowly destiny--but the great one hee it not. _they_ live

in a sphere set apart from grovel notion--they spurn the folly

of romantic, sickly fancy, to hold on their course like meteor! I be

a parent most miserable. I be deprive of all I labour to advance.

my heart be anchor upon the glorious destiny of three child,

who have betray their high calling--but Bell have do the bad. a

dukedom be offer her!--a dukedom be tender to she, I say! and the

puny coward strike it from her!--oh, that hour to a mother's heart!..."



Lady Wetheral's vehemence overpower her strength. the sudden

and unaccountable appearance of her daughter, without any previous

warning, almost lead she to suppose a spirit from the dead have rise

to taunt she with her deep disappointment. it seem as if a spirit

from another world have seek she, to jeer and mock at her misery as

a defeated mother, and that form assume the likeness of her banish

Julia. what! have she hear the word "infamy" spoken?--did she hear

that Lady Ennismore have fly from her husband? be this to be add

to Clara's death, and Christobelle's ingratitude? be she indeed to

endure this accumulate burthen of crushed hopes?--to see _all_ her

long year of anxious effort destroy, and behold the very being

she have raise so high, turn to rend she? what spirit could bend under

such fearful ingratitude, that possess one spark of _her_ indomitable

determination?



a deep pause succeed. Julia still listen, with her face bury in

her hand, and her dress be yet in the grasp of her mother's hand,

when a cry from Mrs. Bevan startle she. Lady Ennismore look up in

terror. she behold her incense parent stand before she, in the

attitude of reproach, but her eye be dull, and her form have become

rigid: contend passion be war with terrible violence in her

heart.



it be a fearful and affect scene to witness, but it could not

long last. Lady Ennismore's terror at her unfortunate mother's state

obliterate for the moment her own sorrow, and she fly to assist Mrs.

Bevan in her care. Sir John Wetheral and Christobelle be instantly

summon, and the Castle become a scene of alarm and confusion. Mrs.

Spottiswoode be again a true-hearted and valuable friend in their

affliction.



Lady Wetheral sink into a long illness. her strife of heart--the strife

of a high and determined spirit contend with bitter mortification

in all those thing which she have so fondly cherish--have nearly

prove fatal to her frame, and she be long vibrate between life

and death. but her naturally good constitution, and the unremitting

attention of her daughter, overcome the attack of a dangerous

malady, and gradually Lady Wetheral become again convalescent. the

body slowly acquire some portion of renew health, but the mind be

fix in gloomy irritability. nothing could exceed her ladyship's

unbearable tyranny to those gentle being who strove to soothe she

long confinement. the victim of her ambitious project be now the

object of constant petty and vexatious attack. Christobelle have one

near she who could lure her disquiet into happy tranquillity--but Lady

Ennismore almost sink under their distressing influence.



Sir John Wetheral bear all his trial with the resignation of a man

who receive good and evil thing from his maker's hand, and accept

they as mean of evidence his patience and resignation. he endure

his lady's most disagreeable taunt with the fortitude belong to

his estimable character: he only appear to suffer when those taunt

be level at the heartbroken, gentle Julia. Lady Wetheral's

tyrannical temper seem irrecoverable even by the operation of time,

or gentle forbearance. Mrs. Bevan remark "that her lady's eye

and manner be peculiarly vehement in their expression, during her

reproach address to Lady Ennismore." it _must_ have be a powerful

feeling which could produce such a change of manner in one whose whole

existence have be devote to the exercise of self-command, and who have

ever deprecate the bad taste and uselessness of "scene;" it must have

be an overwhelming feeling of ambition trample to the earth, which

bear down a mind so keenly alive, so restless in its purpose, and so

successful in its scheme.



Christobelle have the blessing of Sir John Spottiswoode's society,

to balance she many hour of disquietude. she could turn to he for

happiness, when her spirit be sad, and, under his soothing, her

mother's harsh remark be forget. every disagreeable feeling

pass away in the sunshine of his presence. she only bent, in grateful

acknowledgment, to the being who have commit her infancy to her

father's care, to receive his wise admonition, and be caution to

renounce the fearful dictate of ambition. Christobelle see how it have

lure its victim to woe. she know it have destroy the happiness of

Julia--that it have aim the death-blow to Clara--that it have work

desolation upon her mother. every one who drink of the cup which a

reckless ambition present to their lip have taste a deadly poison,

which slowly and surely produce desolation of heart. Christobelle feel

she have be spare. she have not be overwhelm by its cold precept:

she have receive strength to endure oppression, and have not barter

peace of mind for the empty glare of worldly distinction. Christobelle

be indeed grateful, as she ponder these thing in her mind.



Lady Ennismore be call to a less fortunate destiny. her spirit

be break by the continual and ruthless observation which be

shower upon she by her irritable parent, under the pressure of time

unemployed, and the total failure of resource. Lady Wetheral's mind

turn to the past, for material to employ her weakened energy;

and the past could only give back harass recollection. such

recollection produce a constant state of irritation, which be

hurtful to herself, and intolerable to those around she. Wetheral

Castle appear the grave of every hope, and the "_oubliette_" to

rational, tranquil comfort. the heart of Lady Ennismore be depress

beyond recall, by continued and unsuccessful effort to appear cheerful

under accumulate suffering. it be impossible to give satisfaction to

an exact and imperious mother. she could only weep in privacy, and

pray to be "lay by Clara."



Mrs. Spottiswoode be unwearied in her kind visit during Lady

Wetheral's illness. the Penelope of former day be the same attach

friend at the present hour; and Lady Ennismore feel how blessed be the

possession of a gentle heart, which have clung to she through good and

evil report--which never exact selfish sacrifice, or shrink from the

task of endure much, to soften the distress of an uncomplaine

spirit. Mrs. Spottiswoode bear the petulant remark of Lady Wetheral

with patient good-humour. if the "blood of the Wycherlys" rise

occasionally into her cheek, and latent fire sparkle in her eye,

the door of her lip be hermetically seal, and she never resent

the offensive petulance of a defeated and angry manœuvrer. her only

desire aim at ward off for a few hour the painful observation

which must otherwise have be level at two unoffending object.



Lady Wetheral do not object to receive Mrs. Spottiswoode. however

strongly her character approximate to that of her aunt Pynsent in its

outline, her manner be less abrupt, and her temper more yielding.

Mrs. Spottiswoode have also "creep in" so silently and regularly, that

a visit every other day be consider a thing of course; and if Lady

Wetheral have any thing particularly disagreeable or offensive to say,

she contrive to say it to Mrs. Spottiswoode. Mrs. Spottiswoode bore

every thing with smile: she know it spare the feeling of her own

friend--the suffering, the injured, and dependent Julia.



Lady Wetheral confine herself entirely to her apartment, and

decline all society. she derive no satisfaction from the visit of

friend, whom she be sure come on purpose to deride her sorrow. she

particularly command to be deny to Mrs. Pynsent. she tell Mrs.

Spottiswoode it be unpleasant to be restrict from communion with her

neighbour, but she must be aware her aunt Pynsent be inadmissable

from her loud tone of voice, and uncouth way of blurt out offensive

remark. her aunt be a misery in a sick room, and she only wonder

how Clara could endure it, to the exclusion of the mother who have

promote her marriage, and endure so much to effect it.



Lady Wetheral also confide to Mrs. Spottiswoode her opinion upon

Christobelle's folly.



"your brother, Mrs. Spottiswoode, be a very gentlemanly man, but a poor

baronet be a sad match for Bell--I will never lend myself to it. I know

Sir John allow he to visit here, and Bell be engage to he in some

way or other, I dare say. perhaps they be wait for my death? Bell

refuse a dukedom, and be content to accept a Worcestershire baronet!

can you believe any thing so degrading?--and waiting, too, for her

poor mother's death! this be very dreadful! how can I look any of my

neighbour in the face? I be tell Lord Farnborough be go to marry

Fanny Ponsonby: it serve Bell quite right, and I hope she will feel

it severely. a pleasant sight it will be to see the forfar equipage

dash by, while Bell be only a poor baronet's wife in a britzska. I

cannot endure such thought. Bevan, where be my salt?"



"but, my dear Lady Wetheral, if my brother make Christobelle happy,

and if he indulge she with all the comfort of life, what more can a

human being require?"



Lady Wetheral shudder.



"the comfort of life! bread and cheese to eat, and a stuff gown and

straw bonnet to wear--be this the vulgar and popular idea of existence?

you, Penelope, have marry into the family, and be justify in

uphold it, but I will never see Bell, if she can endure degradation!

my health be sacrifice to outraged feeling! Lady Ennismore, if it

be not too much trouble, will you be so considerate as to move this

cushion a little high. your ladyship have have little practice, I

fancy, in the nursing department: it never occur to you how much I be

suffer."



Lady Ennismore silently adjust the cushion, but the allusion to

her banishment from her lord's sick room, renew the grief of her

heart: tear spring to her patient, expressive eye. this could not be

overlook by Penelope Spottiswoode.



"Lady Wetheral, I demand, and insist upon the necessity of Lady

Ennismore's removal for a few day to Lidham. I must not allow you

all to waste away in witness each other's depression. Christobelle

and Sir John will take Julia's place, while I run away with my friend

this very morning. I shall not return to Lidham till you be ready to

accompany I, Julia."



there be "a Pynsent tone" in Mrs. Spottiswoode's speech, which Lady

Wetheral feel unable to contend against: her ladyship detest that

_hatton_ expression of voice. she reply languidly, with an injure

and offended air,



"pray do as you please, Mrs. Spottiswoode. every one have do, and, I

suppose, _will_ do, as they please with I. I be too feeble to resist

violent resolution. I beg to decline have any one force upon I.

Lady Ennismore have renounce control of any kind, and, of course,

she will continue to act as she think proper, without consult

her mother. Sir John and Christobelle, I suppose, will visit I,

without be 'offer.' I conclude my family will relieve my solitude

voluntarily, though I be consider of secondary importance. Bevan,

where be my pocket-handkerchief?"



"in your hand, my lady."



"oh, very well. I wish I be equally blind to more distressing

annoyance. I wish I could lose sight as easily of other thing."



Mrs. Spottiswoode turn a resolutely deaf ear to all covert attack.

it be imperative, in her opinion, to withdraw Lady Ennismore to

Lidham, and the harsh conduct of Lady Wetheral only rivet she

resolution. Sir John concur in her view. he be aware his daughter

endure much, and he wish she to be remove altogether from a scene

so destructive to her peace. it be impossible to hope Julia could ever

regain tranquillity, when the wound of her heart be tear open by

daily and hourly invective. Christobelle and himself would attend the

querulous invalid, in patient hope that time would soften the asperity

proceed from a diseased mind, but he see the absolute necessity of

withdraw Lady Ennismore from her attendance. Sir John Wetheral

hope she would remain a long season in the society and hospitality of

Lidham.



yet Julia quit her father with great reluctance. she know she

sister be happy, and support by the occasional visit of Sir John

Spottiswoode. her heart be occupy by a powerful attachment, and

sorrow have not throw a mantle of gloom over _her_ young vision yet.

her affection be bless by a father's approval, and the smile of

rejoice friend; yes, Christobelle could contemplate her futurity

fearlessly--but who would, or could, pour balm upon her father's

solitary hour? his study be still a sanctuary, but he carry into

its precinct a disturbed and heavy spirit. Julia could not bear the

idea of quit her father.



Mrs. Spottiswoode smooth every thought which could ruffle her

friend's equanimity, and plane away all difficulty. she unburden

her mind to the four friend who surround she, as she hastily partook

of sandwich.



"my dear Sir John, I have achieve a scheme, which will set my

Julia's heart at rest, and your, too. I counsel you to keep the

'poor Worcestershire baronet' at Wetheral, till happy time arrive.

why should not he bear some share of the evil, when the good be

before he? and by his sparkling eye, and intelligent glance at

Christobelle, I judge he be willing to undertake the task. this be

my advice, as far as concern _yourself_; now for my brother-in-law:

listen, young man, and be guide! I counsel you to be gentle mannered,

and prompt in action, as I have be. _Creep in_, as I have do;

and bear all irritate remark, as I have bear they. learn to be

enduring, patient, and silent, and I will undertake to promise you

sufficient success. who undertake to refute my word?"



Mrs. Spottiswoode look round at her auditor, but there be no

refutation. Sir John Spottiswoode alone reply, and he only speak his

eager wish to assist in tranquillizing Lady Wetheral's objection

to his suit. he would wait in patience and persevere attention, to

attain that bless reward of his labour, if it be require, even for

year.



"six month will do, John, if you be politic. Sir John Wetheral, pray

lead Lady Ennismore to my carriage, and I will follow, after a few

word in a corner with my brother."



Sir John lead out his daughter, while Christobelle clung to her sister's

hand. she be go to lose she for an indefinite period, and she

should miss her gentle voice and affectionate smile. Spottiswoode would

be with she, and she could not but own his society be a charm to

balance a thousand ill. nevertheless, she must miss Julia every hour.

she would have the satisfaction, however, of know how much she would

be prize by Mr. and Mrs. Spottiswoode.



Mrs. Spottiswoode do not long detain her friend. her word be few,

and decisive.



"John, that unhappy woman be as mad as a March hare. I never can

believe she sane, therefore, I bear with she. let her abuse you and

your friend; and allow she to speak whatever she think of aunty

Pynsent, and I be sure you will become necessary to she. her manner

be so completely change, that I be confident she be derange, and it

be no use quarrel with mad people."



"it be an extraordinary method of make oneself acceptable, Penelope.

I be not sure I can endure to hear my friend abuse, but I will

endeavour to be pleasing, and you may be sure I shall 'creep in' after

Christobelle. once fairly admit into the invalid's room, you need

not fear my second dismissal."



"very well, I have no more to say, then. I will not relinquish Julia,

as long as I can prevail upon she to remain at Lidham. she be endure

too much for human nature to bear. farewell." Mrs. Spottiswoode then

join her friend.



Sir John Wetheral press Mrs. Spottiswoode's hand, as he assist she

into the carriage. "accept," he say, "the grateful thank of a father

for this kind and thoughtful step. may _you_ never be call to sorrow

which your warm heart be seek to alleviate in your friend!"



Mrs. Spottiswoode return the pressure.



"I do not ask you to come to we very often, because I know you cannot

exist long from Julia; but be sure you always bring good news with you

from Wetheral. God bless you all!"









CHAPTER XXXII.





Lady Ennismore recover some degree of tranquillity under the soothe

influence of her friend, who congregate round she at Lidham, but

her spirit never recover their tone of elasticity. she meet the

Pynsents with severe distress, and struggle visibly for fortitude,

as recollection of the past crowd to memory; but when that fearful

interview be once effect, Anna Maria's society be productive of

much good. Tom Pynsent be unchanged; he be the same excellent and

honourable creature: he be an affectionate and value husband; they

appear to be, and be, the happy couple in the world. no wonder

Anna Maria look young and handsomer than ever. her heart be at

rest. how warmly have her father speak of Tom Pynsent's good quality.

alas! _she_ have prefer splendid misery, and be now reap a

harvest of woe. she would not, dare not, think too deeply.



Lady Ennismore could contemplate Mrs. Boscawen with unmixed

satisfaction. she be change in person, and improve in manner. Mr.

Boscawen be proud of his lady; and how could he help it, since he be,

in her eye, the good and handsome of create being? it must be a

new and delightful existence to the once alarming, grim-look, though

excellent, Mr. Boscawen.



the sight and sound of those she love be of important benefit to

Lady Ennismore. the accent of affection, the voice of mirth, the form

of her long banish friend glide before she, rouse her dormant

energy, and awaken she to the joy of life. she pay short visit

to Hatton and Brierly, to see her nephew and niece; and, though her

lip never utter a remark, Mrs. Spottiswoode fancy she more languid

and pale after her brief absence. Doubtless, the "rural sport" at both

place be too powerful for her weakened frame and shattered nerve.



Lady Ennismore continue three month with Mrs. Spottiswoode. Sir

John Wetheral bring a bulletin daily of his lady' health, and

each account be less favourable in its purport. Christobelle write

despondingly upon the subject to her sister. she regret to say

her poor Spottiswoode fail in all his patient exertion to win her

mother's approbation. she be happy to think there be no cause of

complaint against he of any serious nature. his crime consist in

having step in between her poor mother's ambition and a dukedom; and

this would ever be unpardonable in her eye. her mother be relentless

towards Spottiswoode. she would not pronounce his name, or receive a

message from he. she only allude to he as the "poor baronet," or

"the man whom Sir John uphold." it be vain to hope against hope. her

mother's dislike grow more powerful as her strength decline, and it

would end only in the grave. her mother receive no one; she appear

to have renounce society, and her movement be exclusively confine

to her own range of apartment. Mrs. Daniel Higgins be admit

frequently, because she have be the depository of her lady's secret

in day of yore, and be now a patient listener to her regret; but,

beyond that, all be silent at Wetheral. Christobelle consider she

father in much well spirit. he have become apparently reconcile to

her mother's change of habit; and he be more cheerful, more call

forth, she think, than when her mother be the dominant spirit. it

might be that his mind be at rest concern his child; that he be

no long dread plot and system, and be gratify by the constant

society of Spottiswoode, who be so attentive and companionable to

he. she could not tell, but so it be. she be distressed to think

they be so happy together, when her poor mother's situation be so

cheerless, and her health so visibly decline!



such be the tenor of Christobelle's communication to Lidham, and they

renew Lady Ennismore's anxiety to return to Wetheral. she long to

relieve Christobelle from some portion of her fatigue; and, above

all, her spirit fly back to her father. she could never sufficiently

value his parental anxiety, or the protection he be afford her

sorrow. a father's presence be a shield from every worldly blast; and

the perfect seclusion of Wetheral Castle suit well with her present

state. Lidham be almost too gay, though she only meet look and word

of kindness and approbation. it be time that Christobelle should

also enjoy a period of happy communion with Sir John Spottiswoode;

and that period could not arrive, unless some one assume the rein

in her place, and bear the disagreeable of the nursing department.

Mrs. Spottiswoode's good sense acquiesce entirely in Lady Ennismore's

reasoning.



"my dear friend, _you_ be perfectly right, and _i_ be only perfectly

sorry to lose you. I anticipate much comfort in the present state of

thing, however dismally Christobelle represent they. you will all be

happy at Wetheral, and I shall see your face beam with smile, in

spite of Lady Wetheral's monastic retirement. don't look distressed,

Julia; I be go to explain myself."



"my mother have receive an incurable wound, Penelope!"



"I know that. Lady Wetheral have receive an incurable wound in her

ambition, and that have close her hope and pleasure on this side the

grave. she have no child to plan for--not one now to sacrifice. all be

end which employ her mind, and feed the craving passion of her soul.

her resource be cut off, and she will never more resume her position

in society. be it not wisely order? if Lady Wetheral recover her

health, would she not be scheme for her grandchild, and pour

her besetting foible into their innocent mind? my dear Julia, enough

misery have be originate. let it end here. let we not wish it

otherwise."



Lady Ennismore could not refute her friend's argument. Mrs.

Spottiswoode continue.



"Wetheral Castle will never, perhaps, resume its festive scene, for

there have be too much of evil connect with their remembrance;

but you will enjoy profound peace of heart, and receive your friend

without alarm. if Lady Wetheral remain seclude in her apartment,

there be no reason why the rest of the family should not enjoy

themselves: forgive I if I say it will prove _true_ enjoyment."



Mrs. Spottiswoode speak truly. Wetheral Castle do become a home

of domestic peace, because its restless mistress no long wield

the sceptre of power, to transform the element of good into the

instrument of evil. Lady Wetheral sink into ill health and apathy,

irrecoverable. her mind and body seem stunned into torpor, by two

event which she have not foresee, and could not parry--the refusal of

a dukedom by Christobelle, and the flight of Lady Ennismore from her

home. these two event be ever upon her thought, and in her speech,

because "she have particularly arrange each splendid match, and be

doom to be foil by her own child in their accomplishment. she

know her energy be wear down, and her strength exhaust. she

could not walk three step from her sofa without fatigue, and the

least noise produce severe nervous attack. she be a pretty speciman

of maternal care! she advise all parent to allow their headstrong

daughter to marry whoever would encumber themselves with they; for

marry they would, and it be hopeless to endeavour to lead their taste

in a proper channel. she expect Mrs. Higgins would let _she_ little

girl grow up in insubordination, and the child would most likely marry

a bricklayer, instead of look up to a man in a well establish

grocery-business. she detest mean mind."



Lord Ennismore and his mother, the Dowager-countess, appear again

at Bedinfield. her ladyship's point be gain. she have recover

entire control over the destiny of Bedinfield, free from continual

alarm, lest her son should escape her powerful influence, and become

infatuate by the loveliness and yield disposition of his gentle

wife. but she do not long enjoy the fruit of her unnatural conduct.

Ere a year have elapse after the separation record, Lord Ennismore

sink into the family vault at Bedinfield, unwept and unhonoured, save

by the generous-hearted creature whom he have not the capacity to

appreciate.



when Lady Ennismore receive the information of her unfortunate lord's

decease, she weep to think how desolate have be the existence of

a human being, bear to become the tool and victim of his mother's

insatiable love of power; and she weep to remember he have die without

the consolation of be watch over by a wife, who would have act

honourably and faithfully in her duty.



Sir John Wetheral also suffer. he feel a conviction that his own want

of firmness have foster his lady's ambitious turn of mind; and he

dwelt upon the melancholy idea that his own hand have bestow, however

unwillingly--that his consent have be extort, however painful to

himself--to give a beloved child to the imbecile Lord Ennismore. it

be a thought he never could banish from his memory, and it pain he

most when Julia's society become his great comfort. it be, however,

vain to regret the past. Sir John's mild nature be unequal to contend

with the persevere system adopt by his lady, and he could never

comprehend or combat effectually her uncease effort to forward

her view upon the mind of her child. the Gertrude of his early

affection be now sever from his companionship, and he turn to

Julia, to receive from her hand the care and attention necessary to

his future comfort.



Lady Ennismore fully requite her parent's hope. she seek no society

beyond her own family, and the little circle of friend who have ever

value her affectionate heart. Mrs. Spottiswoode, the friend and

beloved companion--that solace to earthly tribulation--that gift

tender to few--be near she. Hatton be a home of affection, and

Brierly throw open its portal with triumph at her approach. all

have respect and honour the hapless wife, and all surround the

release widow in silent gratulation. Mrs. Pynsent publicly declare

"it be a deep trick of that woman Ennismore, whom she never could

endure; and if the poor young Julia Wetheral have not fall into

the hand of two she-Philistines, she never would have marry that

sickly little chap, whom the mother lead about by the nose. some thing

which should be nameless be already come to pass, and she hope Old

Nick would fly away with all manœuvre mother. a certain lady

be shorn of her beam, who expect to command the world; and after

brandish her arm, and catch all the prime match up, she be

cut down into a mighty small space, with an evil conscience to chat

with. if Lady Ennismore would be advise, she should counsel she to

change her name and title, by marry a comfortable Shropshire lad.

there be plenty unprovide for."



but Lady Ennismore decline all thought of marriage, and devote

herself to the comfort of her parent. Colonel Neville write, at

the expiration of her mourning, and he lay claim to her compassion,

in consideration of the patience and constancy which have accompany

his involuntary and fervent attachment. he have condemn himself to a

perpetual banishment, even from the country which she inhabit. but

now that the bar be withdraw, the hour of disclosure be arrive,

and Julia must have respect the love which consume he. she could

bear witness that he have never breathe an unhallowed sentiment, or

endeavour to take advantage of her situation, during their long and

constant association in Florence.



Julia sigh as she read the declaration of Neville, but her heart

renounce a second engagement. "no," she write in reply to her lover's

epistle--"no, my heart have suffer too much disquietude to enter upon

fresh tie. I feel a calmness and consolation in watch over my

father's comfort, and take charge of my stricken mother, which my

married life deny I. that portion of my existence be a period of

deep misery, and it have break down my hope and my spirit. be happy,

Neville, with a woman who have not be call to suffering, and forget

one who will never more trust in man, or in herself. I will not give

hope, for you do not deserve to be treat lightly, and I cannot now

meet your wish. may I soon hear you have meet with a woman deserve

your esteem, and that your day be devoted to her happiness. my own

day be consecrate to the father whose counsel I would not heed, and

who have suffer so much through my obstinate folly."



and what shall be say of Christobelle? her portion be not the cup

of bitterness, though her patience be severely test. Lady Wetheral

become indifferent to all pass event so gradually, and her mind

dwelt so little upon any thing unconnected with her own ease and

immediate gratification, that Mrs. Daniel Higgins adventure to touch

lightly upon the subject, during one of her visit.



"I be happy, my lady, to be hear of Miss Chrystal's likelihood, at

last, to marry Sir John Spottiswoode. Higgins think it a very pretty

match, and he have visit Alverton more than once, and admire the

place extremely. for ever and a day!--to think of Miss Chrystal's turn

be come!"



"I know nothing about it, Thompson, and I don't care. the

Worcestershire man shall never enter _my_ room, though he be quite good

enough for a young lady who refuse a dukedom. if Julia would attract

the old Duke of Forfar, now she be at liberty, I should still recover

my health; but I be lay on the shelf. no one care about my health.

Lady Ennismore might easily win his grace; only, I dare say, she would

run away from he, as she do from Lord Ennismore."



Christobelle marry Sir John Spottiswoode soon after Lady Wetheral's

assurance to Mrs. Higgins that she "do not care" about the affair, and

no one apprise her ladyship of the actual solemnization. she never

ask who be the "Lady Spottiswoode" whom people talk so much

about, and always address she by the title of Miss Wetheral.



do Christobelle ever repent her refusal of a dukedom, or experience

a repentant feeling that she have give her whole heart to the husband

of her choice? no. life bring too many care to allow of perfect

enjoyment upon earth, but Christobelle never regret the vow she

pay at the altar: she never regret the hour when she become the

bride of Spottiswoode, and exchange Wetheral Castle for the tranquil

grove of Alverton.
